Course Overview
Course Overview
Hi, everyone. My name is Kevin. Welcome to my course, Securing ASP.NET Core with OAuth2 and OpenID Connect. I'm a Microsoft MVP and freelance solution architect currently focused on APIs and security. Correctly, securing your web applications and APIs is essential. To achieve that, we use standards like OAuth2 and OpenID Connect. This course will teach you how to do that. Some of the major topics that we will cover include learning what OAuth2 and OpenID Connect are and how they help with authorization and authentication for your web applications and APIs, creating, configuring, and integrating with an identity provider, storing your users, integrating with other identity providers like Microsoft Entra ID and Facebook, and getting ready for production and deploying your identity provider. When you're finished with this course, you will have the skills and knowledge needed to secure your web applications and APIs with OAuth 2 and OpenID Connect. Before beginning the course, you should be familiar with C# and with building web apps and APIs with ASP.NET Core. I hope you'll join me on this journey to learn how to secure your applications with the Securing ASP.NET Core with OAuth 2 and OpenID Connect course, at Pluralsight.

Getting Started with ASP.NET Core Security
Coming Up
Correctly securing your web applications and APIs is essential. In this course, Securing ASP.NET Core with OAuth2 and OpenID Connect, you will learn how to do that. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. This course is a pretty deep dive into the subject. You'll not just learn how to secure a web app and API, but you'll also learn why we make certain decisions and what the potential trade‑offs are. The why is probably even more important than the how when talking about security, so I hope you'll find that as important as I do. First of all, we'll dive a bit deeper into what you can expect of this course, and we'll have a look at the course prerequisites and tooling. After that, we're starting with a part on application architecture security, this is a short dive into the history of how applications were built and how they were secured. This will help us understand why what we used to do when securing apps isn't sufficient anymore, and it will also help us understand where OAuth and OpenID Connect are coming from. Then, we will learn why using a central identity provider is important, and we'll introduce OAuth2 and OpenID Connect. If you happen to have any questions, the best place to post them is on the Discussions tab on the course page. Like that, the questions and answers can help other people out as well. If you want to know about my upcoming courses, user group events, or are interested in the technology I'm interested in, feel free to follow me on Bluesky or X. Let's position this course.

Positioning This Course
When working with OAuth and OpenID Connect, which is what this course is about, you will very quickly notice that integration with user authentication starts from client applications, so that's what we'll start with. Clients often talk to APIs. So, after we learned how to log in and log out of a client app and work with the user at that level, we will learn how we can assure that the API the client app talks to is secured as well. We will also learn how we can securely access the API from the client app. In the first part of the course, we will work with in‑memory users, that allows us to focus on the integration with client applications and APIs. But that's, of course, not a realistic scenario, in real life, you don't want to work with in‑memory users, you will typically have them stored in a database you manage, or you'll integrate with other identity providers. So that's what we'll cover next, users and user management from storing them in your local database over integrating with Active Directory, Azure Active Directory or Microsoft Entra ID, as it's called these days, and social logins, right up to implementing multi‑factor authentication. We'll also learn how we can integrate with ASP.NET Core identity and why you might want to do that or avoid that. At the end of the course, you will learn how to get ready for production and how to effectively deploy your identity provider. So, as you notice, this is not a short course. I hope you'll enjoy it. Let's have a look at the course prerequisites and tooling.

Application Architectures and Security
Let's take a step back to a few years ago. In those days, we were typically working on thick client applications, like Windows Forms, or WPF applications. If we were not working on those, we were probably working on a server‑side web application, those were not service based and tier applications. For those thick clients, Windows credentials were typically used if identity‑related information was required. Users would log into their PC on the domain of their employer, and that was sufficient. For intranet web applications, Windows Authentication was often used, and for internet‑facing web applications, Forms authentication was used, with a login screen at the level of the application itself. If you were in the sector a few years ago, I'm sure you know the drill. As mentioned, these web apps were not service‑based, and that made them quite easy to secure. Moreover, a lot of the time they were living on the same domain, so a cookie with an authentication ticket in it could be shared for a rudimentary single sign‑on implementation if needed. That is a strategy that's definitely advised against today, by the way, so don't do that. But then the application landscape changed, we started building service‑oriented applications, often with the help of frameworks like Windows Communication Foundation, WCF. But even then, we were still talking about thick client applications that live inside of the company domain or server‑to‑server communication from a server‑side ASP.NET web forms or MVC application to a WCF service. With WCF taking care of most of the hard work through the WS‑Security standard, it wasn't that much more work to keep all of this secure with all these apps still nicely hosted on the same domain and/or running in the same application pool. Currently, WCF is end of life, the dinosaur image kindly gives that away. Often, this was then combined with IP‑level configuration on the firewall, only requests from server A would be allowed to server B, for example. A popular protocol when the exchange of authorization authentication data between security domains was required was, or is, SAML 2, it's still used today, especially for server‑side web applications in enterprise environments. But it's not such a good fit for more modern application architectures, as we'll see. Now, I am taking a few shortcuts with this explanation here, but you get the idea, we're typically talking about applications that live inside company walls or server‑side applications living in the same domain and/or under our control. There was still quite a large focus of application security and infrastructure level, and then the application landscape changed again. We started building web APIs or even RESTful APIs, those no longer necessarily lived in the same domain, and sometimes they weren't even under our control. We started creating applications that integrated with different APIs. A client application could talk to its own API, but at the same time talk to Google's APIs for Maps integration, to name one example. And those client applications changed as well, we're no longer just building server‑side web apps or thick clients that live inside company walls, we're building client‑side web apps with Angular, for example, that communicate with our APIs. We're building mobile applications that communicate with APIs, and we're building APIs that communicate with APIs, all of this means that we can no longer protect our APIs by keeping them inside of the company walls, safe for using something like a VPN. Mobile applications, JavaScript‑based applications, or Blazor WASM‑based applications run at the client level, so if they need to talk to a web API, that API must be public, it cannot be secured with forms of authentication anymore, a mobile application doesn't even have a notion of that. And a server‑side web application doesn't necessarily only talk to in‑company APIs. We might decide to host the API outside of the company walls and monetize it, or the application might need access to other APIs, so we're a bit stuck here. The things we used to do don't work anymore because they weren't designed for these scenarios. What did we try to do then? Well, we started with sending username and passwords over the wire on each request from client to API, and that quickly proved to be a really bad idea. All that is required to steal your password is a packet sniffer, and chances are that with that password, an attacker will gain access to much more than just this one application. Enter the age of token‑based security. Instead of sending over the username and password combinations on each call to the API, we are now sending tokens. These tokens represent consent, for example, consent granted by the user to the client application to access an API. Some tokens can be used on the client, others can be passed to the API and are used at that level to authorize access. But how do we create these tokens, and how do we safely deliver them to the applications that require them? A while ago, we would write token services for that. Typically, a call to a self‑created /login endpoint that would accept your username and password and return a JSON Web Token. This is definitely an improvement, we are no longer sending the username and password on each request, we're only sending it once. We get a token and on requests after that, we'd simply send that token to an API where it's validated. And this will work for all these modern types of application architectures, in fact, this homegrown approach is still used today. But sending your username and password to a self‑created action on an API and getting a token in return isn't a good fit for a lot of applications, nor is it a good idea. For one, it still requires us to share our username and password with that application or API. That may be okay for your own trusted application, although I am not a big fan, but it's a very bad idea for third‑party apps, including other applications that might live in your company. Moreover, once we start creating endpoints like these ourselves, we are essentially reinventing the wheel, we have to implement token expiration ourselves, we have to implement signing of tokens and token validation, we need to think about the token format, and we might want to implement opaque or reference token support, we'll need to separate authentication and authorization, we need to find a way to securely deliver these tokens to all these different types of applications, and that is just a few concerns. In short, mistakes are almost inevitable. And even if we don't make any mistake, keeping homegrown systems like these secure isn't easy either, as new vulnerabilities are regularly discovered. So, we are really trying to solve two issues here, one is no longer handling user credentials at application level, this should be handled by another component. In other words, we are looking for a central component to handle common identity‑related tasks. The other one is finding a way to ensure that these tokens are safe enough to use for both authentication and authorization for all these different types of applications. A server‑side web app is different from a user agent‑based web app, is different from an API, is different from a mobile application. So, we are looking for a proven protocol that handles common concerns we will inevitably forget when creating token services ourselves. Let's tackle these two issues one by one. First, the need for a central identity provider for these common tasks.

Working Towards a Central Identity Provider
In the past, we used to create login screens inside of a client application, and often, user management, password rules, password resets, etc., as well. These days we no longer do that, or at least we shouldn't. An application shouldn't be responsible for checking if a user is who they say they are. It's the responsibility of an identity provider to authenticate the user and, if needed, safely provide proof of identity to an application. Often, such an identity provider is also a gateway to user and account‑related tasks, like letting users manage their account. Now, why do we use these identity providers or a bit broader, identity and access management systems? There's a few reasons for this. Let's think about identity and access management‑related tasks first. User registration, account management, implementing password policies with rules for password strengths and resets, locking out users, and so on, are tedious tasks, easily allowing for mistakes, and they are also prone to change. It's better to have this handled in one central location so it can be reused across applications and decent penetration testing can happen. An identity provider is part of such a centralized identity and access management system. Let's clarify the reason for this with one simple example, passwords. To safely store a password in a database in the early days, encrypting it in a non‑decryptable way was considered okay, but most encryption mechanisms can be quite easily brute forced. If an attacker has your encrypted, nondecryptable password, they just have to try each and every combination, and that becomes a lot easier as computing power increases. Today, mechanisms like key stretching are used to prevent this, key stretching means encrypting passwords over and over and over and over again so it takes a few seconds to get to the correctly encrypted value. Encrypting the same password 10,000 times isn't exceptional, and this discourages brute forcing because of the time it takes. For that, strong algorithms like Argon2 or bcrypt are currently used. And probably, in a few years, new algorithms will supersede these. Now, with this in mind, imagine having a custom account database for each application. You don't want to go and change all of this for all these applications, one central system where these credentials are stored is a much better option. And while we're on the subject of passwords, there's more than just username and password combinations. Users might want to authenticate themselves with different means, username/password is one way, but other applications or systems might require certificates or proof of possession, in other words, a second factor of authentication or a third factor of authentication, like a physical digital pass or a smartphone, which would then typically have an authenticator app on it. Imagine that you have to implement and maintain support for this in each client application you have in your company, that will become very unmanageable very quickly. So, multiple applications require access to the same user accounts. Tasks related to those accounts are common for all applications and not‑application specific. Best practices concerning safely storing credentials change and means of authentication can change, these are just a few of the reasons to keep all of this in a central location. A user will thus no longer prove who they are in the application itself, they will do so at the level of the identity provider. That is a solution for our first concern. Solutions like these have existed for a while, by the way, Active Directory services is an example of this. So with that we have our central location, but we're not there yet. Our client applications require proof of identity, they need to know who the user is, and they often also need some form of authorization to access their API on behalf of the user, or to access third‑party APIs, and that brings us to solving that homegrown token service problem. Once we have a central identity provider and identity and access management system, we can add code at that level. So we can add a login endpoint or a token endpoint in a centralized location, but as we learned, that is just not good enough. We're now back at that reinventing the wheel slide. We don't want to come up with solutions for all of these ourselves, so how do we solve this then? Luckily, when problems arise, standards emerge, and that is where OAuth2 and OpenID Connect come into play. Let's have a look.

Introducing OAuth2
Let's start with the definition. OAuth2 is an open protocol to allow secure authorization in a simple and standard method from web, mobile, and desktop apps. From that definition, we can learn a few things. One, OAuth2 is all about authorization. In other words, through OAuth2, we can authorize a client application to access an API. OAuth2 defines an access token, and a client application can request such an access token and pass it through to the API to gain access to that API. Next to that, the definition speaks about web, mobile, and desktop applications. Not all applications are created equal. A server‑side web application like an ASP.NET Core 6 MVC app has different means of storing things like secrets, then, for example, an Angular application which completely runs on the client and thus cannot be trusted by default. So, OAuth2 also defines how an application can securely get such a token from a security token service or, in other words, how a client application can achieve authorization. We can already feel that our reinventing‑the‑wheel issues like expiration, delivering tokens to different client applications, authorization, and so on are being solved. The homegrown endpoints we talked about are being replaced by endpoints as defined by the OAuth2 standard, and the standard also states how to use these endpoints from different application types, we'll go into more detail on this later on in the course. So, our identity provider should implement this standard. In this regard, as we're talking about authorization, these identity providers are also named authorization servers or the more generic name, Security Token Services, Identity Server, which we will use in this course, and Azure AD are a few examples, but others exist as well, Ping, Okta, Auth0, WSO2 IdentityServer, TrustBuilder, and so on. All of these well‑known identity providers implement OAuth2. But those access tokens OAuth2 defines should only be used to access resources, an API, they should not be used to sign into a client application, they weren't created with that scenario in mind. So we're only halfway there, OpenID Connect is the last piece of the puzzle.

Introducing OpenID Connect
OpenID Connect is a simple identity layer on top of the OAuth2 protocol, so it extends OAuth2. With OpenID Connect, an application can receive an identity token next to an access token if it needs one. This identity token can then be used to sign into a client application, while that same application uses the access token to access an API. Likewise, principles as for aOAuth2 apply, it defines how different types of client applications can safely get those tokens. In this regard, as we are now talking about authentication or identity through that identity token, both Azure AD when the identity server and all the other services I mentioned can be named identity providers, or IDPs, next to authorization servers. We will go into more detail on this starting with the next module, but this should already give you an idea of the issues OAuth2 and OpenID Connect solve. OpenID Connect is the superior protocol, it extends and supersedes OAuth2. In essence, once you're dealing with users you should be working with OpenID Connect, even when you only want to access an API and don't need to log into a client application. OpenID Connect obsoletes some of the less than stellar parts of OAuth2. Lastly, OpenID Connect isn't just for new applications or for API‑based applications, it can and is often used by those, but there is no reason why you wouldn't be able to secure a web application that doesn't talk to an API with OpenID Connect. So there we go, we now know that the issues we are facing can be solved. I'd say it's time to introduce the demo project.

Demo - Introducing the Demo Application
In this demo, we'll introduce the starter application we're going to be working with. The demo files can be downloaded from the Exercises tab on the course page. Starter files and finished demos are included for each module, but they do build on each other, so you can start with the initial starter files and build on that. As an alternative, you can get the starter files and finished solution from my GitHub. That, by the way, is probably the best place to get the code from, as any bugs will be fixed there first. Also, you'll notice that the code base on my GitHub has the latest‑and‑greatest branch, that branch contains changes that were incorporated after recording. Most of these changes are language features that are relatively new and/or in preview, like primary constructors, switch expressions, and so on. Most of these changes will eventually make it into the main branch and into a course update, but if you can't wait and you want to work with the latest‑and‑greatest features, that's the branch for you. Be wary, though, it won't necessarily completely match what you will see in the clips. If you want to be able to follow along exactly, use the main branch. Anyway, onto the code. We're going to be working with an ImageGallery application. The solution we're starting from consists of three projects, an ASP.NET Core web frontend, ImageGallery.client, an ASP.NET Core web API, ImageGallery.API, and a shared Model project. The underlying data store is a SQLite database accessed through Entity Framework core. I chose to choose SQLite, as that ensures that the codebase works, regardless of the operating system you're using. It's available on Mac, Linux, and Windows. The DBContext contains just one DBSet, Images. Such an image has four properties its Id, a Title, a FileName, and an OwnerId. It's this OwnerId that determines who an image belongs to. The idea is that only the images of the logged in user should be returned from the API to the web app. The database is also seeded with some dummy data to work with, it hasn't been created yet, but the necessary migrations have been added. All you have to do to create a database is execute and update database statement, for example, via the Package Manager Console. You can typically find the Package Manager Console on the bottom of your screen, but if it's not there, you can also find it via the VIEW menu or via the Search box. Let's make sure that the ImageGallery.API project is selected here, and let's execute the update‑database statement. And there we go, this should have resulted in the database being created. And if we look at our Solution Explorer, we indeed see an ImageGallery.db file, and that's the database. So, all in all, this is a pretty standard, easy‑to‑setup solution to start from. Let's make sure that both the API and the frontend application start when we build and run, so we choose to configure the startup projects, and we check that ImageGalleryAPI has Action Start and ImageGallery.Client has Action Start. It's already okay on my machine, let's click OK, and let's build and run. We end up at the Home page, and we see a list of images. These images are fetched by the web application from the API. Currently, there is no security at all. We didn't have to log into the application, and we can see images of different users. We see images by Emma, and we also see images by David. If we click the Add an Image button on the top here, we see that the user interface to create an image is there. But we're we to click the Add image button, it wouldn't work yet, that's because an image has to be added for the specific user, which is the owner of the image, and currently, our application doesn't have a notion of a user yet. And it's not only the web application that isn't secured, the API the web app talks to isn't either, the only thing that's already okay is that we are running over HTTPS, which ensures that traffic is encrypted, that's a given. There really isn't any excuse for not running over HTTPS these days. But anyway, next to that we have nothing, in other words, there's quite a bit of work to do before all of this is secure. Luckily, we've got a full course to fix it all.

Summary
Important to remember from this module is that we've come a long way. New application architectures emerged that couldn't easily be secured with the old paradigms, so we started trying to secure our applications with homegrown endpoints. But because there's so many things to keep in mind when doing that, we were probably opening us up to security holes. Currently, it's advised to use a central location for identity and access management, an Identity Provider is part of such a centralized system. This provider should implement protocols that safely allow authentication and authorization for different application architectures, and that's what OAuth2 an OpenID Connect solve. Without further ado, let's dive into OpenID Connect in the next module.

Understanding Authentication with OpenID Connect
Coming Up
The OpenID Connect protocol consists of a few important concepts we have to look into to be able to understand what OpenID Connect really is. That's what this module is about. We're starting this module with a high‑level perspective of how OpenID Connect works. Then, we will cover the concepts related to it, confidential and public clients, the various endpoints, and the flows and grounds. And when that's finished, we can start setting up our identity provider. Let's learn how OpenID Connect works.

Learning How OpenID Connect Works
From the previous module, we know what OpenID Connect is. Let's dive into a bit more detail and look at how it works. We're only focusing on identity at this moment, which means we are focusing on identity tokens and not access tokens. A client application requires to know a user's identity. That client application is also called the relying party as it relies on the identity provider to return the result of the authentication in a secure manner, so the client can use it and can rely on it. A request is created by the client application which redirects the user to the identity provider. At the level of the identity provider, the user proves who they are, for example, by providing a username and password. You've likely seen this in action before. If you've ever clicked a login with Google or login with a Microsoft account button, log into a site, you've seen the browser redirect to Google or Microsoft. It's very likely that that is the beginning of an OpenID Connect flow in action. Eventually, the identity provider creates an identity token, signs it, and returns it to the client application. Such an identity token can be seen as a token that contains the user's verifiable identity. Important to know here is that the two arrows you see here, so from client to identity provider and back, in reality, consists of multiple requests to different OpenID Connect endpoints. How that flow of request is handled, that's what the different OpenID Connect flows describe. We will look into this in a lot of detail during the course. For now, just remember that there's a lot more to it than what you see on this slide. Anyway, the client application eventually gets that identity token and validates it. If it checks out, the client application now has proof of identity. In an ASP.NET Core application, this identity token is then used to create a claims identity from, which in most cases, is stored as an authentication ticket in an encrypted cookie. The browser automatically sends that cookie on each request to the client application, thus providing proof of the user's identity, so that's the high‑level picture, but as we know by now, not all applications are created equal, and OpenID Connect is designed to work for various types of applications like server‑side web apps, client‑side web apps, and mobile apps. To allow us to decide on the correct way to implement this for our application, which is a server‑side web app, it defines client types and grounds or flows. Let's look into the different client types first.

Confidential and Public Clients
Two client types are defined, confidential clients and public clients. A confidential client is a client that is capable of maintaining the confidentiality of their credentials. That's client credentials, by the way, a client ID and a client's secret, typically. These are not the user's credentials. So yes, in OpenID Connect, a client application can authenticate and a user can authenticate. Client credentials are typically a client ID and a client secret. Other ways of authenticating a client are possible as well, but that would lead us too far. These client credentials are stored at the level of the client application. A typical example of such an application is a web app that runs on a web server like an ASP.NET Core MVC web application. As this app runs on the server, it can safely store its client credentials on that web server. They are, in other words, not exposed to nor accessible by the user. Confidential client applications can thus safely authenticate. The second type is a public client. These, as you can guess, are clients that are not capable of maintaining confidentiality of their credentials. They are, in other words, executing on the device in the browser or on a mobile device. That's just at the level of the client. These types of clients cannot safely authenticate. That means that JavaScript applications like an Angular application, WebAssembly applications like Blazor WebAssembly apps, or mobile applications are public clients. No matter what we try, a secret stored in JavaScript or on a mobile device is not a secret. It's always accessible. I do have to be a bit careful here though. These days, mobile operating systems allow using hardware APIs to safely store secrets. They are still considered public clients because it remains a fact that a secret is stored on the device. Yet, some leeway is sometimes given to these compiled to native applications because of this. Anyway, now we know about the two client types, let's have a look at the flows.

OpenID Connect Flows and Endpoints
Let's think back about those two arrows in our high‑level view, how to safely get an identity token, and eventually, once we talk about authorizing access to APIs, an access token is determined by the flow. So the flow determines how the code and/or tokens are returned to the client. You can look at such a flow as a number of requests and responses, often between client applications and the identity provider. In very simple cases, it's one request with one response, but in most cases, it's a bit more complicated than that and consists of a series of requests and responses, and you may not realize it, but as mentioned in the beginning of this module, you've probably seen this in action already. For example, have you ever needed to log into the main portal at your job or access an application offered by your company or wanted to log into a site which offers the option to log in with a Microsoft account or log in with Google? Chances are pretty good that you've noticed that your browser redirects you to a site where you can prove who you are, often by providing a username and a password. Once you've signed in, you're redirected back to the site you wanted to log into, and you're logged in to that site. It may even be that you've seen more than one redirection, for example, from the app you're trying to access to some in between site or to the site you enter your credentials on and back. Well, chances are pretty good that that was an OpenID Connect flow at work. Know that in most of these flows, there's not just visible redirection. There's also a set of requests and responses that happen that are not visible to the browser as you will learn soon. Depending on the client type, confidential or public, and the requirements you might have like the need for long lifetimes, a different flow or different variation of a flow must be used. Those flows will talk to different endpoints. These endpoints replace those pesky homegrown endpoints, so we cannot talk about flows without talking about endpoints. Let's look at those. First up, the authorization endpoint. Remember that we sent an authentication request to the identity provider a few slides back? Those requests are sent to the authorization endpoint at the level of the identity provider. This endpoint is used by the client application to obtain authentication for identity tokens and/or authorization for access tokens from the user. This is done by redirecting the client application to the identity provider. So from the authorization endpoint, an authorization code or other tokens can be returned to the client application. Important to know is that in OpenID Connect, TLS, or as it sometimes wrongly named SSL is a requirement. In other words, traffic must always be encrypted. That's already the case for our demo application, but as it is so important, it's worth mentioning again. Not encrypting traffic means we're vulnerable to man‑in‑the‑middle attacks, which in turn means our tokens could be intercepted, and we do not want that. Anyway, back to the endpoints. The second one is the redirection endpoint or callback endpoint. We're talking about redirection here. The client redirects to the authorization endpoint at level of the IDP and the IDP redirects back to the client. The URI where the IDP redirects the client back to, that is what is called the redirection endpoint, so it lives at level of the client application, and it is used by the identity provider to deliver the authorization code or other tokens to the client application, and then there's the token endpoint. From this endpoint, which again, lives at the level of the identity provider, client applications can programmatically request tokens, and that is typically done via an HTTP POST without redirection. Calls to the token endpoint can be authenticated with client authentication for confidential clients. For public clients, these calls are not authenticated simply because it doesn't make sense as a public client cannot safely store a secret anyway. There's more endpoints, and we will encounter some of those later on, but to start explaining the flows, the endpoints we just talked about are sufficient. Three major flows have been defined by OpenID Connect. The authorization code flow, the implicit flow, and the hybrid flow. If you've seen previous versions of this course, you may remember that I looked into these into detail. The thing is though, in security, things move fast and best practices regularly change. Vulnerabilities are regularly discovered and countermeasures thus need to be implemented. What was a good idea a few years ago might not be so good anymore today or may have slightly changed. You're currently looking at the OAuth2 security best current practice RFC. This expires every 6 months, and it's, thus, also updated at least once every 6 months. As OpenID Connect is built on top of OAuth2, this document is the one you want to look at when you want to learn about the current best practice flow. At the moment of recording, the best practice flow for both confidential and public clients are variations of the authorization code flow, so implicit and hybrid can be regarded as obsolete. We've got two types of clients, confidential clients and public clients. For both client types, PKCE protection should be used together with the authorization code flow. In fact, it's recommended for confidential clients and obligatory for public clients. It's a countermeasure against code injection attacks, but more on that later. For now, just remember that for both client types, the authorization code flow with PKCE protection is the current best practice. The authorization code flow gets its name from the fact that as a start of the flow, it returns an authorization code from the authorization endpoint. This authorization code can be seen as a short‑lived single use credential, which is used to verify that the user logged in at level of the identity provider is the same one who started to flow at level of the client app. In this flow, tokens are returned from the token endpoint. For confidential clients, the request to the token endpoint should be authenticated. That is safer than an unauthenticated call, and because of that, this flow allows long‑lived access for confidential clients through the use of a refresh token. For public clients, the story is a bit different. As we remember, public clients cannot safely store their credentials, so sending an authenticated request to the token endpoint is useless. It does not add anything security wise. From that, it follows that long‑lived access is restricted. A variation of a refresh token is allowed, but only for one‑time use. We will deal with refresh tokens later on in the course. Now, it's a bit out of scope for this course, but seeing a lot of you are probably building public user agent‑based client application like Angular, React, or even Blazor WASM applications, I do have to add that the current consensus on securing these apps is moving away from handling it in the user agent, so in the browser. Instead, I would currently suggest handling it at the level of the server. In other words, let the site that hosts your user agent‑based app handle the OpenID Connect flow, either as a regular ASP.NET Core application host, or potentially, as a back end for front end depending on your architecture. This used to be quite different a few years ago. Then, I would have suggested using something along the lines of oidc‑client.js to handle the OpenID Connect flow, another example of how fast things change in the security area. Anyway, that's it for the current best practice flow. We are building an ASP.NET Core web application, which is a confidential client, so we're going to implement the authorization code flow with PCKE protection and with client authentication. Now, why is it so important to choose the correct flow? Well, because choosing the wrong one or the wrong variation may open you up to security vulnerabilities. For example, if you are creating a public client and you return a regular refresh token like you would for a confidential client, you've just opened up a pretty big security hole. Now, this is just one example, and I realize that if you're new to this, all of this is pretty hard to grasp at this moment, but you don't need to understand all of this yet. The important thing to remember from all of this is that technically, there is nothing stopping us from choosing the wrong flow and implementing the wrong flow, which is a serious risk, and that's the thing with security. A lot of approaches will work, but most of them are not a good idea. It's not because you write code that allows you to get a certain type of token in a certain way, that it is safe, or that you should do it. And let me add, what is a good idea changes over time. You'll notice throughout the course that, even though we will use the authorization code flow, even with that flow, we will still have to make quite a few decisions, for example, on how to deliver claims on whether or not we include them in the token and so on. All of these might have an impact on how secure our application will end up being. Throughout the next few modules, you will learn a lot more about all of this. I don't know about you, but after all this theory, I think it's time to look into how we can set all of this up starting with the identity provider.

Introducing Duende IdentityServer
If we look at the OpenID Connect standard, you see that it is not a single line of code, it's a set of rules, a guidance if you wish. So what we need is a component that implements this standard, so we can create an identity provider that supports this. All of these endpoints that are defined here, the flows, the tokens, and so on, that is not something we want to code ourselves, and this is where when the identity server comes into play. Identity server is an OpenID Connect and allow two framework for ASP.NET Core developed by Duende software. It's officially certified by the OpenID foundation, and it's part of the .NET foundation. It's also completely open source. We're looking at their GitHub right now. We can, for example, click through to IdentityServer and that one will get you through the full source code for Duende IdentityServer. It's still the defacto standard for using OAuth and OpenID Connect in .NET. It's also advised by Microsoft and even used in some of their Visual Studio templates. Important to know is that Duende IdentityServer isn't exactly new. If you've worked with OAuth and OpenID Connect in the past, you'll probably remember that it used to be named IdentityServer X where X was the major version number, instead of Duende.IdentityServer. Dominic and Brock, the people who built it in their free time, decided to start Duende Software. They rebranded IdentityServer and potentially started charging for it, and that makes sense, of course. The amount of time it takes to build and maintain a solution like that is staggering, almost impossible to keep on doing in your free time, but understandably when this happened, quite a bit of confusion and even fear existed around the price and the impact surrounding all of this. Luckily, all of this has evened out by now. It's actually pretty straightforward now. Duende.IdentityServer has a free Community Edition license. The features of this edition are on par with the Enterprise Edition. You can use it in development, testing, and in production. All the details can be found via the link on screen. Essentially, unless your company makes more than $1 million US in gross revenue, you can use this edition. Otherwise, you should apply for another paid license. Also important to know is that you don't need a license yet for evaluation, development, or testing, nor for personal projects. However, if you're going to production, you will need to apply the license. We will do that at the end of the course. During the course, we will thus use this Community Edition.

Demo - Setting Up IdentityServer
In this demo, we'll set up IdentityServer. The Duende Software team created a set of templates for this, and we can use those, but if you don't have them on your machine yet, you need to install them first. I've opened the command prompt. To install these templates, execute a dotnet new install command, followed by Duende.IdentityServer.Templates. And there we go. I already had the latest version on my machine, but nevertheless, whatever was on my machine was uninstalled and the latest new version was installed. As you can see, a list of IdentityServer‑related templates shows up here. Those are the ones that were installed. You've got templates for an empty IdentityServer. You've got one for integration with ASP.NET Core Identity. You've got one for integration with Entity Framework and so on. We, however, are going to start with a very basic setup as that allows us to learn about IdentityServer step‑by‑step. The most basic template is the empty template, so let's use that. In case you are not in your demo directory, navigate to it. In my case, my demo directory is Pluralsight demo\ImageGallery, so that's where I need to be. I'm going to execute the .NET new command, which will result in a new project followed by the template I want to use isempty, followed by ‑n for name and Marvin.IDP as that's the name I want to give my IdentityServer. Mind the namespace here. An identity provider is typically not set up for one client application, rather it's reused across client applications. I prefer to reflect that in the namespace, so we're not naming it ImageGallery.IDP, but Marvin.IDP where Marvin is the name of the company, and there we go, and it was created. Let's switch to Visual Studio. I've got our demo solution loaded here. Let's add this Marvin.IDP project to it. So I choose to add an existing project, and in my ImageGallery folder, I can find Marvin.IDP. Let's select the project, and there we go. We ended up with a mostly empty project which references the IdentityServer version 7.0.0 and Serilog for logging. This may be a little bit different depending on when you're watching this course as Duende updates its templates regularly, especially when newer versions of IdentityServer are released. To follow along, it's best to reference the exact same version I'm using. Even though, I absolutely expect most of the code to remain the same in upcoming versions, I can't predict the future. Let's have a look at the Program class. Not much is happening here. A default web application builder is created on which the host is configured to use Serilog. Serilog is one of the most well‑known .NET logging frameworks. After that, the application is set up and run. This may look a bit odd. In a run‑of‑the‑mill web application, configuring the services and pipeline is done in the program file. In fact, if we have a look at our MVC client application, that is exactly what you see. First, services are configured and added on the container. Afterwards, the request pipeline is set up, and finally, app.run is executed. Let's switch back to the Program class of Marvin.IDP. What happens here is, in fact, the exact same thing. The Duende Software teams simply put that code into extension methods to keep the code base a bit cleaner. In configured services, the services are configured and added to the container, and in ConfigurePipeline, the pipeline is configured. Let's have a look at ConfigureServices. On the services object, AddIdentityService is called. This call registers the identity server‑related services on ASP.NET Core's built‑in dependency container. The next three calls are calls to configure IDP‑related resources like identity resources, API scopes, and clients. Let's have a look at that Config class in which we can find these three methods. There's not much to see here yet. Identity resources is a list of identity‑related resources. These map to claims of user like first name and last name. Currently, only OpenID has been added which maps to a user's identifier. A client application can request the OpenID scope to get access to that user's identifier. More on this later. API scopes map to API scopes. By requesting such an API scope, a client application can get access to an API, and those client applications are defined via clients. So for each application, you will need to define at least one client application. We will look into this in detail in the next module. For now, we just want to be able to build and get this up and running, so we're okay with leaving these lists empty for now. We see that these calls relate to in‑memory resources. After all, they are defined in that config class and that one is not persisted anywhere. And that's okay for now. Later on in the course, when tackling what we should think about before going to production, we will use a persistent store for this. One more thing on IdentityServer, options.EmitStaticAudienceClaim set to true. This ensures a specific claim, an audience claim, will be included in tokens by default. Again, more on that later on in the course. If we switch back to the Program class, we see that ConfigurePipeline is executed next. This configures the HTTP request pipeline. There's not much to see here at the moment. Logging middleware is added to the request pipeline and potentially a developer exception page is shown when we're in the development environment. There's also a call into app.UseIdentityServer which adds IdentityServer to the request pipeline. We're almost ready to give this a try. Just one more thing. Let's have a look at the LaunchSettings file. We see an application URL that starts with https. This ensures that our project runs over TLS, Transport Layer Security, so traffic is encrypted and that is important because in OpenID Connect and OAuth, there is no out‑of‑the‑box encryption of tokens. It relies on transport security for that. The second thing we notice is that the project is started with a self‑host profile. It is run as project, which will open a console that will make it very easy to see what's going on as IdentityServer logs a lot of information by default. If at any time something goes wrong or you hit an error, those logs are the first thing you'll want to have a look at. The one thing I'm going to change here is the LaunchBrowser setting. I prefer to set that to false to avoid a browser popping up all the time when we run our solution. We already have a browser popping up for our ImageGallery client and that is sufficient. Alright, let's give this a try. We only want to launch the Marvin.IDP project for now, so either set it as a startup project or make sure that it is selected, and let's build and run. The application seems to be up and running. If you look at the console window, everything is looking good. As you can see, we already see quite a bit of information being logged here. Let's navigate to the URL IdentityServer is running on, and there's not much to see here, and that actually makes sense. We didn't add a user interface yet, but there is one thing we can already have a look at, the discovery document, that can be found at the discovery endpoint, also known as the well‑known endpoint, which by convention, lives at /.well‑known/openid‑configuration, and that works as well. We see our supported scopes and related claims, a bunch of supported endpoints, and more information. It's this document that is read by other pieces of middleware when they want to find out where, for example, those endpoints can be found. For example, by looking at the authorization_endpoint field, the middleware knows at which you write the authorization endpoint can be found. One more thing of interest. When we stop running our project and have a look at the project folder, we see that a new folder, keys, has appeared. In OpenID Connect, tokens are signed, and for that, key material is required. By default, IdentityServer generates this on the fly, and that is what you see here. When deploying, you will want to replace this on the fly generation by more persistent key material. For example, a certificate. That too is covered in the module on going to production. Great. We've set up IdentityServer. Let's add a user interface in the next demo.

These folders contain all the views, related models, JavaScript, and CSS files for the UI, for example, a login view and the back‑end code for that. Demo - Adding Users to Test With
In this demo, we will add a set of test users to our project. And in fact, when we added the UI, a test user class was created as well. This exposes a List of TestUser. TestUser is a class specifically created for dealing with in‑memory users. By default, two users have been added, Alice and Bob. Let's replace those with two of our own users, David and Emma Flagg. The subject ID is their unique identifier. This must be unique at the level of this identity provider. We also set a username and password, and we add a list of claims for each user. Those claims represent information about the user, so we give David a given name and a family name and we do the same for Emma. Now, how do we get these claims to a client application? Claims are related to scopes, so let's set them up next. Let's switch to the Config class. I already briefly mentioned this when we looked into the identity resources method here. Identity server differentiates between resources that have to do with a user's identity and resources that have to do with APIs and API access, those are mapped to API resources and API scopes. We're currently focusing on identity, so we're going to ignore the API part for now. As we are using OpenID Connect, we must support the OpenId scope. This is a requirement, it ensures that the user identifier, which is the subject ID we've just seen, can be requested. So if a client requests the open ID scope, the user identifier claim is returned. This was included by default. What we want to add now is support for the profile scope, that maps to profile related claims like the given name and family name we gave our two users. In other words, if a client requests profile as a scope, our given name and family name claims will be returned. To add support for it, we add it to the identity resource list. This enables support for this scope across the full identity provider. OpenID Connect has defined additional scope to claim mappings. We already covered the OpenId scope which maps to a subclaim and the profile scope which maps to a set of claims related to a user's profile, but there's more standardized claims. The email scope maps to email and email verified. The address scope maps to an adverse claim and the phone scope maps to phone_number and phone_number_verified claims. A special scope defined by OpenID Connect is the offline access scope which doesn't map to a claim, but allows long‑lived access, more on that later. Other scopes and claims that you can define yourself are possible as well, so you're not restricted to what you've just seen. We will encounter an example later on in the course. One thing that's left, we need to ensure that IdentityServer knows about our test users. So let's switch back to the ConfigureServices method and let's call in to AddTestUsers passing through our two test users. With that, IdentityServer will know that our TestUsers are the ones we want to work with. That should be it. Let's build and run, and let's open a browser. Our IdentityServer still seems to be working. Let's have a look at the discovery document. And as we can see, we now have an additional supported scope, the profile scope. We can't log in yet though and we haven't yet created a client registration. What we should do is create a client application registration at the level of the identity provider for our ImageGallery client web app. We'll do that in the next module in which we will learn how to secure our web app. As far as this module is concerned, our IdentityProvider is up and running so we can have a look at the module summary.

Summary
We cannot talk about OpenID Connect without knowing about concepts it uses. The first concept we looked into were the client types. A confidential client is a client that can safely store secrets. A good example is a server‑side app. Public clients like JavaScript‑based clients, Blazor WASM clients, or some mobile apps cannot do this. Depending on the client type, a different flow or variation of a flow can be advised. Such a flow can, in this context, be seen as how an application can achieve authentication, in other words, proof of identity. Later on, we will see it's also how a client application can receive authorization. We learned that for our application, the authorization code flow with PKCE protection and client authentication is the best fit. Flows use one or more endpoints. The authorization endpoint at level of the identity provider is used by the client application to obtain authentication for identity tokens and/or authorization for access tokens from the user. Token endpoint, which is also living at the level of the identity provider, is where client applications can programmatically request tokens. The redirection endpoint then lives at client level. It's where the tokens are delivered to from the authorization endpoint. Important to know is that systems like these should always run over TLS. As tokens are not encrypted by default, we would be vulnerable to man‑in‑the‑middle attacks if we don't do this. I like to look at the subject of security in general as the amount of holes we allow in a box. Imagine that this box is a full system, applications, identity providers, servers, everything. Once we put it out there, we are, by definition creating holes in the box. There's no such thing as a completely foolproof secured system. Through these holes, attackers might come into our system. It is our responsibility to keep these holes as small as possible. To go back to our example when explaining confidential and public clients, returning a long‑lived multi‑usage refresh token to an Angular application, well, that's a pretty big hole. So let's try and keep these holes as small as possible during the rest of the course, starting with the next module, Securing Your User Authentication Processes.

Securing Your User Authentication Processes
Coming Up
Now we know about the theory, It's time to put it to practice. In this module, we will learn how to secure our user authentication processes. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. We just learned that the authorization code flow is a good option for our application as long as it is protected with PKCE protection. We'll start by looking into this flow, and while doing so, we will learn how to log in and log out. We will also learn whether it's a good idea to include identity claims in an identity token and what a possible alternative is. Let's start by looking into the authorization code flow.

The Authorization Code Flow
So the advised flow is the authorization code flow with PKCE protection. Let's look into the flow without PKCE protection first, and we'll add it in later on to avoid too much complexity at once. Each of the OpenID Connect flows starts with a request to the authorization endpoint. This is a simple redirection to a URI at level of the identity provider, so we have to create that URI. We're looking at an example of one. I simplified a few things here like the host address for readability, but at the moment, we can already see a few familiar concepts coming back. There's the endpoint itself at level of the IDP. We recognize that from the discovery document. Then there's the client id, that's the identifier of the client application. We also see a redirection URI passed through. That's the URI at level of the client application where the response from the authorization request will be delivered to. We also see scopes. The application requests access to the openid and profile scopes. So it wants access to the user's identifier and the profile‑related claims like given name and family name. The part I want to focus on now is the response type, it's currently set to code, but what does that mean? Well, it's the response type value in that request that determines the flow that's used. The values set as response type are the things or tokens returned from the identity provider to the client via browser redirection, for example, in the URL. A response type of code signifies that we will use the authorization code flow, and at the same time, that authorization code is returned to the client via browser redirection. Other response types like id_token or code id_token signify implicit and hybrid flows. These are, at the moment of recording, no longer regarded as best practice and can thus be viewed as obsolete. Let's have a look at the code flow with response type code, the one that's preferred. Our web application creates an authentication request with response type code and other parameters like scopes, and the web app sends the request. At level of the IDP, the user authenticates, for example, by providing a username and password combination. At this moment, the identity provider knows who the user is. The client application still doesn't. The identity provider optionally asks the user for consent. In other words, if you want, you can configure it to ask you if you want to allow the application to get, for example, access to your profile information. After that, the identity provider sends us back to the web application via URI redirection or via a Form POST. It passes through the authorization code, that is the response type we asked for. This code is thus delivered via the URI, that is called front channel communication, communication that is visible to the browser. As we remember, this code can be seen as a very short‑lived token that's proof of authentication. It's linked to the user that is just signed to the identity provider. So in essence, it binds the front‑end browser session to the back‑end session between client and identity provider. In the next step, the web client then calls the token endpoint through the back channel, and with back channel, I mean that to request does not use browser redirection, and thus, is not visible to the browser. This is a server‑to‑server HTTP request, and that is important to know because this means that the request and response are not exposed to the browser. Back channel communication is thus more secure than front channel communication. Forward this request to the token endpoint we passed through the authorization code and a way for the client to authenticate. As we know, most often that happens via a clientid and clientsecret combination. In other words, the client application has to authenticate itself, and if all of that checks out, the identity provider generates an identity token and sends it to the client in the response. At the level of the client, the token is validated. We'll cover later how that validation exactly happens. Important for us right now is to know that if validation checks out, the middleware will extract the user's identifier from the identity token. So this token, after it has been validated, is proof that the user is who they say they are. The client application now knows who the user is, and if we've got something that proves who the user is, client applications can use it for that purpose. A very difficult thing to do is thus create a claims identity from the information in that token and use it to sign into the MVC app. Now, I did deliberately skip on a few things to keep this simple. In reality, access tokens and refresh tokens can be returned from this token endpoint as well, but don't worry about that for now, we're covering all of that later on. So we've got an identity token and we're logged in. These were a few requests back and forth, but it's still quite okay, right. Now, as I mentioned in the beginning of the course, I feel it's important to know why certain decisions are made in regards to security. So let's learn a bit more about front channel versus back channel communication. When communicating via the front channel, the information is delivered to the browser, either via a URI or via a Form POST. That was that response mode value from the authorization request we saw on the slides earlier. So, when we use code as response type, we are returning the authorization code from the authorization endpoint via the browser. Other tokens, or new versions of the same ones, can then be returned through the back channel from the token endpoint, and that is server‑to‑server communication so the browser does not see this, which tends to be the safer option as tokens are not exposed to the browser. In fact, let's have a look at that best practices RFC again, more specifically, the section about delivering access tokens via the authorization endpoint, in other words, via the front channel. If we scroll down a bit, we read that in order to avoid issues like token leakage, clients should not use the implicit grant or any other response type that issues access tokens in the authorization response. In other words, via the front channel. Now that is not to say that this is the only way to solve these issues. In security, a lot of vulnerabilities can be solved in different ways, as can these, but an important principle in security is defense in depth, meaning that it is a good idea to put different types of protection against the same vulnerability in place. If one mechanism fails, the other mechanism would still be there, so I would suggest following this guideline, which is what we do and which is why the implicit flow is now obsolete. Anyway, looking back at our current flow, we do see quite a few steps. We need to create the initial request. We need to catch the identity token, we need to validate it, create a claims identity from it, and so on. We can do all of this manually, and that's actually a good thing. All any application built in any language needs to be able to work with OAuth and OpenID Connect is support for HTTP and a way to parse tokens, adjacent parse where it is often sufficient, but luckily, we don't have to do this manually. There's middleware that will help us with this. In the next few demos, we will learn how to log in with this flow, and we will encounter that middleware.

Demo - Configuring IdentityServer to Log in with The Authorization Code Flow
In this demo, we will make the required changes to allow logging into our MVC application. We're looking at the Config class at the level of our identity provider. Here, we must configure a client. We've got this client's method here that returns an IEnumerable of client, but no client is in there, so let's actually add one, instead of returning an empty list. So I new up a client. First of all, let's give it a name. Image gallery sounds like a good name as that is what we're working with, and let's also give it an ID, imagegalleryclient. This is the identifier we will use to refer to this client. As allowed ground types, we want to set the ground types that are allowed for this client. We are working with the code flow, so let's set it to code. There we go. When we just looked into the flow in detail, we learned that the code is delivered to the browser via URI redirection, that means we need to add a valid URI this client is allowed to receive tokens on. That URI is the host address of our Image Gallery web application followed by signin‑oidc. That last URI segment is something we can configure at the level of our web client. We're getting to that right after this, but signing OIDC is the default value, which is why we're using that. We also want to configure which scopes are allowed to be requested by this client. We've configured the identity server to support the OpenId and Profile identity resources. Those map to OpenId and profile scopes, so let's say that we want to allow both scopes for our client. Let's import the Duende.IdentityServer namespace for this to resolve. And lastly, we need to configure a client secret for client authentication to allow the client application to execute an authenticated call to the token endpoint. So we give it a secret, the value is secret, and we hash it so it can safely be stored, and that is all we need to do at the level of the IDP. Let's switch to the client in the next demo.

Demo - Logging in with the Authorization Code Flow
There's a bit of configuration required at the level of our web application as well. First of all, we'll need support for OpenID Connect. For that, let's open the NuGet dialog and let's browse for Microsoft.Asp.NetCore.Authentication.OpenIdConnect. There we go. Let's install it, and so far for that. Let's open the Program class at the level of our ImageGallery client. There's two things we need to do here. We need something to take care of the client‑side part of the OpenID Connect flow, and we need somewhere someplace we can use to store the user's identity. We start by calling into builder.Services.AddAuthentication. We'll use that to configure the authentication middleware. On the options, we set the DefaultScheme to cookies. That's a constant value that can easily be accessed via CookieAuthenticationDefaults.AuthenticationScheme. We do need to import the Microsoft.Asp.NetCore.Authentication.Cookies namespace for that. You can choose this value, but it should correspond to the logical name for a particular authentication scheme. In our case, we're quite okay with cookies. By setting this value, we can sign into this scheme, we can sign out of it, and we can read scheme‑related information and so on simply by referring to this cookies scheme name. This is not strictly necessary in our case, by the way, but I prefer to be explicit about this so we can get a better understanding of what's going on. Moreover, if you're hosting different applications on the same server, you'll want to ensure that these have a different scheme name in order to avoid them interfering with each other. We also need to set the DefaultChallengeScheme to a value, and we're going to set it to OpenIdConnectDefaults.AuthenticationScheme. Let me import the necessary namespace. This gives it OpenID Connect as value, and this will have to match the scheme we use to configure OpenID Connect, as we'll see soon. Then we call into AddCookie. We passed through an authentication scheme here, our CookieAuthenticationDefaults of AuthenticationScheme. This configures the cookie handler and it enables our application to use cookie‑based authentication for our default scheme. What this means is that once an identity token is validated and transformed into a claims identity, it will be stored in an encrypted cookie, and that cookie is then used on subsequent requests to the web app to check whether or not we are making an authenticated request. In other words, it's the cookie that is checked by our web app because we configured it like this. The next call we want to make is a call into AddOpenIdConnect to register and configure the OpenID Connect handler. This enables our application to support the OpenID Connect authentication workflow. In our case, that will be the code flow. In other words, it is this handler that will handle creating the authorization requests, token requests, and other requests, and it will ensure that identity token validation happens. Let's see what we need to set on this. We register these services for the OpenID Connect scheme. This will ensure that when a part of our application requires authentication, OpenID Connect will be triggered as default as we set the default challenge scheme to OpenID Connect as well. The next thing we see here is that the SignInScheme is set to cookies, and that matches the DefaultScheme name for authentication. This ensures that the successful result of authentication will be stored in the cookie matching this scheme. We have a few other things to configure. The authority should be set to the address of our identity provider because that is the authority responsible for the identity provider part of the OpenID Connect flows. The middleware will use this value to read the metadata on the discovery endpoint so it knows where to find the different endpoints and other information. Then there's the ClientId which should match the ClientId at the level of the identity provider, and we also pass through the client secret which matches the secret we configured at the level of the identity provider. This ensures that our client can do an authenticated call to the token endpoint. Up next is the ResponseType. We want to set that to code. ResponseType corresponds to a ground or flow. By setting it to code, we signify that we want to use the code ground. For that one, PKCE protection is required, and the middleware automatically enables this when code is the response type. We will look into that later. Up next are the scopes we want our application to request. Seeing we support the OpenId and profile scopes, those are the scopes we want to request. We can do that by calling into Scope.Add passing through the scope name. The thing is, though, we don't have to. By default, openid and profile scopes are requested by the middleware. So let me comment it out, but leave it there for reference. And then there's the callback path. Remember that redirect URI? We set it to our host, followed by the port, followed by a slash, followed by signin‑oidc at IDP level. We did that in the Config class. We also need to configure this at this site as it's part of the validation process of the request, but signin‑oidc is the default value used by this middleware, so we don't have to set this manually either. Again, I will leave it here for reference so you know how to change it in case you want to choose another endpoint. The last thing I'd like to do is set options.SaveTokens to true. This allows the middleware to save the tokens it received from the identity provider so we can use them afterwards. Now, let's scroll down a bit. Here, we can configure the request pipeline. What we want to ensure is that the authentication middleware is effectively used, and for that, we call into app.UseAuthentication. It's important that we add this to the request pipeline as we want the request to be blocked for unauthenticated users. A great place to put this is between UseRouting, so the middleware potentially has access to the route data, and MapControllerRoute, so the middleware can effectively block access to the endpoint. That's it for configuring the request pipeline. What we have to do now is ensure that a part of our application cannot be accessed without an authenticated user, more specifically our Gallery controller, so let's open that and let's decorate it with the Authorize attribute that's defined in Microsoft.Asp.NetCore.Authorization, so we need to import that namespace. Lastly, to make our lives a bit easier during the demos, let's add a bit of helper code to lock the identity token and resulting user object. That'll make it easy for us to see what's going on. So let me scroll down a bit and here near the bottom, let me paste that in. To get to a saved token, we call into GetTokenAsync on the HTTP context. To get access to that method, we do need to import to Microsoft.Asp.NetCore.Authentication namespace. The token we want to get, in this case, is an identity token, so we pass through OpenIdConnectParameterNames.IdToken, that simply refers to a string, id_token. So we have the identity token, and we log that, and next to the identity token, we also log all the user's claims. We can find those claims in the user object which is exposed by controller base, so on each controller, you have access to the user object which contains the claims that are the result of the complete OpenId Connect flow. And let me import system.text so StringBuilder resolves as well. Authorization Code Injection Attack
There's a problem with this authorization code flow. It's vulnerable to code injection attacks. That attack, in essence, means that an attacker got a hold of the authorization code linked to the victim. As we learned, that authorization code is short‑lived proof of who the user is, and it binds the front‑end browser session to the back‑end session between client and IDP. So if the attacker got hold of the victim's code, they can use that code in their browser session to switch their session to the victim's session. In other words, the attacker now has the victim's privileges. If you're interested, I added two URIs that describe the attack in a bit more detail. We're more interested in how to mitigate it. The advised way to do that is through the PKCE standard. PKCE means Proof Key for Code Exchange. The related RFC can be found via the link on the slide. Essentially, it means that for each request to the authorization endpoint, a secret is created by the client. When calling the token endpoint, that secret is verified. Tokens are only returned when the secret matches. Like that, code injection is mitigated because the attacker doesn't have access to the per‑request secret and will thus not get back anything from the token endpoint. Let's have a look at how that works when we fit it in with our previously described flow.

The Authorization Code Flow with PKCE Protection
The flow starts with our client application, the web app, creating a random string called a code verifier. The web app then hashes that code_verifier and that hashed version is called the code_challenge. Our web app creates an authentication request with response type code and it includes the code_challenge. The web app sends the request, it redirects to the authorization endpoint, and at level of the identity provider, the code_challenge is stored. The next steps are familiar. The user authenticates. The IDP now knows who the user is. The IDP optionally asks the user for consent. After that, the identity provider redirects back to the web application with the authorization code in the URI. The web client then calls the token endpoint authenticated with clientid and secret. It passes through the authorization code and the code verifier this time. The identity provider hashes this code verifier. It checks whether the hash matches the stored code_challenge. Only when it matches will the identity provider potentially return tokens. We're still ignoring all tokens, except the identity token, so let's assume that that is all that's being returned. This token is validated at level of the client app, and if validation checks out, claims identity is created from it and that is used to sign into our ASP.NET Core MVC app. And with that, you know what PKCE protection is all about, it's enabled by default. Up next is logging out.

Logging out of the Identity Provider
In this demo, we're going to ensure that we also log out of the identity provider when we click the Logout button. We're back in that log out action on our AuthenticationController. There's one statement we want to add here. We want to sign out of the OpenID Connect scheme as well. This will result in a redirection to the end session endpoint at level of the identity provider. That's one we haven't encountered before yet. This endpoint exists to allow the identity provider to clear its own cookie. In principle, applications can only clear their own cookies and not cookies belonging to other applications. There are a few exceptions to this rule, but that's the general idea, so that means that you need to redirect to some sort of endpoint at the level of the identity provider to allow it to clear its own cookie and thus log out of the identity provider, and that's exactly what this SignOutAsync statement does when we pass through the OpenID Connect scheme. Let's give that a try. Let's log in and let's log out, and we are now redirected to the identity provider and logged out of it. So far so good, but this is not really a good user experience either. We're stuck at the level of the identity provider. Let's have a look at identity servers output, and let's scroll up a bit. Here we go. There's a warning here. The post logout redirect URI, we input it, or rather which was inputted automatically by executing the sign out async statement was invalid. This is part of the reason we remain stuck at the level of the identity provider. We have not registered a URI, the identity provider should redirect to after logging out, so we need to fix that. Remember that it ends on signout‑callback‑oidc. We'll need that in the next demo.
