The UserInfo Endpoint
By default, Identity Server does not include identity claims safe for the user identifier in the identity token. We could allow this by setting the always include user claims in ID token property to true when configuring a client, but we don't want to do that. There's two main reasons for this. One, in some flows or variations of flows, the identity token can be returned from the authorization endpoint directly. If a set of claims are included in that token, it becomes bigger, potentially resulting in URI length limits. These are dependent on the browser, and that is not a good place to be. Most modern browsers don't have issues with long URIs anymore, but older browsers still do, so it still is something to keep in mind. Moreover, you don't know in advance which helps proxies, caches, and/or servers your requests and responses will pass through between your client app and your identity provider. It's not unrealistic that one of those won't play nice with long URIs. And two, identity tokens are sometimes passed around. For example, when ending a session or in some federation‑related scenarios. That increases the chance of an attacker getting a hold of it. Therefore, the less information that's in there, the better. Not including claims in the identity token that aren't required is thus the preferred approach, but how do we get access to those claims then? Well, we learned about the token endpoint and the authorization endpoint at level of the IDP, but there's another one, the user info endpoint. This is an endpoint the client application can call to get additional information on the user. Calling this endpoint requires an access token with scopes that relate to the claims that have to be returned. So if we want profile information from the user info endpoint, the access token must contain the profile scope. It is a bit too early to dive into access tokens in this part of the course, so for now, it's sufficient to know that our OpenID Connect middleware needs one to call that UserInfo endpoint. We'll cover this in more detail later on. Okay. So it needs an access token, but how does it get that? Well, remember our flow diagram. I mentioned we skipped on a few parts here and there to keep it simple. This was one of those parts. Let's run through it again. We'll skip on the first part. We already know about this. At the bottom of this diagram, the token endpoint is called. Let's continue. We're at a point where the tokens are going to be returned. Up until now, we only took the identity token into consideration, but as mentioned, other tokens can be returned from this endpoint as well. Access tokens and refresh tokens, for example. So in reality, with our current flow, an access token is returned next to that identity token. It is delivered to the client, so the middleware now has access to it. In the next step, after having validated the identity token, the middleware sends a request to the user info endpoint, passing through the access token. At that level, the access token is validated, and the user info endpoint returns the user's claims that are related to the scopes in the access token. Typically, those user claims are then added to the claims identity, so there's easy access to them throughout the application. So as you've noticed, we're gradually improving our flow. Let's continue with a demo.

Demo - Returning Additional Claims From the UserInfo Endpoint
In this demo, we're going to learn how to let the middleware call the UserInfo endpoint to get additional claims. As mentioned before, the middleware takes care of creating the requests, parsing the results, and so on. Having it call the UserInfo endpoint is a matter of setting the GetClaimsFromUserInfoEndpoint property to true. That should be it. Let's give this a try. Let's log in, and let's have a look at IdentityServer's output. Well, that's interesting. We see something new here. We see that a request to the UserInfoEndpoint ended successfully, and we also see that a few claims were issued. Let's have a look at the output of our web client, and there we go. We see that this time given_name and family_name were included. Just to be sure, let's have a look at that identity token again. The token still does not include our given_name and family_name claims, they are thus coming from the UserInfo Endpoint. By the way, if we look at our user interface, we still see all images from all users, so we are securing the web app one‑by‑one and step‑by‑step, but we're not doing anything with the API. That is still free for all and it still returns all images all the time. We will fix that once we look into securing the API. That's it for this demo. We've seen an identity token a few times now, and it includes quite a few values we haven't talked about yet. Let's have a look at what they mean.

Inspecting an Identity Token
The format of an identity token is JWT, or Jason Web Token. An example of a decoded identity token can be seen on screen. Let's start with what we know. To subvalue or subject. This is the user's identifier, a claim that is always returned when using OpenID Connect. As we know, optionally, other claims and their values can be returned as well matching the requested scope. For example, the given name. Iss stands for issuer, the issuer of the identity token. In our case, that's the identifier, a URI of our identity provider. Aud means audience. In other words, this is the intended audience for this token. In our case, that's the client application signified through the client ID. Then there's four numeric values. These values represent the seconds that have passed since January 1st, 1970. Iat, or issued at, is the time at which the JWT was issued. Exp, or expiration is the expiration time on or after which the identity token must not be accepted for processing. Nbf, or not before, identifies the time before which the token must not be accepted for processing, and auth_time represents the time of the original authentication. Amr means authentication methods references. This is a JSON array of strings that are identifiers for authentication methods used in the authentication. In our case, it's pwd, but in other cases, this might be a one‑time password for example, or a combination used in multi‑factor authentication, and then there's the nonce, or number only used once. This is a value that's generated at client level and that is sent back by the identity provided in the token. Comparing these during validation helps protect against cross‑site request forgery attacks. Lastly, we have the at_hash value or access token hash value. This is a Base64‑encoded value of the left most half of the hash of the octets of the ASCII representation of the access token. I don't know that from the top of my head, by the way, this is simply coming from the standard. It's not that important to know or remember the exact algorithm that leads to these numbers, but it is important to know that this is used to link the access token to this specific identity token. That means that validation checks and procedures can potentially use this value to ensure that the potential access token we're working with was effectively used during the same procedure. Now, this is just one example of an identity token. Identity providers sometimes add their own claims to a token. So your results may vary depending on the identity provider you're integrating with. The claims we looked at here are all part of the standard. Time for the module summary.

Summary
We learned how to use the authorization code flow with PKCE protection in this model. The response type for that is code. This means that the authorization code is returned via the front channel. When communicating via the front channel, the information is delivered to the browser. When communicating via the back channel, like when calling the token endpoint from an ASP.NET Core application, we're communicating from server‑to‑server, so the browser does not see this which makes it the more secure option. PKCE protection is required for this flow to avoid code injection attacks. During the flow, an identity token is returned from the token endpoint. From a validated identity token, a claims identity is created which is then used to log into the application. Such an identity token contains a set of claims. These can be returned directly in the identity token, but using the UserInfo endpoint for this is the preferred option. It keeps the identity token smaller and it keeps the attack surface smaller. As far as logging out is concerned, it's important to remember that just logging out of the application itself is not necessarily sufficient. You may also want to log out of the identity provider for which the end session endpoint is called via a sign‑out async call. Is that all there is to say about securing a web app? No, we learned that a claims identity is created from the identity token. In the next module, we will dive into working with these claims.

Working with Claims in Your Web Application
Coming Up
The information in the claims can be used for showing identity‑related information in our application, and they can be used for authorization. In this module, we will learn how. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. So this module is focused on working with claims. We will look into a bit of transformation first. Claims returned from the IDP are sometimes renamed to other claim types and often an application doesn't even need all the returned claims from the token. We will see how we can handle these cases. A very common claim to work with is a role claim. We will look into what we can do with that. Let's start with claims transformation.

Demo - Claims Transformation: Keeping the Original Claim Types
In this demo, we're going to transform claim types to something that's easier to work with. Our application is up and running and we're logged in. Let's have a look at that identity token content again. It contains claims like sub, idp, amr, and so on. Now, let's have a look at the claim types in the claims collection on our user object. We see that these claim types can sometimes be different. For example, instead of having a sub claim type, the claim type has a much longer identifier. What we want to do is ensure that our claim types stay the way we define them, instead of being mapped to different claim types. What actually happens is that once the identity token and additional claims are received by the middleware, a mapping dictionary is looked at. Depending on the defined mappings, claim types are mapped to different claim types, and it is quite easy to get rid of this. All we have to do is clear that mapping dictionary. If you've worked with this in the past, be careful, that mapping dictionary used to be defined on the JWT security token handler, which is what .NET used underneath the covers for all things related to JSON Web Tokens. However, since .NET 8, they switched to a new JSON Web Token handler, instead of the old JWT security token handler. The correct approach now is a call into JsonWebTokenHandler.DefaultInboundClaimTypeMap.Clear. JSON Web Token handler is defined in Microsoft or IdentityModel.JsonWebTokens, so let's import that namespace, and there we go. If you're interested, the reason why these default claim types look as they do is because these were the default claim types used in the past by Microsoft's WS security standard. So I'm assuming they put it in there for backwards compatibility, but in today's world, they don't really make that much sense anymore. Anyway, let's give this a try. Let's log in and let's have a look at the output. There we go. The original claim types from the incoming token are now kept as‑is. There's other things we can do. Maybe you don't need all these claims in your application. Let's see what we can do about that.

Demo - Claims Transformation: Manipulating the Claims Collection
In this demo, we'll get rid of the claims we don't need, and we'll add one we do need. We've got the claims list from the user object on screen, and we could just leave it like this, but it contains claims like idp, the identity provider, and sid, which is the session ID at the level of the IDP. Some applications might have a need for those, but we don't need them in our application. In fact, keeping them makes the cookie that contains the authentication ticket created from our claims identity larger than it has to be. On the other hand, if we have a look at the identity token, we see that there are claims in the identity token that are not in the identity like aud, audience, which is a claim that contains information on what the token is intended for. In our case, the client id of our web app. Let's say that, as an example, we'd like to have this in our claims identity. So what's going on here? Well, the OpenID Connect middleware, by default, filters out some claims it thinks we don't need like the not before time, expiration time, audience, and so on. We can effectively see that at work by having a look at the source code. In my opinion, that's one of the big advantages of ASP.NET Core going open source. You can simply have a look at how the internals work when you're not sure what's going on or when you want to learn something. We find that two scopes are requested by default, openid and profile. We already knew about that. We also find a few statements that delete claims. These claims are not added to the claim's identity. We see that, for example, the audience claim, not before time, and expiration time, and more are removed. And we find mappings to map incoming claims from the identity token to claims in the claims identity. We can manipulate this. Let's scroll down to where we set up our OpenID Connect middleware. On the options object, we can access ClaimActions. This allows us to add, change, or remove claim filters. To ensure that the audience claim is not filtered out, we remove that filter by calling into ClaimActions.Remove, passing through aud as the claim type, so this removes a filter. There's also extension methods on claim actions available we can use to remove claims. The lead claim is one of those extension methods. It's defined in Microsoft.Asp.NetCore.Authentication, so let's import that namespace, and now we have access to the DeleteClaim method, and we can use that to remove claims, so we pass through the types of the claims we want to remove. In our case, that's sid, and idp. To be honest, I find this naming to be quite confusing, especially needing to call remove to ensure that the audience claim is in the claims collection, but it is what it is, I guess. There's additional extension methods available as well. For example, to map a certain claim type from a token to another type in the claims collection in case you need them. We'll encounter this later on in this module. Anyway, let's give this a try. Let's log in, and let's have a look at the output, and there we go. This time, the claims list on our user object contains the audience claim, but it no longer contains sid and idp claims. That's it for this demo. Time to look into role‑based authorization.

Role-based Access Control
Up until now, we've worked with authentication. That is the process of determining who you are. The identity token, in our case, is proof that you are who you are. Authorization, on the other hand, is the process of determining what you are allowed to do, and there's a few approaches to that. The typical approach a lot of people are used to working with is role‑based authorization or access control, RBAC. This essentially means that a user is going to get one or more role claim values and such a role carries a specific set of privileges. For example, someone in the paying user role could be allowed to create an image. Attribute‑based access control, or ABAC, is another way of handling this, and it is often preferred over RBAC. There's a full module on that coming up, including working with authorization policies, later on in this course. It's a shared concern between a web client and an API, so we are covering it after we have covered securing the API. It's also known as claims‑based access control, CBAC, or policy‑based access control, PBAC. But anyway, as role‑based access control is still something a lot of us will come into contact with, let's learn how we can work with those roles in the next few demos.

Demo - Role-based Authorization: Ensuring the Role Is Included
In this demo, we're going to ensure that a user has a role and we'll make sure that our application gets access to a user's role or roles. The first thing to do is give our users a role claim, so let's open the test users class at the level of our identity provider. So let's say we've got free users and paying users. If a user is in the paying user role, additional functionality will be unlocked. Such a user will be allowed to add an image, so that is what we will use this role for. Let's add a new Claim role to David's claims list, and let's give it FreeUser as value. For Emma, we'll add a role claim with value paying user. That's it for our test users. Let's save this. What we have to do next is create a new identity scope. Let's open the Config class. We've got two identity resources defined at this moment, OpenId and Profile. These map to two standardized scopes, OpenId and Profile, but a role scope is not one of the standard defined OpenID Connect scopes, so we'll just have to add a new one. So let's new up an identity resource. The constructor allows us to state a scope name. Let's name that roles. A user can have one or more roles. Let's also input a display name, say Your roles, this will be shown at level of the consent screen, and lastly, we need to input a list of claims that must be returned when an application asks for this role scope. In our case, that's the role claim. So with this, we have defined a new identity resource, roles, which is available throughout our identity provider, but this alone doesn't yet mean that our client application is allowed to request it. We have to explicitly allow this for our client application, so let's scroll down to where we configure that, and let's add roles to the allowed scopes list. This is it as far as identity server configuration is concerned. From this moment on, the image gallery client will be allowed to request the roles scope, so let's do that. Let's switch to our client application and let's request one additional scope, roles, we already know how to do that by adding it to the Scope list. There we go. Let's build and run this. Let's log in as Emma. On the consent screen, we see that permission to access our roles as requested. So far, so good. Let's allow this. We're logged in, and let's have a look at the claims we see. We would expect the paying user role to be in the Claims list, but it isn't, so what's going on? Well, one of the first things to check is did the identity provider return it from the UserInfo endpoint? Let's check that. For that, let's have a look at the output of our identity server, and we see that, indeed, the UserInfoResponseGenerator tells us that the profile service returned a role claim, so what's going on here? Well, remember that previously we looked into removing claims and adding claims to the claims identity? For that, we used methods on claim actions. And before we did that, we looked at the defaults. Now, let's have a look at that again. We see that we have some mappings here, mappings that map incoming claim types to claim types in the claims identity. What we don't see here is a mapping for our role, so we need to add a mapping ourselves. That's easy enough to do. We can see on screen here how to do it. So on ClaimActions, we call into MapUniqueJsonKey. We pass through the claim type and incoming token, and we pass through the claim type we want to see in the user's Claims list, that's two times role, in our case. However, we don't necessarily want to use MapUniqueJsonKey. MapUniqueJsonKey is for keys or types that only show up once. However, seeing a user can be in multiple roles, the key might show up multiple times, so instead of using MapUniqueJsonKey, we use MapJsonKey. Alright, let's give this another try. Let's log in. Let's have a look at the output, and there we go. Our role claim is now in the claims list. So far, so good. Now, let's do something with that role. In the next demo, we will ensure that the button to add an image will only be visible for the users in the paying user role.

Demo - Role-based Authorization: Using the Role in Your Views
We're looking at our Image Gallery web client. In the top menu here, you see an Add an image button. We want to ensure that this one will only become visible when a user is in the paying user role. Let's open the shared layout file for that because that is where the button is defined. Here, we see our Add an Image button. If you leave it like this, every user will see this button. We want to avoid that and only show it when the user is in the paying user role. To do that, we can add a bit of code to check for that. On the User object, we call into IsInRole passing through PayingUser as the role type. If that evaluates to true, the link will be rendered. But to make this work, we need to tell the framework where it can find the user's role. Let's open the Program class. On the middleware options, we can set the TokenValidationParameters object. This contains configuration values on how the validation of the token should happen, but next to that, it also allows us to specify the NameClaim and RoleClaimTypes. Say we want to set the NameClaimTypes to one of the claims we return given_name and the role claim type to our role. Like this, when we ask for the user's name or we ask for the user's role, these claim types are what will be looked at. Alright, let's give this a try. Let's log in as David first. David is in the free user role, so we shouldn't see the button, and indeed, no button shows up. Let's log out, and let's log in as Emma. She is in the PayingUser role, so we should see a button. And indeed, there's our Add an image button. So far, so good. However, this isn't really secure yet. Let's log out again and log back in as David. The button isn't shown, but that's it. We're simply not showing the button, but we're not doing anything else. If we manually navigate to Gallery/Add image, David is still able to access this page. Let's close this, and let's continue with the next demo to fix this.

Demo - Role-based Authorization: Using the Role in Your Controllers
In this demo, we will learn how to authorize access to a specific action depending on the role. Let's switch to our GalleryController. Here, we want to ensure that only a user in the PayingUser role is authorized to access the action to add an image. Let's scroll down to that a bit. Here's the get action to add an image. This is the action that is executed when the user wants to view the page. If we decorate this action with the Authorize attribute and pass through PayingUser as roles value, we ensure that only users in the PayingUser role can access this. By the way, if you have multiple roles that are allowed access, simply separate them with a comma. In our case, we only have one role that will allow access to the AddImage method. What we don't want to forget is to also apply this attribute at the corresponding Post method for adding an image. It's not because you don't see a UI anymore or can't get the view anymore that you cannot send a Post action to a certain method. We want to explicitly disallow that as well. Alright, this should be it. Let's give this a try. Let's first log in as Emma. We see the button, and if we click it, we are allowed access. Let's log out and let's log in as David. We don't see the button, and if we manually navigate to gallery/addimage, we're not allowed access anymore. We see that the application tries to navigate to some sort of AccessDenied URI. Judging from the convention, it tries to find an access denied action on an account controller. That's the default behavior. It implies that we should have an account controller somewhere with an access denied action on it, but we haven't got that. And moreover, we don't necessarily want to keep that name. Let's see how we can change this.

Summary
We started this module by learning about claims transformation. We can make sure that the original claim types are kept by resetting the mapping dictionary. We also learned that we can manipulate the ClaimsIdentity's claim set through ClaimActions to ensure that claims are added or removed from the list. Removing claims we don't need keeps the cookies smaller. Lastly, we looked into what we could do with a role claim that's built in support in ASP.NET Core for this via the Authorize attribute. With that, we're done with securing the client application for now. Let's dive into OAuth2 and authorization in the next module.

Understanding Authorization with OAuth2 and OpenID Connect
Coming Up
In this module, we will learn how authorization fits into OpenID Connect. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. We're starting this module with an explanation of how OAuth2 works and how OpenID Connect superseded it. Once we know about that, we can check out how we can use OpenID Connect for authentication and authorization. After that, we will look into the OpenID Connect/OAuth2 flows, and we will inspect an access token. Let's dive in.

Learning How OAuth2 Works
We remember from the introduction module that OAuth2 is intended for authorization or delegated authorization to be exact, in other words, authorizing access to resources like an API. In those scenarios, a client application would request an access token from an authorization server. Let's assume that we're talking about letting a user decide on access, in other words, not just machine to machine communication. A user is involved in this example. The client application starts to flow, this redirects the user to the authorization server. Here, as we know by now, users prove who they are, for example, by providing a username and password. From this moment on, the authorization server knows who the user is. It can create an access token and sign it. As you already know, the arrows you see on screen are in reality more than just a request and a response. The access token is eventually returned from the token endpoint and it represents consent given by the user to the client application to access data the user owns, for example, resources exposed by the API. Client application stores this token and sends it as a bearer token on each request to the API. At the level of the API, the access token is validated and access to the resources can be granted or refused. That's the high‑level picture, but I've mentioned a few times already that OpenID Connect is a superior protocol. It is an extension of OAuth2. Even if only access tokens are required, you're often using OpenID Connect without even realizing it. It standardized additional claims and verification methods even for flows that don't require an identity token, so from that point of view, you could say that you're using OpenID Connect for both authentication and authorization, even though you will still encounter people who prefer to talk about OAuth2 when only authorization is required. It's a matter of preference, I suppose. Let's continue with extending our load diagram a bit.

Using OpenID Connect for Authentication and Authorization
Let's look at this from a high level first. We'll go into more detail in the next module. So we have a client application that requires authentication at client level and authorization to talk to an API. The client application creates an authentication request. This redirects the user to the identity provider. As you notice, we're again talking about an identity provider, instead of an authorization server. In this case, the identity provider includes the functionality of an authorization server, as is often the case. Here, users prove who they are, for example, by providing a username and password. The IDP now knows who the user is and what happens next depends on the flow that's being used as we know by now. Important is that eventually the client application will receive both an identity and an access token. In our case, those are coming from the token endpoint, so keep in mind that the arrows you see here on the slide in reality consist of different requests to different endpoints. In any case, the client gets those tokens. The identity token is validated and used as proof of identity. With this, we sign into the client app, the access token is stored by the client application on each request to the API it is sent as a bearer token. There is a limited form of validation going on that uses the access token like creating a hash from the token to check if it matches the AT hash value of the identity token. Yet, the actual place for validation of the access token is the API. So at the level of the API, the access token is validated and access to resources can potentially be granted. That's the high‑level picture. We already know about confidential and public clients from when we looked into OpenID Connect, and we know about the authorization token and redirection endpoints. These remain the same. That said, it is interesting to look into the flows of OAuth2 introduced and see how they relate to OpenID Connect.

OAuth2 and OpenID Connect Flows
You might remember this slide from when we looked into OpenID Connect, so I won't go into detail on this anymore. The ODIC authorization code flow and implicit flow are extensions to those two flows from the OAuth2 standard, so these still exist. The three variations of the hybrid flow only exist in OpenID Connect. There was no and is no hybrid flow in OAuth2, but as we know, hybrid and implicit are no longer best practice flows. If we can, we don't use them anymore. Next to that, OAuth2 also supports two other flows. Those are flows that don't exist in OpenID Connect. One of these is the Resource Owner Password Credential flow. This is the only flow that allows an in‑application login screen. The user isn't redirected to the identity provider to provide credentials. Instead, typically a username and password field are included in your client application, so this is something a lot of people are actually looking for because they want to have those input fields for username and password in their client application, but it was only added to OAuth2 for legacy reasons, and it should only be used by trusted applications. As we learned, these days, we really don't want to use in‑app login screens anymore. Checking credentials and so on should not be the responsibility of a client app. Moreover, this is not a redirection based flow which will make integrating with other identity providers through federation impossible. It makes single sign‑on scenarios harder and so on. Most of these are a requirement for enterprise applications. As it doesn't exist in OpenID Connect, using it would mean there is no identity token, and we can also not link an access token to such a verifiable identity token, so this one should be avoided. The other flow that is supported is the client credentials flow. As the name implies, this flow only uses client authentication. In other words, typically the client ID and the client secret. These are then exchanged for an access token. For that reason, it should only be used by confidential applications. It's also not part of OpenID Connect as OpenID Connect includes a user authentication step, and in this flow, there is no user. So this flow can be very useful when you require machine‑to‑machine communication without user involvement. Let's have a look at an access token.

Inspecting an Access Token
Unlike an identity token, an access token does not have to be a JWT, although it often is. Reference or opaque tokens, which we will cover later on, are a prime example of access tokens that are not in JWT format. A lot of the values we see here were already covered when we inspected the identity token. Let's focus on the ones that are different. We covered sub of issuer, so next is aud or audience. This field now has two values. We can see that the intended audience for this access token is no longer the client application. It's the name of an API scope, we can choose ourselves. This signifies that it is to be used at the level of the API, but there's another value, and that's at level of the identity provider. This signifies that this access token also has resources at our IDP as intended audience, and that makes sense. We pass through the access token when calling the UserInfo endpoint, and that lives at the level of the identity provider. Client_id is also new. This is the client identifier and it signifies the client application that requested a token. Note that we do get that same imagegalleryclient as a value in the identity token, but there it was part of the audience array as identity tokens are to be used at that level. The other values on this slide are the same as an identity token, so let's continue. We see scopes, openid, imagegalleryapi, and profile. That means that with this access token, we can get access to the ImageGallery API, an API resource, but also to identity‑related information. The profile scope, for example, ensures that when we call the UserInfo endpoint with this access token, we will receive profile‑related information about the user. And lastly, we have the authentication methods reference also the same as in an identity token. Alright. What about we start using this? Onto the next module after this one's summary.

Summary
We learned that we can use OpenID Connect for authentication and authorization. OpenID Connect offers additional security features that are not available in OAuth2. Access tokens and identity tokens are returned from the identity provider, and the access token is sent to the API as a bearer token on each request. There, it is validated. As far as flows are concerned, the authorization code flow with PKCE protection is the way to go for our application. OAuth2 defines two flows that are not available in OpenID Connect. The resource owner password credentials flow, included for legacy reasons only, should be avoided. The client credentials flow can be used, but it's only there for machine‑to‑machine communication. There is no user involved. We also know what an access token looks like now, so I'd say it's time to dive into using this knowledge to secure our API in the next module.

Securing Your API
Coming Up
It's not sufficient to just secure the client. Arguably, securing the API is even more important as that is where the data resides. In this module, we will learn how to do that. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. So in this module, we will learn how to secure our API. We will start by checking out our well‑known authorization code flow with PKCE protection again in more detail, and this time, including an access token to access the API. Then we'll dive right into the demos. We will learn how we can get and pass an access token to the API on each request, and we will learn how to validate that token at the level of the API. Along the way, we will learn about API scopes and API resources. Then, we will start using information from the token at API level, so we can ensure that only the correct images are returned for an authorized user. Lastly, we will learn how we can include additional identity claims in an access token. This will allow us to use role‑based authorization at the level of the API. Let's dive in.

The Authorization Code Flow with PKCE Protection
This flow will look very familiar, after all, it's the same flow we've used before in the course with the advised response type code and with PKCE protection. Our web application starts by creating a random string called a code_verifier. The web app then hashes that code_verifier, and that hashed version is called the code_challenge. Our web application creates an authentication request with response type code and includes the code_challenge. The web application sends the request, and at level of the identity provider, the code_challenge is stored. The user authenticates and the IDP optionally asks for consent. After that, the identity provider redirects back to the web app with the authorization code in the URI. The web application then calls the token endpoint authenticated with clientid and clientsecret, and it passes through the authorization code and the code_verifier. The identity provider hashes this and checks if it matches the stored code_challenge, only if that's the case will the IDP return tokens. We get an access token and identity token back. The identity token is validated at the level of the web client. Part of this validation is calculating the hash from the access token to see if it matches the at hash value in the identity token, so the access token takes part in the validation procedure of the identity token. If validation checks out and a claims identity is created from the identity token, and that is used to sign into our ASP.NET Core MVC web application. We've also got an access token now. Optionally, as we know, the UserInfo endpoint can be called for additional user information. We, however, are more interested in calling another API, our applications API. So the access token is stored, and on each request to the API, the token is included as a bearer token value for the authorization request header. At level of the API, the access token is validated. This validation procedure is different from the validation that happens at the level of the web application. The specifics are looked into later on in the course. If validation is successful, access to the resources can be granted, depending on whatever your authorization rules are at API level. Let's check that out in the upcoming demos.

Demo - Securing Access to Your API (Part 1)
In this demo, we will secure access to our API. The first thing we'll do is add support for an additional scope. We will require an access token with that scope in it before we allow access to the API. We're looking at the Configuration class at level of our IDP. Now, even though I am talking about scopes, I am not going to add an API scope. Instead, I'm going to add an API resource. This may look a bit weird, but there's a good reason for this. I'll explain it right after this demo. So the ApiResources method returns an IEnumerable of ApiResource. To this array, we add one new API resource. We'll give it imagegalleryapi as name and Image Gallery API as display name. In the list of allowed scopes for our client, we want to add the imagegalleryapi scope to the list. Like that, clients can request it, and in the hosting extensions file where we set up identity server, we need to ensure that this API resources list, which I just created, is effectively used. We do that by calling into AddInMemoryApiResources passing through our list, but as you can see, there's also a call to AddInMemoryApiScopes here. And on top of that, as we've already seen in our config cloud, there is an ApiScopes IEnumberable, which we can also use. So what's the deal with that, and why am I using an API resources list instead of an ApiScopes list when talking about scopes? Let's have a look.

API Scopes vs. API Resources
The scope concept is pretty old. It's coming from the OAuth2 specification, and it simply means the scope of access that is requested by a client. So you could have a read scope which would allow a client access to read resources at level of the API, a write scope which would allow access to write certain resources, and so on. It's up to us at the level of the API to read out these scopes and use them to allow or disallow certain things. So that is a simple approach, but it's often not sufficient. Resource is another concept that allows for more complex structures. You can look at a resource as a physical or logical API. In our case, the Image Gallery API is a resource. In more complex systems, you may have multiple physical APIs which can all be different resources, or you might decide to split up your one physical API into different logical APIs, all with their own resource name, for example, an employee API, a customer API, and so on. These resources can have scopes which can be used for more fine‑grained authorization rules inside of the API. For example, our imagegalleryapi resource can have an imagegallery.read and imagegallery.write scope, so this allows more flexibility. Also, it's not hard to imagine that different client applications that need access to our Image Gallery API are allowed different levels of access inside of that API. We could, for example, give our Image Gallery web client access to the imagegallery.read and imagegallery.write scopes, but give a yet to be developed mobile image gallery client only access to the imagegallery.read scope. Whenever a scope related to a resource is requested by a client application, the access token will contain the resource as an audience value, and the scope will be in the scopes list. So if our web client would request imagegalleryapi.read and imagegalleryapi.write scopes, we would end up with an access token with imagegalleryapi in the audience list and imagegalleryapi.read and imagegalleryapi.write in the scopes list. If a mobile client requests imagegalleryapi.read, we would end up with a token with imagegalleryapi in the audience list and imagegalleryapi.read in the scopes list. And like that, you can use these scopes to build a fine‑grained authorization layer for your API. For our current demo, we will keep things simple. We'll create one scope, imagegalleryapi.fullaccess linked to our Image Gallery API resource. We will only use the audience value for now. In the next module, we will dive deeper into authorization. There, we will extend our example with different scopes and policies that effectively use these scopes. Let's continue with our demo.

Demo - Securing Access to Your API (Part 2)
So, let's adjust our example. Let's define a scope, imagegalleryapi.fullaccess. We add this scope to the imagegalleryapi API resource. The result will be that when the imagegalleryapi.fullaccess scope is requested, we'll end up with an access token with imagegalleryapi.fullaccess in the scope list and imagegalleryapi in the audience list. Now let's scroll down a bit and let's make sure that the allowed scopes list contains imagegalleryapi.fullaccess at level of our client configuration. Like that, the client is allowed to request it. That's it for our identity provider. Now let's open the Program class at level of our web client. Here, we want to ensure that we get an access token with this scope included. We're already getting an access token as we just learned through the back channel via the token endpoint, so in that regard, we're good, all we have to do is add the imagegalleryapi.fullaccess scope to the requested list of scopes, and that should be it, but our API still isn't secure. We're only working on the client at this moment, so let's make sure that our API effectively requires an access token. Let's switch to the API project and let's open the NuGet dialogue. The first thing we want to do is look for the Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authentication.JwtBearer package. There it is. This contains the middleware we can use to check the access token which is passed through as a bearer token. Let's install it, and let's open the Program class at level of the API. Here, we will want to configure the JWT Bearer middleware. Much like we did on the client, we call into AddAuthentication on the Services collection. This time, we pass through bearer as key. There's a constant for that we can use which is defined in Microsoft,AspNetCore.Authentication.JwtBearer, so let's import that namespace. As you can see, it contains bearer as value. On this, we call in to AddJwtBearer, that's coming from the package we just added. We passed through the authority, that's the address of our identity provider. The middleware uses this to load metadata so it knows about the endpoints and keys. When this middleware is hit for the first time, it will read that metadata from the IDP. It will also catch the result so it doesn't have to continuously read it. This middleware is also responsible for validating the access tokens. As valid value for the audience property, we pass through imagegalleryapi. This makes sure that imagegalleryapi is checked as an audience value in the token. Another small something that is relatively new is checking the type header of the token. This is done to avoid JWT confusion attacks. That's an attack that allowed APIs to become confused between quotation marks in regards to the tokens it would accept as valid. It allowed attackers to circumvent token signature checking by providing an arbitrary token protected with an HMAC. Needless to say, allowing an API to accept arbitrary tokens is very bad because you could just generate an arbitrary token yourself. One way to mitigate this is via this type check. This didn't exist a few years ago, so this just goes to show how fast attacks are discovered and standards that mitigate them are developed. Security is a very fast moving subject. All it takes is one line of code. We state that the only valid type we want to accept should have at+jwt as type. What I also prefer to do is ensure that the incoming claims and validation on those claims are mapped and executed the same way as they are on the client like on the API if both are under your control. That avoids a lot of potential confusion and seemingly unexplainable issues. Having things insync helps. As mentioned before, since .NET 8, the token handling is done by the new JSON Web Token handler, instead of the old JWT security token handler, so the correct way to do this is by calling into JsonWebTokenHandler.DefaultInboundClaimTypeMap.Clear just like we did at level of the client app. And when setting up the token validation parameters, we do not only want to pass through valid types. We also want to state that the name claim type is given_name and the RoleClaimType is role just like we did on our client. We're almost there. We need to add the authentication middleware to the request pipeline, so let's scroll down a bit and let's call into app.UseAuthentication. This is much like what we've done on our MVC client, right? We want to make sure we add this middleware to the request pipeline before the call into map controllers. Like that, this middleware can check if API access is allowed before the request is passed through to our controller actions. That's it for the Program class. Now, we only need to make sure that the ImagesController, which is what's interacted with from our web client actually requires authorization, so we decorate it with the Authorize attribute which is defined in Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authorization, and that should be it. Let's give this a try. Let's log in. On the consent screen, we see something new showing up. Next to requiring permission to access my personal information, we are now also asked to give permission to access an application, that's our API. Let's allow this, and we get an error 401 unauthorized. It looks like the client application is no longer allowed access to the API, so this is okay. Our API is now secured. To make sure our client is allowed to access the API again, we need to pass that access token to the API on each request. Let's do that in the next demo.

Demo - Passing an Access Token to Your API
In this demo, we will re‑enable the client application to access our API by passing the access token through, that should be done on each request through the API, and the token should be passed as a bearer token. There's various ways of approaching this. We're looking at the Index section on our GalleryController. Here, an API client is created which is an HTTP client I registered in the Program class. It makes it easy to interact with our API. And what we could do now is get the token from our context here and manually add it as a bearer token, but that would mean that we would have to add code to add that access token at each place in our code where we use our API client, and that is quite annoying. Personally, I think creating a custom delegating handler that would be responsible for adding the access token on outgoing requests is a clean reusable approach. In fact, that's the approach I took in a previous version of this course which targeted ASP.NET Core 3.1. You can have a look at it over there if you're interested, but the good news is that since then life became easier. There's a popular package IdentityModel.AspNetCore which contains such a handler, so we don't have to write it ourselves anymore. Let's manage the ImageGallery.Client NuGet packages list, and let's browse for IdentityModel.AspNetCore. This package is created by the people who created IdentityServer. Let's add it, and there we go. Now, let's open the Program class at level of our ImageGallery.Client, and there's two things we want to do here. First, we want to call into builder.Services.AddAccessTokenManagement. This registers the required services for access token management coming from our IdentityModel.AspNetCore package. And secondly, we need to add a call into AddUserAccessTokenHandler when configuring our API client. This will ensure that the access token is passed on each request to the API that is made by our API client and that's it already. While we're at it, let's extend the method that logs the identity token, so it additionally logs the access token, that'll make it easy for us to inspect it later on. So here's the LogIdentityInformation method. We get the saved AccessToken, now we log it. Let's give this a try. Let's log in and there we go. We are again able to access our API because we're passing through a valid access token. Let's have a look at that access token. If you open the output of our ImageGallery.Client, we see that it is written out to the console output window, and let's paste it in. The first thing we see is that at+jwt is added as type to the token header. IdentityServer, by default, does that. What we see when we look at the access token contents are the requested scopes, including our imagegalleryapi full access scope. Imagegalleryapi is one of the values of the audience claim. The token thus allows access to our API as our API is one of the intended audiences for the token. What we also notice is that other scopes are included in this token like profile, roles, and openId. Why would that be? Well, you might remember that our OpenID Connect call calls the UserInfo endpoint, and while doing so, passes through this access token. Well, that is why the address of our identity provider is in the audience's list. The identity provider itself where the user info endpoint lives must allow access to it with this token, and that is also why openid profile and roles are in the scope list. This allows the UserInfo endpoint to know which information about the user to return, so the identifier, profile‑related information, and potential roles. Interesting to know is that in some identity provider implementations, it is assumed that a token that is issued by an identity provider also allows access to the UserInfo endpoint of that identity provider by default, so the reference to resources at the level of the identity provider isn't always included in the audience list. But anyway, that depends on the identity provider, so this is how all of this nicely ties together. Scopes provide access to resources which can be APIs, but also identity‑related resources. Let's close this and let's give this another try. This time, we're going to disallow the client application to access the API. For that, we uncheck imagegalleryapi.fullaccess. And there we go, we no longer have access. That's because validation of the audience value is one of the included steps of access token validation. We still have a problem though. All the images are still returned regardless of the user, and that's not okay. Let's learn how we can fix that.

Demo - Using Access Token Claims When Getting Resources
Currently, the call to get images still returns all images regardless of who the user is. We want to ensure that only images for the current user are returned. We're looking at the ImagesController at API level. It's the GetImages action that is called when the web client requests a list of images. We need to allow access to this action, but at the same time, we need to limit the amount of images returned to the images of the current user, but how do we know who the current user is? Well, just as User.Identity on the controller is a claims identity at level of the client coming from the identity token, there's a user object at the level of the API as well. After all, it's the same controller‑based class we're working with. But this time at API level, the ClaimsIdentity is constructed from the access token. So we access the current Users.Claims and look for the sub claim that identifies the current user as we learned before. From the repository, we only want to return those images that belong to this user, so we passed through that owner ID to the GetImagesAsync method. We could write an overload for that allowing the GetImagesAsync method to still exist without requiring an ownerId parameter, and that's perfectly valid if you have a use case for getting images regardless of the user, but we don't, so instead of creating a new method, we'll change the existing one. The reasoning behind this is that at any level of your application, if you don't need a piece of code, don't leave it hanging around. So let's open the IGalleryRepository interface, and let's ensure that the GetImagesAsync method requires the ownerId to be passed in. On to GetImagesAsync method implementation on the GalleryRepository class. This now accepts an ownerId, and we can now add a Where clause to ensure that only images for which the ownerId equals the passed‑in ownerId are fetched and returned, and that should be it. Let's give this a try. Let's log in as Emma, and there we go. Only Emma's images are returned and shown in the UI. Let's have a look at our ImagesController again. Getting an image collection is currently the only place where we need to enter the action to filter the data that's returned, but we've got other actions here. We've got an action to get one image, to edit one image, or to delete one image. Surely, we want to protect those as well. We need to ensure that a specific image is only accessible to get, update, or delete if the user in the token owns that image. If we don't do that, anyone with a valid token will be able to manually call the API to get other people's images, assuming they know the URI. As an example, let's fix that when getting one image. We can fix it by getting the user ID from the token and checking if that's actually the image owner, so this would be much like what we did in the GetImages method. The problem with this is that like that we still have to enter the action. There's a better way of handling situations like this, a way that effectively blocks access to the action altogether, authorization policies. We are looking into that in the next module. For now, we will leave this code as it was, so remember that this isn't completely okay yet, but we will fix it later on. So let's refer it to what it was. There we go. Let's learn how to include additional identity claims, so not just a sub value, in our token. A good example would be the role.

Including Identity Claims in an Access Token
Adding a new image, that sounds like something we don't want to allow anyone to do. Let's say we only want to allow users that are in the PayingUser role to add an image. That's a bit of an issue. As we've seen an access token contains scopes, but no identity claims. In theory, we could call the UserInfo endpoint with an access token from our API, but that would mean an additional call to the identity provider on a lot of requests. That's quite a bit of additional traffic to the IDP, and it can result in a slowdown. Moreover, even though it is technically possible, the UserInfo endpoint is intended to be called from the client that requested a token and not from the API that requires that to. There's another way. We can ensure that claims are returned directly in the access token when asking for a scope by including them in the claims list related to that scope related API resource. In the next two demos, we will ensure that the user role is included in the access token, and we will use it to secure our action to create an image.

Demo - Including Identity Claims in an Access Token
In this demo, we will ensure that the role claims are returned in the access token. Let's open the Config class at level of our identity provider for that. Here, we defined our API resource. There's an overloaded constructor available which allows us to input a list of claims that have to be returned when requesting the related scope, so let's add that to an enumerable that contains the role claim type. There we go. We do not have to change anything at level of our web client. The imagegalleryapi.fullaccess scope is already requested. Let's build our own. Let's log in, and let's have a look at the output. Here's our access token that's inspected, and there we go. The access token now indeed contains the user's role. So far, so good. In the next demo, we'll make good use of this role claim at API level.

Demo - Protecting the API When Creating a Resource (with Roles)
In this demo, we will ensure that the API is protected when creating a resource. We will ensure that only users in the Paying User role are allowed to do this. We're looking at the ImagesController at the API level. Let's scroll down to the CreateImage method, and let's decorate it with an Authorize attribute passing through PayingUser as the value for all. When a valid access token is passed through to the API which contains a role, the role from the token will be in the user's claims, which is what this statement will look at. But while we're at it, let's have a look at the ImageForCreation class. This is what the body of the request is serialized to. It contains a title and the bytes used to create an image from. What is important here is what is not in this class. There's no ID as in our implementation the API is responsible for choosing that, and also there is no owner ID, and that's on purpose. Let's open the Image entity class and put it next to this so we can compare, and the Image class does have an owner ID, which means that in our database, there is an owner ID. So why is there no owner ID in the ImageForCreation class. Well, it's very important that we do not send the owner ID from client to API in the URI or in the request body. It's the API that has to fill this out in the action to create the image itself. Let's open that. If we scroll down a bit, you can see that I already had a part in the code here that's currently commented out in relation to this, so let's get the owner ID and fill it out. We get a sub value from the claims collection, which is the user identifier, and we set it as owner ID. Now, why is this so important? In other words, why should you get the owner ID like this instead of sending it in the URI or sending it in the request body? Well, this owner ID, this sub value is coming from the access token, and that is something that was validated, and thanks to the fact that it was signed, we also know that it wasn't tampered with it, and that's not something you can say from IDs passed through via the URI or the request body. That type of data isn't signed, so it's more easy to tamper with. Working like this ensures that it's always the correct owner ID in case someone with bad intentions tampers with what's sent over the wire. This may seem like a small thing to keep in mind, but I know from experience that it's these small things that are easy to forget, and a small mistake like that can have dire consequences. We're looking at the Image entity again, it's especially easy to make mistakes against this when using entity classes to serialize as the Image entity includes both an ID and an owner ID. So a good tip never work with the entities directly for serialization and keep the DTOs separate, do not reuse them across actions. Let's give this a try. I'm going to put a breakpoint in our images controller so we can see what happens. Let's log in as Emma. She is a paying user, so we should be able to upload an image. Let's choose to add an image. I've got one ready here. Let's select it and let's give it a title, and let's click AddImage. We hit our breakpoint. Note that this means that the role was checked and it apparently checked out, our roll check thus works. And if we look at the owner ID value, we indeed see Emma's ID. So far, so good. That's it for this module. Let's have a look at the module summary.

Summary
We learned how to use the code flow with PKCE protection to achieve authorization. After we get an access token, it needs to be passed to the API as a bearer token where it is validated with the JWT bearer token middleware. We can use API scopes for simple scenarios and API resources for more complex scenarios combined with scopes. Most APIs will benefit from using resources linked to scopes, which is what we did, that resulted in an access token with both audience and scope claims. At the level of the API, we can use the user object to get claims that are in the access token. We can use this sub value to ensure the list of images is filtered, and if we need additional identity claims, we can configure that at level of Identity Server. As an example, we included the role claim in the access token so we could use it for role‑based authorization. So, now we've used that role at level of the web application and at level of the API, but that is only a small sample of what can be achieved as far as authorization is concerned. In fact, role‑based authorization has been surpassed by a more flexible approach. In the next module, we will look into authorization policies and access control.

Authorization Policies and Access Control
Coming Up
Building an authorization layer goes beyond just requiring a token with the correct audience. We need to be able to check off complex rules like only allowing access to a specific resource if the resource belongs to the current user. Authorization policies help with that. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through this module. We've worked with role‑based authorization at the level of the web app and API, but these days, another type of access control is preferred. Attribute‑based access control, or ABAC, that is then used in combination with an authorization policy. Authorization policies allow much more than just replacing a few pieces of role‑based authorization. When used correctly, they result in a full‑fledged authorization layer. In this module, we'll look into that, both at level of the web client and at level of the API. To start, let's have a look at the differences between role‑based access control and attribute‑based access control.

Role-based Access Control vs. Attribute-based Access Control
We've used role‑based access control, or RBAC, up until now, but there's an alternative, attribute‑based access control, or ABAC, sometimes also referred to as claims‑based access control, CBAC, or policy‑based access control, BBAC. Let's put these two next to each other. In RBAC, we define a set of predefined roles like free user or paying user. Each role carries a specific set of privileges, I.E. a paying user can add an image. In ABAC, access rights are granted to users through policies. A policy combines a set of attributes together. An attribute might be the country someone comes from or a subscription level. In other words, these attributes are the claims we defined. So yes, a role claim is also an attribute we can use in a policy. It's just one of many attributes in that case. And these policies allow for much more complex rules. For example, if a user is from country A and has role B and was born between 1980 and 1985, then they are allowed action X. If we try to put requirements like that in roles, we quickly end up with tens to hundreds of different roles, while we would typically just need a few policies to replace all of them. ABAC has been around for a while and it is preferred over role‑based authorization. In ASP.NET Core, there is built‑in support for creating authorization policies. So we have used RBAC in both our web app and at API level in the previous modules. In the upcoming demos, we're going to use ABAC, instead of RBAC, and we're going to do that with authorization policies. We'll start with focusing on the web client application. Let's say that just checking a user's role isn't sufficient anymore to allow them to add an image. We want to slowly roll out this functionality, so we will start by only enabling it for users living in Belgium that are in the PayingUser role. Simple role‑based authorization won't cut it, in this case. We need to use an additional attribute to country claim value. Note that we will still use the role as well. In ABAC, the role is a claim or attribute like any other claim, so it can be used typically in combination with other claims. We will start by doing that at the level of the web client. At the level of the API, we can go one step beyond that, but that's coming up after these demos. Let's continue.

Demo - Creating an Authorization Policy
In this demo, we're going to create a policy we'll then use for authorization, instead of the role‑based authorization we've worked with previously. To allow for that, we're going to need a country claim to use in that policy, so let's add such a claim. We're in the Config class of our identity provider. Let's add a new identity resource here linked to the country scope which will result in the country claim being returned. We already know how to do that. We will also ensure that our client is allowed to request the country by adding the scope to the AllowedScopes list on our client configuration. And lastly, we'll add a country claim for each user, so let's open the TestUser class. There's our list of users. Let's let David live in the Netherlands and Emma in Belgium. There we go. Let's save this because that's it already for the identity provider. On to our web client application. In the Program class where we set up the OIDC middleware, we want to request the country scope, so we add that to the Scope list. From this moment on, the country claim will be returned from the UserInfo endpoint. As we want it to be in our claims identity, we also add the necessary mapping. We call into MapUniqueJsonKey on ClaimActions stating that we want to map the incoming country claim to a claim of type country in our claims identities claims list. Now let's create a policy. Often, but not always, policies are reused to cross service boundaries, by which I mean that if you want to check something on the client, you will typically also want to check it at the level of the API. Most of the time, that last part is even more important than what's going on on the client. Therefore, I prefer putting these policies in a class library to encourage reuse, so let's add one. We choose to add a new project. We look for class library and we select the Class Library template. Let's name it ImageGallery.Authorization. Now let's click Create. To this class library, we want to add one static class, AuthorizationPolicies, so let's just reuse that automatically generated Class1 file here, and let's rename the file itself as well. There we go. In this class, we're going to use the AuthorizationBuilder to build a policy, so we add a new static method, CanAddImage, that returns such an authorization policy. The authorization policy type doesn't seem to resolve, that's because we are missing a NuGet package, so let's right‑click the project file and choose to search for NuGet packages, and the one we're looking for is Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authorization. Let's select it, and let's install it. There we go. And now we can import the Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authorization namespace which results in the AuthorizationPolicy resolving. So what do we want to do here? Well, we're going to use the Authorization Policy Builder to build an AuthorizationPolicy. So the first thing we need is an AuthorizationPolicyBuilder. We new one up, and through it, we build a policy. There's quite a few things we can require. We can require a certain claim, required a certain role, username, require an assertion, and so on. All of these help us with building our policies. We're going to start with requiring an authenticated user, and then we're going to require a certain claim. The claim we require is country, and the value we need it to be is Be for Belgium. Then we also want to require a certain role. We need the user to be in the PayingUser role, and that's already it as far as the requirements of our policy are concerned. We call into build which results in our AuthorizationPolicy being created. It's that that is returned from this method. Note that if multiple roles are okay, you can just add them to the list here, and the same goes for values for the claims you require. For example, if we want to allow both people from Belgium and the Netherlands, code like this would make sure of that. Alright, let's save this, and that's it for our policy. Onto our web client. First, let's add a reference to ImageGallery.Authorization. So we choose to add a project reference, and we select ImageGallery.Authorization. Alright, then let's open the Program class. To add a new policy, we call in to AddAuthorization on the Services object. This accepts an options parameter. Let's name it authorizationOptions, which is used to configure this. On these authorizationOptions, we can call AddPolicy. We give our policy a name, say UserCanAddImage, and we refer to the CanAddImage static method we created in our class library, so that's AuthorizationPolicies for which we need to import the ImageGallery.Authorization namespace .CanAddImage. Alright. Let's give this a try. Let's log in. We see that our country is requested. So far, so good. Let's allow this, and let's have a look at the output, and there we go. We see that the country claim is now indeed in our claims identity. We also created a policy, but we're not doing anything with it yet. Let's check that out in the next demo.

Demo - Using an Authorization Policy (Web Client)
In this demo, we're going to replace our existing role‑based authorization with authorizing through our new authorization policy. We check for the role at two different places in our web client. First place is in the user interface. Let's open the Shared _Layout page. Here, we check whether the user is in the PayingUser role to decide whether to show the Add an image button. We want to replace this, and the first thing we need to be able to replace this is an authorization service. so let's scroll to the top of this file and inject it. We name the injected instance AuthorizationService. From this moment on, we can use this authorization service, so let's scroll down a bit. We want to replace this User.IsInRole call. What we want to do is call into the AuthorizeAsync method on the AuthorizationService, and seeing it's an async method, we also want to await that. This allows us to pass through the user object and the policy name. That AuthorizeAsync call results in an authorization result, and that authorization result has a property succeeded, which we can check to see whether or not the policy was fulfilled, so this line of code is all that is needed here. But as we know from when we implemented role‑based authorization, that is not enough. We also want to ensure that the action, AddImage, can only be executed when this policy is fulfilled. For that, we open the GalleryController. We've got an Authorize attribute here on the AddImage action for role‑based authorization. We can change that to policy‑based authorization. We do that by setting the Policy property to our policy name, UserCanAddImage, and we do the same for the corresponding post action. There we go. By the way, a big advantage of using policies, instead of some custom code in a controller action is that if something changes to our rules, we only have to change the code in one place where we defined the policy. Let's give this a try. Let's first log in as David. His claims shouldn't allow him to add an image. We don't see the Add an image button, so far so good. And if we try to manually navigate to the Add image URI, we get an access denied. In other words, both checks, the one on the view and the one at action level work. Let's log out and let's log in as Emma. Emma does see the Add an Image button, and if we click it, we indeed see the Add Image view. In other words, the policy checked out, but what about the API? We still only check the role at the level of the API. Let's fix that in the next demo.

Demo - Using an Authorization Policy (API)
In this demo, we will reuse our policy at API level, but first things first. Let's first add a reference to our ImageGallery.Authorization project. The API project doesn't have a reference to that yet, and there we go. Then let's open the Program class level of the API, and let's register the policy in that class. All I need to do is import the ImageGallery.Authorization namespace, and now let's apply it to CreateImage action. Here's that CreateImage action. And here too, we do the same thing as we did on the client. That's easy enough, right? But this won't work yet. Let's try it anyway to see what happens. Let's log in as Emma, and let's choose to add an image. Let me select one. Now let's click Add and we hit an issue. We get back a 403 Forbidden. What's going on? The first thing we can gather from the stack trace is that the 403 forbidden was returned from the API. It's on the response.EnsureSuccessStatusCode. Let's have a look at the output. What I'm interested in is the access token, so let me copy this and let's have a look at what's in there or rather what's not in there. What's missing here is one of the claims that's used in our policy, the country claim, and without that country claim being there, the policy can never be fulfilled because it cannot be checked, and therefore, we eventually get back a 403 Forbidden. So what happened here? Well, identity‑related scopes like country and role that are requested result in the related claims ending up in the identity token, but not in the access token. To get the claims in our access token, we need to configure our identity provider in such a way that the claims are returned when we request a scope related to an API resource like ImageGalleryApi.FullAccess. So what we need to do here is the same thing we needed to do to get our role to show up at the level of the API, and that is change a little bit of configuration at level of our identity provider. So let's open the Config class and let's scroll up a bit to where we defined the imagegalleryapi ApiResource. Here, we add the claim we need to the claims list, and the claim we need a level of the API when imagegalleryapi.fullaccess is requested as a scope is the country claim next to the role claim, and that should be it. There is no need to change anything else. The imagegalleryapi.fullaccess scope is already requested by our client. Let's try that again. Let's log in as Emma again, and let's add an image, let's click Add image, and there we go, our image was added. This again works as expected, but can we do better? Well, we actually can by extending on how we use scopes. Let's check that out next.

Fine-grained Policies with Scopes
In the previous module, we learned about API resources and API scopes. I mentioned there that scopes can be used inside of an API to check whether or not something is allowed. You can see an example on screen now of such an access token. Important to remember is that we are now talking about non‑user related rules. It's client applications that can be allowed to request scopes or that can choose to request them regardless of who the actual user is, so this is another level of authorization. Lots of applications combine both. We haven't used this yet though. In the previous module, we made our life easy by only defining one scope, imagegalleryapi.fullaccess. Well, we're going to improve on that. We're going to define two new scopes, imagegalleryapi.read and imagegalleryapi.write. We will check for that last scope at API level before allowing access to the action to add an image. On to the demo.

Demo - Fine-grained Policies with Scopes
First things first, where do we add that additional check? Let's have a look at our shared class library. Maybe this is a good place. We previously defined CanAddImage here. We could extend or copy this and add an additional scope check, but I prefer not to add that code to our shared class library. After all, the scope check we want to add is something that only makes sense at level of the API. An API scope is only in the access token and not in the identity token. Therefore, putting that policy here in the shared class library doesn't make sense to me, unless of course you're going to share policies across different physical APIs, but that is not the case in our example. So let's switch to the API project and we find the policy over there. Instead of adding it to a class of its own, I'm going to add it in a quick and easy way which we haven't covered yet directly when configuring the authorization middleware in the Program class. So we're looking at where we defined our previous policy. Here, we now again call into AddPolicy, we give it a name, ClientApplicationCanWrite, for example, and we use a policyBuilder at this place to build our policy. We want to check for a claim value, and this time, the claim type we want to check for is the scope claim type, and the value we want this to be is imagegalleryapi.write, and that should be it. Now let's apply it. We already know how to do that. Let's open the images controller, and on the CreateImage action, we add an additional authorized attribute passing through ClientApplicationCanWrite as name for the policy. Now, this will not work yet, of course, we haven't defined these scopes yet, so our application and our identity provider don't yet have a notion of imagegalleryapi.read and imagegalleryapi.write. Let's do that next, and this should feel familiar by now. We opened the Config class at level of our identity provider. To the ApiScope list, we add two new API scopes, imagegallery.read and imagegallery.write, and we link them to our imagegalleryapi resource. No need to change anything to the claims that are included. We still need them in our CanAddImage policy. We do want to change something where we defined the client. We're going to start with only allowing it to request read access, so imagegalleryapi.fullaccess is no longer allowed, and imagegalleryapi.read is allowed. Alright. That's it for the identity provider. Let's switch to the client application, and let's request imagegalleryapi.read as a scope. There we go. Let's give this a try. Let's log in. We see that imagegalleryapi.read is requested this time. We're logged in as Emma. Let's check out the access token. We see the expected role and country, so this user is allowed to post an image according to those two claims and our CanAddImage policy.
