Moreover, the audience, imagegalleryapi and scope are there as well, so our client application is allowed to read, but there is no imagegalleryapi.write scope here. That means that the client application is not allowed to write, so adding the image should fail. Let's give that a try. We choose to add an image again. Let's click Add image, and we get back a 403 Forbidden. So far, so good. That means that our policy is working. Let's stop, and now let's additionally give our client write access, so we, again, open the config file at level of the identity provider, and we add imagegalleryapi.write to the allowed scope list. We also request this additional scope, and let's give this another try. Both scopes are requested. So far, so good. Let's choose to add an image again, and let me again select the image from La Boqueria in Barcelona. If you're not familiar with that, it's a large market that's open every day in the center of Barcelona with fresh fish, fresh meat, lots of little places to eat, and as you can judge from the image, lots of different fruit juices to drink. If you're ever in Barcelona, don't miss it. Alright. Let's choose to add an image, and there we go. It again works as expected. Pretty cool, right Let's continue with learning how to create policies that require complex logic.

Extending Authorization Policies with Requirements and Handlers
In the demo, we set a few requirements that have to be met when adding an image. Country must be Belgium and role must be PayingUser, both requirements must be met. Sometimes we need more complex logic. Say that our company wants to launch the image gallery service in a new country, Spain. At launch, we want to increase our user base, so we decide that all users, and not just paying users, can add an image if they're from Spain. We don't want to create a new policy for that as that would require us to apply it to all applicable actions across our application, but we also can't just create these rules with the simple policy we already have, it requires customization not offered by default. We need Boolean operators, in this case. And let's think about our API. We're currently allowing users to access the get, put, and delete actions for images they don't own. That is not secure enough. The request shouldn't even be allowed in those actions. Could we solve that with a policy? It would mean our policy would need access to the repository to check if the user owns an image, and it would also need access to the route data. It needs to know the image id from the URI. We cannot achieve that with a simple policy, but luckily, the authorization policy system is very extensible. Let's have a look. A resource can be protected by one or more policies. We did that in the previous demo. Such a policy has a set of requirements, one or more, and all requirements must be met for the policy to evaluate to true. In the demo, we've used RequireClaim, RequireRole, and RequiredAuthenticatedUser, those are built in, but we can also create custom requirements. We do that by implementing the IAuthorizationRequirement interface, and then there's a concept we have used, but didn't know about yet, handlers. Each requirement has one or more handlers. A handler must derive from AuthorizationHandler of T where T is the type of requirement. If any of the handler for the requirement return success and none of them return an explicit fail result, the requirement is met. So in those handlers, that is where the more complex logic resides, like for example, calling into a repository to check if a user owns an image. So with these policies, we're not just replacing a few roles, we are actually building a full‑fledged authorization layer. Let's try that in the next demo.

Demo - Creating Custom Requirements and Handlers
In this demo, we will learn how we can use custom requirements and handlers to extend what we already know about authorization policies. We're looking at the Program class of our API project. We're going to add a new policy, MustOwnImage. We already want to start with adding the requirement for the user to be authenticated. So far, nothing new, but we'll also want to ensure that the logged in user owns the image. As learned, we can add a requirement for that and then a handler for that requirement. This is, again, a policy that only makes sense at the level of the API, so we will define it there. Let me add a new folder, Authorization. Now let's add a class to it. We'll name the class MustOwnImageRequirement. This class must implement IAuthorizationRequirement, that's defined in Microsoft.AspNetCore. Authorization, and let's implement it. We need to add a constructor. What this class can be used for is storing additional contextual information. For example, if we were to make a requirement stating that a user needs to be from a specific country, we could input the value for the country to match as a constructive parameter, but for our requirement, there is no need for any code here. We do need a handler for this requirement though, so let's add another class. We'll name it MustOwnImageHandler. This should derive from AuthorizationHandler of T where T is our requirement, MustOwnImageRequirement. Let's import Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authorization, so this resolves again, and let's implement this abstract class. There's one method we must implement, HandleRequirementAsync. We are going to write async code in here, so let's already add the async keyword. So far, so good. Now, what do we need? Well, we need the user's sub value, the ID. We need to know a part of the route, the ID of the image, and we need access to the repository to check if the user owns the image. We can get route values like the image ID by calling get route value on the HTTP context, but that means we need an HTTP context. In ASP.NET Core, you get to the HTTP context in any part of your code through an httpContextAccessor, so we inject one of those. We use it to get to the HTTP context and call GetRouteValue on it. We pass through the key, which is ID, in our case, to get the value of the passed‑in ID which identifies the image. We try and parse it to a GUID. If that fails, the requirement is not met, so we call context.Fail. That's the first part. Then the sub claim. We can get that from the User object. The User object is accessible through the authorization handler context. Other things are accessible through this as well, but the one that's used most often is definitely the User object. The fact that we have access to this user inside of the handler means that we also have access to all the claims for the user, so this allows for some really fine‑tuned attribute‑based logic. We try to look for the sub claim, and if it cannot be found, we call context.Fail because we cannot continue. So we've got this, but we need even more. We need to call into a repository to check whether the user owns an image. For that, we need to inject the repository, so let's do that. IGalleryRepository is defined in ImageGallery.API.Services, so let's import that namespace, and let's inject the repository and store it in the GalleryRepository field. Let's scroll down again. We can now call in to the repository's IsImageOwnerAsync method. This checks whether the user effectively owns the image. If that isn't true, we again call context.Fail, and otherwise, all our checks have checked out. So we call context.succeed passing through the requirement, and that's it for our handler. Let's save all of this and let's return to the Program class at the level of the API. We can now add this requirement when building the policy. For that, we call into AddRequirements, and we pass through our requirement, that's defined in ImageGallery.Api.Authorization. We must now also register MustOwnImageHandler and its dependencies on our container, so the container can create an instance when needed. We register it as an implementation of IAuthorizationHandler. We do need to import the Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authorization namespace for that, and we register it with a scoped lifetime. It consumes our repository which is scoped as well, so that's why I'm doing it like this. Using a smaller lifetime would result in an error. Our MustOwnImageHandler class depends on the repository, which is already registered, and it also dependent on the HttpContextAccessor which it uses to get to the HTTP context, so we need to ensure that that is available on our container as well by calling into AddHttpContextAccessor. That's it for setting all of this up. Let's open the ImagesController. We can now apply this policy, and not only to the GetImage action which it needs, of course, but also to the actions that are used to update images and delete images. We only want to allow the owner of an image to update or delete it. By doing so, we're effectively blocking access to the action so we never even enter it, so we have now solved that outstanding issue we had from the previous module, and we've essentially created an authorization layer. By doing this, we have a central location for our rules, and we're avoiding coding mistakes. We no longer have to keep all of this in mind when coding our actions because the authorization layer takes care of this. Let's give that a try. I'm going to try one of these actions. Let's log in as Emma. Let's click Allow, and let's try editing one of our images. By doing so, the GetImage action is called at level of the API, and apparently, that checks out, we are allowed to do that. Let's update the image title, that resulted in the put operation being executed and that also seems to work. Our policy checks out. Now let's try doing the same thing logged in as Emma, but with one of David's images. Let's click Edit again. I happen to have the ID of one of David's images laying around here, so let me paste that in, and once I click Enter, the get action to get one image will be executed, and that should result in a forbidden this time. And that is indeed the case, our policy works. There are other parts of the application where we still use role‑based authorization. but thanks to these few demos, you now know how to switch that out for attribute‑based access control and how to use authorization policies to do that.

Using Custom Attributes for Authorization
Defining custom policies and applying them with the Authorize attribute as we just did works just fine, but another approach exists. We can create a completely customized attribute that, in its own, validates the custom authorization policy. Some people prefer this approach as it tends to be less complex because less classes are required, and it can also be seen as a little bit easier to distribute. The interface that makes this possible is the IAuthorization requirement data interface. It does so by letting custom attributes specify requirements which automatically get combined with other existing authorization mechanisms. Let's rewrite our example from the previous demo using this approach.

Demo - Using Custom Attributes for Authorization
In this demo, we'll create a custom attribute which we will use for authorization. Let's add a new class to the Authorization folder at API level. We'll name it MustOwnImageAttribute. We want this to derive from the Authorize attribute as we are creating a new Authorize attribute that's defined in Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authorization, so let's import that namespace, and we also wanted to implement the IAuthorization requirement data interface. There we go. This interface required us to implement one method, GetRequirements. In this method, we return the requirements, and we've still got a requirement from the previous demo, it's our MustOwnImageRequirement, so let's return an array with that requirement as only requirement. As you know, you can add additional requirements here if needed. And that's it already as far as our custom attribute is concerned. Let's save this, and let's find a good place to apply it. Let's open the ImagesController. Say we're going to switch out one of the previously added Authorize attributes with MustOwnImages policy, the one we apply to the GetImage action, so we commented out, and we apply our new MustOwnImageAttribute. That should do it. Let's build and run. To test this, let's first log in as Emma. Let's put a breakpoint on that GetImage action at API level. There we go. Clicking the Edit title link should result in that action being executed. Let's give that a try. And that is indeed the case. That means that the requirement was met. Let's continue. There we go. What I'm going to do now is try and get one of David's images by entering the URL to the EditImage route for one of David's images directly in the browser just like we did in the previous demo. Let's give that a try, and there we go. This time we get a forbidden result thanks to the custom attribute we just created. And with that, it's time for the module summary.

Summary
We looked into RBAC versus ABAC and learned that in ABAC, attribute‑based access control, access rights are granted through policies. Such a policy combines a set of attributes, claims, together which allows for much more complex rules than RBAC. Scopes from the access token can be used as well for fine‑grained non‑user‑based access. If the built‑in requirements aren't sufficient, we can create additional requirements with their own handlers. ABAC is the preferred approach for most applications these days. We implemented this at the level of our web application and API. And with that, we've already come a long way, but we're not there yet. What should we do when a token expires? Should all tokens be self‑contained tokens like JWTs? And how can we easily test our API which required authentication without having to rely on a client app? Let's check all of that out and more in the next module.

Dealing with Token Expiration, Reference Tokens, Token Revocation, and Generating Tokens for Testing
Coming Up
Tokens don't last forever, or at least they shouldn't, but what do we do when a token has expired? And what if you want to be able to revoke tokens ad hoc? We will learn how to do that in this module. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. So in this module, we will look into token lifetimes. From that, we will learn how to work with refresh tokens to get a new access token when an access token has expired. Sometimes, the need might exist for admins to revoke tokens ad hoc, instead of waiting for an access token to expire. We will add that ability by learning how to work with reference tokens. Then, we will look into how we can generate tokens for testing our API without a client app or identity provider in between. And lastly, we will look into all that token validation logic we talked about quite a few times in the previous modules. Let's dive into token lifetimes, first.

Token Lifetimes and Expiration
If you left your browser open for a while after having signed in, you may have seen that after some time, the API can't be accessed anymore. That's because the access token has expired. Tokens have a lifetime, and after that, they aren't valid anymore. As we know, the identity token is used to sign into the client application eventually. It typically has a very short lifetime. The default set by Identity Server is 5 minutes. That's because that token is used once right after it's delivered to the client and has been validated, it is used to create the claims identity from. Let's have a look at what the specification has to say about this expiration time, that's the exp claim in the token. It's the expiration time on or after which the identity token must not be accepted for processing. And this is important, accepted for processing. Processing the identity token in an MVC app typically means using that token to create a claims identity. But after that, it's up to the client to implement its own policies on when access to the client application should expire. It can use the fixed expiration set on the identity token, but in reality, more often than not, such an application uses its own exploration policy. A typical example of why you'd want to do all that is sliding exploration. As long as the user is active in the web app, the user should remain logged in. Access tokens are different. These typically have a longer lifetime. The default in Identity Server is 1 hour, and after they expire, we need a new one to be able to continue to access the API. That means that we can be signed into the client application, but not able to access the API anymore because the access token has expired. It is not the client who controls this. it's our identity provider. As we're working with a confidential client, refresh tokens are the advised way to get a new access token. Let's look into how we can control the lifetime of these tokens with a demo.

Demo - Token Lifetimes and Expiration
In this demo, we will see how we can decide on expiration times for our tokens and what the consequences are. We are in the Config class level of our Identity Provider where we configured our client. There's three different lifetimes I want to look into. Let's start with the IdentityTokenLifetime, which is accessible via IdentityTokenLifetime. We can set this property to an integer which translates to the number of seconds the token is valid. The default is 5 minutes, so we're going to leave that as‑is. I'll put it in here in comments for reference. Closely related to that is the AuthorizationCodeLifetime. The authorization code is a type of token that is exchanged for one or more other tokens when the token endpoint is called. That's something that happens during the code flow. It also warrants a low lifetime as we don't want to allow using this code for longer than required. Just as for identity tokens, the default is 5 minutes. We also leave that as‑is. Lastly, the AccessTokenLifetime. We can set that via the AccessTokenLifetime property. The default here is 1 hour. Let's set that to a much lower value, say 2 minutes, that's 120 seconds. Let's save this, and let's give this a try to see what happens. Let's log in. We can access our API. So far, so good. Now, let's wait 2 minutes, and let's refresh this page. That triggers our API again. And that seems to work, which is odd because we just put the lifetime to 2 minutes, yet those 2 minutes have passed and we can still access the API. That is actually by design. The access token validation middleware at API level takes a 5‑minute clock's queue into account. It does this to handle small offsets in out‑of‑sync clock times between the server where your identity provider might live and the server where the API lives, so let's wait a few more minutes. And this time, we get our Access denied page. The API no longer wants to accept our token, and the client application can no longer access API resources. That brings us to the next part, gaining long‑lived access.

Gaining Long-Lived Access with Refresh Tokens
When a token has expired, normally a user will have to go through the login flow again to get a new one. The user is thus redirected via the front channel, and depending on how you configured identity server, we'll have to provide credentials again and/or go to the consent screen again. But that's something we'd like to avoid. It's not very user‑friendly to redirect to the identity provider or even to ask the user to input their credentials again when the IDP cookie has expired. With a confidential client like our web application, a refresh token avoids this. This allows us to get a new access token via the back channel without a user having to take any action and without redirection. A refresh token can be seen as a means of authentication, it's a credential to get new tokens. Now, how does this work? The flow is a pretty easy one. The client application sends a post request to the token endpoint at level of the identity provider. The client application must authenticate itself, so that means that the client id and client secret, or another means of authentication, should be passed in. This is typically passed through encoded via the authorization header. In the request body, the refresh token is passed as is grant‑type refresh_token. The identity provider will then validate that refresh token and respond with a new access token, and optionally, a new refresh token so we can refresh again later on. Potentially a new identity token is returned as well. To allow requesting such a token in the first place, we should allow the offline_access scope. By definition, this gives us access to our applications and resources, even when we are offline. If you're wondering why it is named offline_access, offline in this context means that the user is not logged into the identity provider anymore. As long as the user is logged into an identity provider, you can always get new tokens. It won'l be very user friendly, but you can get them by starting a new request. The user won't have to input anything, but when the user is not logged into the identity provider anymore, the user would be forced to provide credentials again. The refresh token avoids this as it is a credential in its own right. Let's give this a try with a demo.

Demo - Gaining Long-lived Access
In this demo, we will learn how to work with refresh tokens. With that level of the identity provider in the Config class, the offline access scope is a requirement for supporting refresh tokens. By setting AllowOfflineAccess to true, this scope will be supported by our client. The refresh token lifetime can also be set here. By default, refresh tokens have an absolute lifetime. In other words, the expiration time is fixed. The default is 30 days. To change this, we can use the AbsoluteRefreshTokenLifetime property, but for demo purposes, the default is okay, but refresh tokens don't have to have an absolute lifetime. Sliding expiration is possible as well. That means that once a new refresh token is requested, its lifetime will be renewed. It's renewed by the amount of time specified by the SlidingRefreshTokenLifetime property value, but it will never exceed the absolute refresh token lifetime. For our demo, we're again quite okay with the default which is absolute expiration. There's one more value of interest to look into, it's named UpdateAccessTokenClaimsOnRefresh. Imagine the case where one of the user's claims is changed, say the address. By default, the claims in the access token stay as‑is when refreshing them, so if a refresh token has a value of 30 days, in a worst case scenario, those changes won't be reflected in the access token for 30 days. By default, the claims in the access token stay as‑is when refreshing them, and that may not always be what you want. If you want to ensure that claim values are refetched or recalculated when a token is refreshed, you can set this property to true. That's it for the identity provider. Let's save this, and let's switch to the client. Here, we want to ensure that we request the offline access scope, so let's add it to the scopes list. There we go. That is all we have to do as far as this middleware configuration is concerned. The middleware itself will now get a refresh token from the token endpoint and save it so we can use it later on. Let's already check that out. Let's open our gallery controller and navigate to the part of the code where we log the identity information to the Output window. Here, let's additionally log the refresh token so we can see whether we effectively get one. So we get the refresh token and now we log it just like we did with the identity token and the access token. Let's give that a try. Let's log in. The offline access scope is requested. So far, so good. Let's click Allow. All is looking okay. Now let's have a look at the output, and we see that our client application indeed received a refresh token. Up next is using this token to get new tokens, and that used to be rather annoying. In a previous version of this course, I manually created the refresh request as you saw on the slides and replaced the old access and refresh tokens with the new ones that were returned in the response. Feel free to have a look if you're interested. The good news is that these days we don't have to do that anymore. Let me open the Program class. Remember that we added IdentityModel.AspNetCore in the previous module? We used one of the built‑in handlers to ensure that the access token would be passed as a bearer token on the requests through the API. Well, that one line of code, AddUserAccessTokenHandler, will also automatically refresh our access token when it's about to expire or when it has expired, so that means that we don't have to do anything anymore. As long as a refresh token is available, we will get long‑lived access out of the box with this handler. Just to be sure, let's wait a few minutes until the access token has expired and let's refresh this page, that should send the request again. We still seem to have access. So far, so good. Let's have a look at the output of Identity Server. We see that the refresh token request came in, and it was successfully validated. That is that handler at work, it automatically refreshed our tokens. Let's continue by looking into how we can gain even better control over lifetimes with reference tokens.

Working with Reference Tokens
Up until now, we've worked with self‑contained access tokens, JWTs. This has the advantage that everything that's needed to validate a token locally is available. Those tokens are thus validated at the level of the API without having to communicate with the identity provider on each call. For a lot of applications, that's okay, but there's also a disadvantage. It's not easy to directly control the lifetime of these tokens once they have been issued. A self‑contained access token with a lifetime of 20 minutes can be used to access the API for 20 minutes, and there's not a lot you can do about it. In some cases, you need more direct control over that. For example, when a token that offers access to sensitive data should immediately be revoked when a system has been compromised. That is where reference tokens come into play. Instead of containing all the information that's in a self‑contained token, these tokens are just identifiers. They are linked to a token that is stored at the level of the identity provider, so whenever we hit our API with such an access token in reference token format, the token is sent to the identity provider via the back channel, it is validated and the actual content is sent back to the API. That is called token introspection, and it does that through the token introspection endpoint at level of the identity provider, this offers a lot more control over the lifetime of those tokens as they only exist as long as they're in the data store of the identity provider. It also has a possible disadvantage, the communication from the API to the identity provider on each request. Let's learn how to support reference tokens in a demo.

Demo - Working with Reference Tokens
In this demo, we will learn how to work with reference tokens, instead of self‑contained tokens. We are, again, at the level of the identity provider where we configure the client. Here, we can choose the AccessTokenType. JWT is a self‑contained token, which is what we've been working with up until now, and the other value is reference which we want to start working with in this demo. Let's give that a try. Let's log in. We get back a 401 Unauthorized. We'll immediately get to why that's the case, but first, let's have a look at the output. We see that our access token is now indeed a reference token. It's no longer a self‑contained token which can be decoded and read. This is just a simple identifier. Alright, so why did we hit this 401 unauthorized? Well, the thing is that at the level of the API, we are still using the JWT bearer token middleware as you can see here. The problem is that this middleware does not support reference tokens, so token validation fails, thus resulting in a 401 Unauthorized. There's another piece of middleware available that does support reference tokens. To be able to support reference tokens, that middleware will have to call the introspection endpoint at level of our identity provider to validate the reference token and to get to the real content of the ground result or the result of the flow. To be able to do that, the introspection endpoint requires authentication, so we need to define a secret so our API can access the introspection endpoint. Let's navigate back to the Config class at level of the identity provider. Now let's scroll up a bit. We defined an API resource here which we've been using up until now. One of the properties we can set on it is the ApiSecrets property. We passed through a new secret, and for demo purposes, the secret is apisecret. So far, so good. Let's save this and let's switch to the API. As mentioned, the JWT bearer middleware does not support token introspection, and thus, reference tokens. We need another piece of middleware for that. Luckily, there's one available. Let's open the NuGet dialog, and we want to browse for IdentityModel.AspNetCore.OAuth2TokenIntrospection, that's the one we're looking for. Let's install it. There we go. Let's open the Program class again, and let me comment out AddJwtBearer and switch to something you add OAuth2 introspection coming from the package we just added. We do have to take over our settings where they make sense. The authority can simply be passed over. ClientId is the API resource name, which replaces the audience check we previously had. The client secret, and in this case our API is the client, is the secret we just inputted, apisecret. ClientId and ClientSecret imply that the API is a client to the introspection endpoint which it indeed is. We also set the name and role claim types again, but we no longer need to pass through a valid types array. That was there as protection against the JWT confusion attack, but we no longer have a JWT, or JSON Web Token. We have a reference or opaque token instead, and that is not susceptible to that attack. That should be it. Let's give this another try. Let's log in, and there we go, we got access again. Up next, we will learn how we can revoke these reference tokens.

Token Revocation
As we learned, when using reference tokens, we get great control over token lifetime. Revoking these tokens can happen in multiple ways. Probably the most common way of doing this is through some sort of administration tool where the administrator can simply delete a token from the data store. Writing an admin tool to revoke these tokens is out of scope for this course, but it is as simple as deleting one line from the store where you persist the tokens or grants at level of IdentityServer. In our case, that's currently an in‑memory store. Next to that, we can also programmatically revoke tokens from a client application. A client application might decide to do this when a user logs out. At that moment, the previously‑obtained access token or refresh token is no longer needed. To this avail, the token revocation endpoint exists. Calling this from a client application allows the identity provider to clean up the tokens from its store. Let's have a look at how we can do that with a demo.

Demo - Revoking Tokens
In this demo, we will learn how to revoke reference tokens. For that, we will have to interact with our identity provider from wherever we want to revoke the tokens from. In our case, that will be the web client, so we need an HTTP client to interact with the IDP. Let's configure one, we'll name it IDPClient, and it's pretty straightforward. The only thing we have to input is the base address which we set to the root of our identity provider. That's all there is to it. We should be good to go. Let's save this, a good place to revoke tokens is when logging out, so let's open the logout action at level of our authentication controller. To revoke a token, we need to create a request to the identity provider, so let's first inject an HTTP client factory. So we can get an instance of our newly‑configured IDP client. So we use constructor injection for that. So what is the first thing we have to do? Well, first of all, we need to create a client using our HTTP client factory. For that, we call into CreateClient on the HTTP client factory passing through the name of our configured client, IDP client. The first thing we need to do is load the discovery document. That's the metadata document that contains the locations of the endpoints, and we'll need to use the revocation endpoint from that document to send a revocation request to. What we can do is use the IdentityModel helper classes for that. IdentityModel is a helper library that contains a set of extension methods, a parser, helper classes, and so on to easily integrate with an OAuth2 or OpenId Connect identity provider. It was automatically added when we added the IdentityModel.AspNetCore package to our project. One of these extension methods on HTTP client is GetDiscoveryDocumentAsync. We do need to import the IdentityModel.Client namespace to make this resolve. So we get the metadata by calling the discovery endpoint with the GetDiscoveryDocumentAsync extension method on the client. If there was a problem getting it, we throw an exception. To revoke a token, we call the RevokeTokenAsync extension method on HTTP client. What that expects is a TokenRevocationRequest. We can also use the new language feature here. On such a token revocation request, we set the endpoint address, and that endpoint is coming from the discovery document response. So you don't hardcode this, you read it out. The revocation endpoint requires client authentication, so we do need to pass through the ClientId and ClientSecret. Lastly, we set the token to revoke, that's our access token. We already know how to get the access token. It's a call to GetTokenAsync on HttpContext, passing through the type of token we want to get, that will read out token from the cookie. Let's import the necessary namespace so this resolves, and that should do it. If revocation fails, we throw an exception, and while we're at it, there's another token we might want to revoke, the refresh token. So let's send a likewise request, this time for revoking that refresh token. Just to test if this works, I will comment out the actual logging out for now. Like that, it's only the token revocation that will be done. We won't have a valid access token anymore, and we won't have a valid refresh token anymore, so access should be blocked. Let's give this a try. Let me log in. There we go. Now let's click Logout, and we don't need an exception or anything, so from that, we can conclude that revocation worked. Just to be sure, let's have a look at the output window. This is the output from the identity provider. We see a revocation request coming in here, that's the first one, and if we scroll down a bit, we see our second revocation request coming in, so two tokens have been revoked. Let's go back to our client application, and let's try navigating home again. Remember, we are still logged into the web client because we didn't clear the cookie. We did not call into sign out async, but due to the fact that the tokens have now been revoked, we shouldn't be able to access the API anymore. And that is indeed the case. And with that, we now know how to revoke tokens. Let me just uncomment the actual locking out so this works again. We've talked about validation a few times. Time to have a look at how this actually happens.

Generating Tokens for API Testing
Let's talk about generating tokens for API testing. We've been working with a local identity provider during this course, so we have full control. Also, we have a web client application to start requests that eventually result in an access token, so we have a way to acquire an access token. However, in real life, you will often need to integrate with an existing IDP which may or may not be under your control. Also, you typically want to test your API without having to rely on a web client application to acquire an access token. All of this has a tendency to become a bit cumbersome or even difficult to work with during development. You still want to test your API authentication layer, but you may not want to or be allowed to integrate with those external identity providers or services from your local machine. Note that I am specifically talking about testing the API. That is typically the most important part of your application as it is where your data resides. Anyway, generating a token yourself can come in handy in such a case. You can use it for automated API testing, for example, and there's a few approaches you can take here. A first possible approach is to write a little piece of code to create a token yourself, for example, as a custom endpoint. You'll see this approach being used online quite often. ASP.NET Core includes built‑in classes for token generation. I've actually detailed this approach in a clip in my ASP.NET Core Web API Fundamentals course. If you're interested in that, you can find it in the demo, Creating a Token Clip in the module, in module, Securing Your API. Be wary though, before you know it, you are writing those custom login and token endpoints we talked about in the very first module and that is to be avoided. Also, be aware of the fact that these tokens are not OAuth2 or OpenId Connect tokens. For that, you need to follow the standards as we've been doing throughout this course. These are custom tokens that typically don't offer the same level of protection as through OAuth and OpenId Connect tokens. Yet, during development, these tokens tend to come in handy for API testing. There is a much better approach than writing the code for this yourself. Microsoft has provided the user‑jwts tool. This allows you to create a token on the fly which can be used during testing. We're looking at Microsoft's documentation for this. It states that the .NET user‑jwts command‑line tool can create and manage app‑specific local JSON Web Tokens. There's a set of commands like create, remove, list, and so on, and options for each of these commands that we can use. Be aware of the fact that just as with the custom endpoint approach, these tokens too are not OAuth2 or OpenId Connect tokens, but they are very useful for API testing, so let's check that out with a demo.

Demo - Generating a Token with dotnet user-jwts
In this demo, we will learn how to generate a token when developing an API. I've opened the command prompt in the project directory. Let's execute the dotnet user‑jwts create ‑‑help command. This provides us with a set of options for generating tokens much like what we saw in Microsoft's documentation. We can add an audience value, that sounds familiar. We can also add custom claims like our name, we can manipulate the issuer, and so on. In other words, this opens up some scenarios. We can create different tokens for testing, one that allows full access, one that only allows access to certain resources, one that will result in authorization policies being fulfilled or not and so on. Let's play with this a bit. I'll start by simply creating a default token without setting any options. For that, we execute a .NET user‑jwts command followed by create, and there's our token already. Let's inspect this on jwt.io. So I'm going to copy it and let's switch to jwt.io and paste it in. Here's our token payload. So we've got a token here, a unique name is kevin, sub is Kevin as well, and we see a few audience values. We also see that the issuer is set to dotnet‑user‑jwts. This means that this token should not give us access. Notably, because the issuer and audience are not valid for our API, we expect the issuer to be our identity provider and the audience to be set to Image Gallery API. Another reason why this should fail is because the signature won't be valid. See, user‑jwts does not have access to the sign‑in key used by IdentityServer, which is the issuer we are relying on to sign the token. That key is in that key directory we talked about in the beginning of the course, and it is currently generated ad hoc when starting IdentityServer. But with that in the back of our heads, let's give this a try anyway. But first things first, this approach does not work with reference tokens. As we know, reference tokens are linked to the result of a token request that's stored at level of the identity provider, so without an identity provider, reference tokens cannot be possible. So for this demo, let's switch back to self‑contained tokens. So let me uncomment this and comment out the AddOauth2Introspection part. And let's launch our API. We're now currently only testing the API, so launching that is sufficient. You don't need to launch all the other projects. Anyway, our API is up and running in the background. Now, we need something to send requests to the API. There's lots of options for this from Fiddler over built‑in tools in Visual Studio to Swagger documentation. I'm going to use Postman which is a tool for sending requests and inspecting responses. If you're interested, you can get it via the link on screen, but feel free to use whatever you want. As long as you can send the request with a bearer token and inspect the response, you're good to go. I've got Postman open here. I'm going to send the GET request to get the images from our API, and let's not forget to set the token as bearer token for these requests, so here we go. And let's send this request, and we get back a 401 unauthorized. That is as expected, no access, but let me try something. I'm going to get rid of all the options we manually set here, and instead, I'm simply going to add a call in to AddJwtBearer. So that is all that's left. Let's launch this again, and let's send this request with a generated token again, and we get back a 200 OK. We have access. We don't see any images, but we do have access. So whats happening here? Well, first of all, don't worry, this does not mean that any token will now just be accepted. What actually happens is some of that magic that some people love and others don't love so much. You see, when you don't explicitly pass through configuration values like the expected authority and audience, which is what we have done up until now, ASP.NET Core goes and has a look at the configuration files, and we've got an appsettings.development file here. If we open that, we see that somehow we now have an authentication tag followed by schemes followed by bearer followed by a set of audiences. These valid audiences match exactly what's in the token and have a look at the issuer claim here down below dotnet‑user‑jwts, that's exactly what is set as a valid issuer. So this is what gets picked up by ASP.NET Core, but what about that sign‑in key then? Even though we may have a valid audience and a valid issuer in our token, checking the signature is also part of the validation logic for an access token, so how does that check out then? Well, that sign‑in key is a secret that is stored locally on your machine. In the documentation, you can find the location of it, and this is read out by ASP.NET Core and used for signature validation, and that is why we have access again. It's also why this is a safe approach for local testing. The key is stored locally on your machine. Okay. So far so good. But we did have to change our code to make this work. So how do we get this to work locally with locally‑generated tokens and when deployed with the real identity provider. Well, two common approaches exist. One thing you can do is check the environment you're in when configuring services. So here in the Program class, you can simply call into AddJwtBearer when you're in a development environment and use the old code otherwise. Another option is to fully switch to configuration files. In the development appsettings file, an authentication part now exists which you can also add to the generic appssettings file. Seeing we only have an appsettings.development file next to the appssettings.json file, whatever is in here will be picked up when we're not in a development environment. So you could copy over the authentication part from the development appsettings file to the generic appsettings file, and you can change the valid audience to match ImageGallery API and valid issuer to match our identity provider. Choose whichever option you prefer. Anyway, we now have access, but we don't see anything yet. No images are being returned. That is, of course, because of the claims in the token. The sub claim doesn't match Emma's or David's user identifier. We can solve that. Let's have a look at the options we have for creating tokens again. We see that we can pass through the name of the user with ‑n. This is what will end up in the token as a sub value, so let's do that. I'm going to create a new token, and I pass through Emma's sub value with a ‑n option. Here's that token. Let's copy it to the clipboard, and let's use it when sending the request to get the images. And here we go, we again have access to Emma's images, and like that, you can keep on manipulating your tokens. To add a specific claim you need, use the ‑‑claim option. To manipulate the issuer, use the ‑‑issuer option, and to add scopes, use the ‑‑scope option and so on. Like that, you can create tokens for different testing scenarios. So what if you've generated all these tokens, but didn't note them down? Well, with user‑jwts, you can do more than just generate tokens. It also acts as a local token store and a way to manage local tokens. You can use the list command to get a list of local tokens for the project. I've got quite a few here, and you can then print out those tokens with dotnet user‑jwts print, passing through the identifier with which the token was saved. You can find that in the first column here, and there we go. This is the most recent token I created. To remove a token, use the remove option and to remove all of them, use the clear option. So there you go. This is how to use user‑jwts to make your life as a developer easier before I forget, let's switch back to what we had before. We still want to keep on working with reference tokens for the rest of the demos. We talked about validation a few times. Time to look into how this actually happens and where.

Token Validation
In this part of the module, we will cover the most important types of validation. This is not something you typically do yourself. The different types of middleware take care of that like our OpenID Connect middleware at web application level and the JWT bearer middleware at API level. So the next few minutes are pretty theoretical, but it might be interesting to understand how the protocol keeps all of this safe. Important to know is that validation procedures are different when using different flows, and they can include additional steps if, for example, additional encryption is used outside of TLS. Next to that, some parts of it are optional and are not implemented by all identity providers or clients, and the other way around is possible as well. Identity providers and clients can decide on additional validation steps, so not every IDP or client will use the same procedures. We will focus on what is required for the authorization code flow in the way that we set it up and have used it throughout the course. We'll start with validation of the identity token at level of the client, and then we'll cover how the access token is validated when it reaches the API. First up, the identity token. As our identity provider signs these tokens, the signature must be validated. The nonce claim must also be checked. This must match the value sent by the client application on the authentication request. It can be used to mitigate replay attacks. The issuer claim in the token must match the issuer identifier of the identity provider. This helps in ensuring that the token wasn't issued by another identity provider. The audience claim must match the client id value, image gallery client in our case. Like this, we tested the identity token was meant to be used by our application and then we have expiration. The current time must be before the time represented by the exp claim, otherwise we're dealing with an expired token and those should not be used. When we looked into the contents of an identity token, we learned that it also contained an at hash value, that's an access token hash generated by the identity provider. At client level, a hash is created from the ASCII representation of the access token. How this happens depends on the hash algorithm used. Then we take the leftmost half of the hash Base64 URL encode it. The resulting value must then match the at_hash value in the identity token. What this does is link the access token to the verifiable identity token. Remember, identity tokens are verified at level of the client, and the access token isn't. These are the must‑do steps. As mentioned, additional steps are possible. We could check the issued at claim to reject tokens that were issued longer ago than we want to accept them, even though they're not expired. Other validation steps can include manual encryption and decryption, checking off ACR values or out time values, and dealing with multiple audiences. It all depends on your situation and requirements. The ones we just learned about already go a long way. The access token is sent to the API where it is validated. An interesting fact is that the OAuth2 standard does not specify how this should happen. In fact, what you're reading now is an exact quote from that standard. The methods used by the resource server to validate the access token are beyond the scope of the specification, but they generally involve an interaction or coordination between the resource server and the authorization server, so that's not very specific is it? One way of validation is calling the introspection endpoint with, for example, a reference token, but as we learned, we don't always want to do that so how is validation of a self‑contained access token handled at level of the API without communicating with the identity provider? First of all, as our identity provider signs an access token, the signature must be validated. Like that, we can be sure that the contents of the token hasn't been tampered with. The issuer claim in the token must match the issuer identifier of our IDP. This helps in ensuring that the token wasn't issued by another IDP. Now, we have expiration, the exp claim. The current time must be before the time represented by that claim. Otherwise, we're dealing with an expired token, and those should not be used. The audience claim must contain a value that gives access to the API, this can be a URI like we've seen for accessing resources at the level of the IDP which is used when calling the UserInfo endpoint. It can also be a scope value, Image Gallery API, for example, which is in the audience list and gives us basic access to the API. Otherwise, this token should not be used to access the API because it wasn't meant for the API, and that's it for validation. Let's have a look at the module summary.

Summary
In this module, we learned about token lifetimes and expiration. An access token should only be used for a specific amount of time. You don't want to allow infinite endless access, but when it expires, we need to get a new one. A good way of doing that is fire a refresh token. It can be seen as a means of authentication. It's a credential to get new tokens. A confidential client like our application can use this token combined with client authentication to call the token endpoint and receive a new access token, so it can access resources again. Then we learned about reference tokens. Instead of containing all the information that's in a self‑contained token, like JWT, these tokens aren't just identifiers. They are linked to contents stored at the level of the identity provider. This offers a lot more control over the lifetime of those tokens as they only exist as long as they're in the data store of the identity provider. We learned how to use the introspection endpoint for this in the demo. A possible disadvantage is the communication from the API to the identity provider on each request. Tightly related to this is token revocation, in other words, how do we revoke tokens so they cannot be used again. The most common way of doing this is through some sort of administration tool where the administrator can simply delete a token or grant from the data store, but we can also programmatically revoke tokens from a client app, for example, when a user logs out. In the demo, we learned how to use the revocation endpoint for that. Sometimes you simply need to generate a token to be able to test your API without integrating with an identity provider and without a web client application. In those cases, dotnet user‑jwts can come in handy. Lastly, we looked into the various validation procedures used in our flow, so we learned a lot about working with OAuth and OpenID Connect, but we haven't touched on JavaScript clients yet, even though that is a common use case. You will notice that the vast majority of code to secure something like that is written at level of the server like in an ASP.NET Core host. Let's have a look.

Best Practices for Securing JavaScript-based Clients
Coming Up
In this module, you will learn all about securing your JavaScript‑based applications like Angular, Vue, or React SBAs. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. We'll start with a bit of history. We will learn how JavaScript‑based clients used to be secured, why that didn't turn out to be the best idea, and what the current best practice is. And then we will dive into that current best practice, the back end for front‑end approach. We will learn different approaches to implement it. After that, it's time for a bunch of demos. We will implement a BFF approach to secure our host, we will learn how to log in and log out, how to get information about the user to your JavaScript client, how to securely call the API, and how to deal with token expiration. Note that if you're not working with JavaScript clients, you can easily skip this module. The demos for this are standalone, so they won't interfere with whatever comes next. Let's dive in.

From Past to Current Best Practice
You may wonder what a module on JavaScript‑based clients is doing in a course on securing ASP.NET Core. Well, you'll notice quickly that most of the security‑related responsibilities should actually be at the level of the hosting application, in other words of a potential ASP.NET Core host. Let's have a look at how these applications used to be secured. From a security point of view, JavaScript and other client‑side code is a challenge. The main issue is that the code doesn't live on the server like it does with an ASP.NET Core app, but it lives and is executed on the client, that is in the user's web browser. You can secure and trust a server under your control, but it is exceptionally harder to do that with clients not under your control, like a browser on a user's laptop or mobile phone. That is why I often say that you cannot log in to a JavaScript client. When we require users to log into our application, we do that to ensure that we can block the execution of a piece of code like an action on a controller, but that is not possible with JavaScript. The code is already on the client, it cannot be blocked anymore. That didn't used to be much of an issue. When I first started developing web apps, JavaScript was something you'd sometimes use for very small tasks, maybe to put a text box in red after some client‑side validation failed or to show a pop‑up or something, nothing really critical. As far as security was concerned, everything was handled by the host. If the JavaScript was hosted on a page that needed to be secured, the host would block the JavaScript from being served until the user could prove their identity. Often, this meant that the host would ask the user's username and password and write the cookie if it checked out. If the user then navigated to a page on the same domain that was secured, the browser would automatically send the cookie together with a request to the server where it would be validated. If validation checked out, the page was served including the JavaScript living on that page. Life used to be so simple, but that is not how we use JavaScript today anymore. We're now writing full‑blown single‑page apps with Angular, Vue, or React. These are often hosted on a simple HTML page without a server‑side framework like ASP.NET Core underneath it, and they talk to an API. They obviously need to be able to safely access that API, so the APIs require access tokens, and whoever talks to them needs to provide an access token, exactly as is the case with a server‑side client like our ASP.NET Core Image Gallery web client. So that sounds simple enough, right? We can simply start a flow from our JavaScript client app, instead of from our ASP.NET Core server‑side web app. It will result in tokens we can store somewhere in the browser and then pass to the API when we request data from it. Simple, except there's a few issues with this. For one, and it's immediately quite a big issue, JavaScript clients cannot securely store secrets. Whatever is in your JavaScript code is readable by anyone with access to a user's machine. This means that OAuth and OpenID Connect flows with client authentication don't make sense, and that's potentially an issue because as you remember, a call to the token endpoint as implemented by us requires client authentication. A client secret or other means of authentication need to be provided, but if you can't safely store that secret, then from a security point of view, it's useless to provide it. So that's one issue, but let's ignore that for a while because it gets worse. The next step is storing the tokens we get, but where will we store them? If we could, we would store them on the server or in an encrypted cookie that can only be decrypted by the server. That's effectively what happens in our current ASP.NET Core MVC approach and that is safe, but we are on the client now, and we've got a few options. We can store the tokens in memory as a JavaScript variable. We could also store it in the browser's session storage or local storage, or we could store it in a non‑HTTP‑only cookie that can be read by JavaScript. HTTP‑only cookies cannot be read by JavaScript, but there's a pretty big issue with all of these approaches. JavaScript is vulnerable to cross‑site scripting attacks which allow a malicious person to potentially read out your non‑HTTP‑only cookies, your local or session storage, your in‑memory JavaScript variables, and so on by injecting executable code. None of the approaches from storing tokens is protected from this. I know that you will find a lot of sites online that will tell you that storing the tokens in a cookie is okay, storing them in memory in a variable is safer than storing them in session storage and so on, and you can effectively argue about that, but in the end, all of these approaches remain vulnerable. Personally, I prefer not to see tokens anywhere where they can be accessed by JavaScript. The problem becomes even more precarious when you take token refresh into account. As you know, a refresh token is a credential in its own right. With it, you can get new tokens. Storing access tokens where they can be intercepted isn't great, but you can diminish the risk by giving them a short lifetime. Refresh tokens, however, by definition, have a relatively long lifetime, so that's a pretty big window of opportunity for someone with malicious intentions. Now, here are two approaches to mitigate or diminish the risk exist like one‑time use refresh or send the constrained tokens. Regarding this, two interesting RFCs exist, the OAuth 2 best practice RFC, I already mentioned before in this course, and the OAuth 2.1 RFC. OAuth 2.1 is essentially an effort to improve OAuth 2 by getting rid of all the bad parts and adding in additional protective measures. If you find the time to read through this, you'll notice that a lot of what is now considered bad was in the standard to enable client‑side flows from JavaScript. Nevertheless, even with these protective measures, the fact remains that none of these offer the same level of security as the server‑side approach we've been working with during this course. Code running on the client is and remains more vulnerable than code running on the server. That goes for JavaScript apps, but also for Blazor WASM apps, for example, so that is main reason number one, the level of security is inherently lower, and that is due to the nature of client‑side technologies. The second main reason is that browsers in which that client‑side JavaScript code executes are restricting specific usage scenarios of cookies mostly to avoid tracking techniques and to improve privacy. That's a good thing, but the thing is also that, that means that a bunch of techniques that used to be employed to enable OAuth and OIDC functionality in JavaScript don't work anymore because of that. Examples are token refresh via a silent renew approach and front‑channel single‑sign‑out. The solution to all of this, use the server, enter the back end for front‑end pattern. Let's learn more about that.

Detailing the Backend-for-Frontend Pattern
What you see on screen right now is how a typical interaction between a JavaScript client, an identity provider, and an API could look. The JavaScript client is hosted on an index.html page. No specific server‑side framework like ASP.NET Core is used by that host. The interaction to get tokens is between JavaScript and the identity provider. This can be one token or multiple tokens like an identity, access, and refresh token. Tokens are stored in the browser, and when needed, passed as better tokens from the JavaScript client to the API to gain access to it. This is the approach that has the previously described issues, and we want to get rid of it. The Backend For Frontend pattern allows for that. Essentially, you can look at it as a layer that sits between a client and a back end. It's pretty common when you, for example, need to develop a web application and a mobile client that both need data from the same underlying back‑end API, but maybe they want their data to look a little bit different. Both clients may want to get different fields or transform the data differently to better suit their UI. In that case, the web application could have a BFF layer that talks to the back‑end API and transforms the data from it in a way that fits the web UI, while the mobile app could have its own BFF layer that does the same, but transforms the data differently, so it better suits the mobile UI, but that's just one thing such a Backend for Frontend layer can do. We can also use that layer to handle security and session management for the front end for us. One approach is to host the JavaScript application in the same project that contains the API. This is called the local API approach. There is no code to interact with the identity provider from the JavaScript application. Instead, the flow is started from the server. We can use the same OpenID Connect middleware we viewed before for this. An identity token is requested and that eventually results in a cookie. It's that cookie that protects the project that includes the host for the JavaScript page and it also protects the API. Calls to the API go from JavaScript to the back end, which is the host for the API, and the browser makes sure that the cookie is automatically sent together with that request. No access tokens are needed in this approach as the calls to the local API are secured by the cookie itself. Another approach is the one where the API is a separate application, potentially deployed on another domain. Here, we host the JavaScript application on a separate host, say an ASP.NET Core MVC host. The flow is, again, triggered from the server just like we're used to. The identity token results in a cookie that protects the host. In this case, however, you will also request an access and refresh token. None of these tokens are ever sent to the JavaScript client, and the JavaScript client cannot access it. So that also means that the JavaScript client can never directly call the remote API. Calls to it are proxied via the host, which is responsible for adding the access token on each request to the API, and potentially, for refreshing the token when needed. So these are the two common approaches. You may have various reasons for choosing the one or the other or a combination of both, including ease of development and maintainability, improving reuse and/or improving scalability. Whether you want to go for the one or the other or a combination of both is a choice you will have to make depending on your architectural requirements. That is out of scope for this course. We have an API that's currently used by our MVC client. What we're going to do is add a new client to the mix, a JavaScript client. So we are going to go for the remote API approach. I'm going to use pure JavaScript for this. I don't want to tie this demo to a specific SPA framework like Angular, React, or Vue because, well, from a security point of view, that doesn't really matter in this case. Almost all of the responsibility and security‑related code is on the server, no matter which SPA you prefer. To implement the BFF pattern in the way we need it, we don't need anything. We can keep using the same middleware we've used up until now, and we can write our own helper classes to proxy calls to the remote API, add the bearer token or requests, and so on. That'll work, but using a helper package will save us a lot of time. A very common one to use is YARP by Microsoft. YARP stands for Yet Another Reverse Proxy. As the name implies, it will help with proxying our calls to the remote API. Another common one is Duende.BFF, this too provides all the functionality needed to proxy calls to a remote API, but on top of that, it also nicely integrates with other middleware for token and session management. It actually uses Microsoft's YARP internally. Seeing I've been using Duende IdentityServer in this course, I'm going to use Duende BFF in this module. It's included in the Community Edition of IdentityServer, however, feel free to use whatever you're comfortable with. Know that all of this is very much about principles and ideas and not that much about which component you want to use to implement these principles. Let's dive in with the first demo.

Demo - Logging In
I've opened the starter files for this module. The solution contains the API and IDP we've been working with up until now. Those are an exact copy of the finished code from the previous module. The ASP.NET Core MVC web client is gone. We will not use that in this module. Instead, I added another project, an ASP.NET Core MVC web client that acts as the host for a JavaScript application. I named it ImageGallery.BFF, that is our BFF host, and on top of it, the ImageGallery JavaScript client lives. As you can notice on top here, the JavaScript client doesn't really do much at this time, but it will soon enough. If you have a look at what the BFF project references, we see that it references the packages we have used throughout the course, the OIDC authentication middleware and IdentityModel.AspNetCore for token management. Let's make sure that our API, our BFF, and Marvin.IDP are selected as starter projects, and let's build and run this, and it works. Our host is launched and the JavaScript code is served, but nothing happens yet. We haven't implemented authentication yet, and we are not calling the API. Remember, we want to do that from our ASP.NET Core host, not from JavaScript. I've opened the Developer Tools, and we are looking at the console output, and as you can see, we get an error. This is because of that little piece of JavaScript code that tries to fetch something, but the thing it tries to fetch is currently named theimagesendpointwehavetocreate, and obviously that doesn't exist yet. We will fix this, but for this demo, lets start at level of our identity provider. I'm going to open the Config class, and as you can see, this is exactly what we ended up with in the previous module, and what I want to do now is add a new client definition, the one we are going to use from our BFF. Well, let me paste that in. As this is a server‑side web app, the same type of definition can be used as for our other web client. We want to use the code flow with PKCE protection. We want to allow the same scopes as before, and we add a secret for client authentication. This is, in fact, a copy of what we had up until now, including support for refresh tokens and reference tokens. The differences are another client ID, new redirect URIs that point to our BFF, and another secret. Let's save this, and let's switch to our host. We want to make sure that this is secured. I'm going to paste that in as well as this will feel very familiar, and it feels familiar because this is also as good as a copy of what we had for our MVC web client. We cleared the DefaultInboundClaimTypeMap to avoid the claim types being tampered with. We add an authentication cookie and register the OIDC middleware as challenge. One thing of importance here is that I changed the CookieScheme and ChallengeScheme names. These are values you can choose, but if you reuse the same names on the same domain, cookies you'd assume to be linked to different applications may interfere with each other, using unique names avoids that. Looking at the rest of our configuration, there's nothing new here. Anyway, I think you're already starting to see why a module like this belongs in an ASP.NET Core security course. Let's scroll down a bit and let's make sure we add the authentication middleware to the pipeline. So as we learned, right before our call into UseAuthorization, we call to UseAuthentication, and then we want to make sure that our home controller which serves up the home index page requires authorization. We do that by applying the Authorize attribute. Alright, let's give this a try. We are immediately redirected to our identity provider. That's looking good. Let's log in. This is all looking very familiar, and there we go, we see the logout button, meaning we are logged in. And as you noticed, we didn't have to change anything to our JavaScript application. All of this is handled at level of the server. So what's next? Well, it would be nice if we could show the user's name next to the Logout button, right? Let's see how we can achieve that.

Demo - Getting Acces to User Information
In this demo, we will learn how to access the user's claims. The important thing here is that we can't read that out from the cookie. It's an HTTP‑only cookie that's encrypted by the server out of the box, so we have no access to it from JavaScript. That's a good thing. What we can do, however, is call the server to get access to user information. Let's say that we want to get the user's given name to show on our logout button. Let's add an endpoint for that. Let's start with a new controller. I'm going to name it UserSessionController, and let's add an action to it, GivenName. This action lives at usersession/givenname following the conventions of an ASP.NET Core MVC default routing library. In it, we get the given name from the claim's identity and we return it as JSON, and let's also apply the Authorize attribute because we only want authorized requests to be able to access this endpoint, and thus, the user's claims. Alright, that's it for the controller. Then, let's add a bit of JavaScript to call the endpoint. The Logout button is set on the shared layout page, so let's open that, and we can find it here. So if we're dealing with an authenticated user, the Logout button is shown. Seeing we are calling the endpoint from a page that will only be served up when the user has already logged in, this means that we already have a valid authentication cookie. Without it, the page with this piece of JavaScript wouldn't have been served. Let's give the button an ID so we can easily refer to it, Logout button sounds like a good ID, and then let's scroll up a bit and let's add the script to the top of the page. The script is pretty straightforward. We call the givenname and point with fetch which is a sync and we'll wait. We parse the response as JSON, and then we add the given name from the response to the Logout buttons in our HTML. This call is secure. The browser will send the cookie together with the request to the endpoint, and it does that automatically. The endpoint we're calling requires an authenticated user, so what will happen is that ASP.NET Core will read out the authentication ticket from the cookie and validate it before allowing access to this endpoint. Let's give that a try. Let's log in, and let's continue. And there we go, we see the user's given name on top of the screen, yet were we to click this Logout button, not much would happen. We haven't written the code yet to log out. Let's do that next.

Demo - Logging Out
In this demo, we will learn how to log out. This too is a matter of calling an endpoint at level of the server. In fact, this is the exact same type of code as we executed when logging out of our ASP.NET Core web app as that is effectively what we're doing. We're going to clear the cookie of the host, including any remaining tokens. Let's add another action on our UserSessionController. I'll name it Logout. In it, we call into SignOutAsync on the HttpContext passing through the local cookie scheme to clear the local cookie and then the challenge scheme so the identity provider can clear its own cookie. This will effectively log us out, but as you remember from the previous module, it is also a good idea to revoke stored access and refresh tokens at level of the identity provider, so let's do that as well. First, let's open the Program class. I'll add an identity provider client here. This client is what we'll use to interact with our identity provider. We want to use it in the Logout action, so we need to be able to gain access to it. For that, we can inject an HttpClientFactory in our UserSessionController. Alright, once we have that, we can use it to create a client, so let's scroll down to the Logout action again, let's new up an IDP client. and the rest is a copy of what we did in the previous module. So, we first read out the discovery document by calling in to GetDiscoveryDocument on that new HTTP client instance. Then we send a request to revoke access tokens. Mind the fact that we are getting the location of the revocation endpoint from the discoverDocumentResponse, and that the ClientId and ClientSecret match our ImageGallery.BFF definition at level of the identity provider, and we do the same for the refresh token. There we go, so far for this method. All that's left is ensuring that this logout endpoint is executed when the logout button is pressed, so we again open the shared Layout file, and we ensure that we refer to the Logout action on the UserSession controller.
