Demo - Calling the Remote API
In this demo, we will learn how to call the API from the BFF. For that, we're going to use Duende.BFF, so let's open the NuGet Package Manager, and let's navigate to it. So we want to search for Duende.BFF and be careful here because what we want to install is Duende.BFF.Yarp. It has a dependency on Duende.BFF, but next to that it includes additional extensions used for remote API integration, and that is what we need. Let's install it, and there we go. Now, this is a new library to learn, and you can do a lot with it. We're looking at the documentation for it right now. It contains components for session and token management for proxying to remote APIs, it contains UI assets you can use, and if you look on the left here, you can find information about all of that. Now, it isn't my intention to run through everything this library has to offer. I'm going to focus on what we need right now, which is remote API access. Let's open the Program class. The first thing we want to do is add the required services for remote API access to our container. For that, we call into builder.Services.AddBff, and on that, we call into AddRemoteApis that makes sure that every service we need is registered. Then, we need to map the route. What we want is that a certain call into a local endpoint, so from JavaScript to its host is proxied through to the remote API. For that, we call into the MapRemoteBffApiEndpoint extension method coming from the Duende.BFF.Yarp package. The first parameter we pass through is the local endpoint we are defining. We can choose this, say bff/images, and the second parameter is the remote endpoint location. That points to the images endpoint at API level. Let's give this a try. Let's log in and let's manually navigate to the /bff/images endpoint. We get back a 401 unauthorized. What's interesting is that if we have a look at the Output window, we can see that the request was effectively forwarded to the remote API which resulted in that 401 response, and that makes sense. We didn't pass through a bearer token. So far, so good. Now, let's ensure that the access token is passed as a bearer token. For that we call into RequireAccessToken passing through User as the TokenType. TokenType here means that we need a user‑based token, one we get from triggering a flow to the authorization endpoint at level of our IDP and which requires a user to log in, and that is exactly what we have. We don't have to add anything else here, token management, and that includes refreshing a token in case it's needed is automatic with Duende.BFF. Let's give this another try. Let's log in, and let's navigate to that local endpoint again. And this time, we effectively get back Emma's images. So far, so good. All that's left now is calling this local endpoint from our JavaScript code. Let's open the next view for that. First, let's give this row div here an identifier, so we can easily refer to it. This will become the container for all our images, and then we can finally update that fetch statement we have on top of the page here. Let me paste in that code so we can run through it, so we fetch the local images endpoint which will proxy through to the remote images endpoint. We read out the response as JSON, and then we run through all the images in there. And for each of these, we create UI elements, an image tag, and surrounding this for positioning and styling, and that's it really. Let's give this a try. Let's log in, and there we go. Emma's images are loaded again. Time for the module summary.

Summary
In this module, we learned how to secure JavaScript‑based clients. The main takeaways are that over the years, a lot of effort has been put into getting client‑side code secure enough to work with a token‑based approach, but they were never really successful. Moreover, due to browser restrictions, approaches for dealing with silent read renewing tokens and single sign‑out from the front channel don't work anymore. Therefore, we need a new, better approach, and that is handling all the OpenID Connect and OAuth‑related flows at level of the server. This approach is also called the BFF, or backend‑for‑frontend approach. We implemented it with the Duende.BFF helper package. We learned how to log in, get user information, log out, call the API, and deal with token expiration. That last one is automatic when using Duende.BFF. We've only worked with in‑memory users up until now. Time to improve on that. In the next module, we will dive into users and credentials. Buckle up.

Storing Users and Credentials in a Local Database
Coming Up
In this module, we will start working with users and credentials. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. We will start by learning how users and credentials fit into OpenID Connect. Then, we will look at some common options we have to authenticate and store users, and along the way, clear up some confusion on what exactly ASP.NET Core Identity and the Microsoft Identity Framework are. Up next, we will create a local database to store our users. We'll inspect how IdentityServer provides extension points for integrating with the custom user database, and afterwards, we will do just that. Along the way, we will learn how to build our identity with an IProfileService. Let's dive in.

How Credentials Fit in OpenID Connect
One of the facts that always gets me some weird looks when I mention it is that OpenID Connect and OAuth by extension doesn't really deal with credentials. That may sound odd because it's what we use for authentication, right? Well, yes, but that does not mean it needs to describe how a user authenticates. Let's have a look at the standard for that. Important here is this part. The authorization server, that's the identity provider, or IDP, in our case, IdentityServer, attempts to authenticate the end user or determines whether the end user is authenticated depending upon the request parameter values used. The methods used by the authorization server to authenticate the end user are beyond the scope of this specification. In other words, the identity provider requires the user to authenticate or be authenticated before being able to provide proof of who the user is to a client app. That proof, which is our identity token after verification, can then be used by the client app to log into it as we know by now, but at the same time, OpenID Connect does not deal with how the end user authenticates at level of the IDP. For OpenID Connect, the way a user authenticates doesn't matter as long as it is done reliably. With that in mind, let's have a look at how a user can authenticate and how reliable all of that is.

Means of Authentication and Approaches
User authentication can be defined as the process or action of verifying the identity of a user, or in other words, verifying that the user is who they say they are, but how do we do that then? Well, various means of authenticating an end user exist. The most common one we all know is the good old username/password combination, but a user can also be authenticated by biometrics, for example, an iris scan or a fingerprint, that is something they are. They can also be authenticated by proving that they own something like having to plug in a USB key in the computer which contains a certificate belonging to the user or via a smartphone app that provides a new six‑digit code every few minutes. Inputting the code means the user owns the smartphone, and there's more exotic approaches like transaction authentication. For example, the system knows I'm from Belgium, but the IP my request is coming from is not based in Belgium. In that case, authentication can be denied as there's a discrepancy in the characteristics the system knows about me versus what is sent. Not all of these factors are good enough on their own. In modern solutions, we want to rely on multiple factors of authentication, for example, a username or password combined with proof that the user owns something. One of those typical six‑digit, one‑time passwords generated by Google authenticator for example. We will look into multi‑factor authentication in detail later on, but there's more. We also need to take into account where these credentials, which are the proof of who the user is, are stored. An identity provider can, of course, get them from a local store, a local database, and that often happens. And in enterprises, users often want to use their Windows credentials to prove who they are, that's integration with Active Directory. Another very common scenario these days is that people tend to already have accounts somewhere else. Credentials are just stored in a remote store. For example, I have a Microsoft account, a Google account, and a Facebook account. A lot of applications allow me to use those credentials to prove who I am, instead of requiring me to create yet another username and password. That means that my credentials are not stored at level of the local identity provider. They are stored at level of Microsoft, Google, or Facebook. Microsoft Entra ID, as Azure AD is called these days, is also an example of that, although, it tends to be regarded as less public and more aimed at in‑company authentication. We will look into all of these approaches. So we've got factors or means of authentication, and then we've got approaches to authentication, and all of these can be combined. That requires logic you don't want to make a client application responsible for. I think that by this time in the course, you're probably already convinced of the use of a central identity provider, but let's reiterate this and even add a few more reasons. User accounts, and the way users prove who they are, are rarely specific to one application, they are reused across applications. Also, means of authentication are sometimes changed, added, or improved upon, and the same goes for approaches to authentication. It's not uncommon to add a new third‑party provider to integrate with, for example, add authenticating through Microsoft after you've added support for Google accounts. You want to keep this in a central place to avoid having to change all your client applications all the time. In essence, as we know for authentication, all a client needs is proof of identity, which it gets via the identity token as defined by OpenID Connect, and user authentication does not belong in a client application, it belongs at level of the identity provider, so that is also where we will implement support for all of this. Now, what are our options for implementing all of this? Let's have a look.

Implementation Approaches: Custom, ASP.NET Core Identity and the Microsoft Identity Framework
The first option is manually implementing all of this from having a local database to storing credentials and claims over integrating with Active Directory, implementing MFA, and creating user management screens, this is the option I've personally been in contact with the most on the job as most companies I work for tend to already have some sort of identity‑related data store functionality, rules, and conditions in place that cannot just be thrown overboard. It's often difficult to nearly impossible to integrate this in off‑the‑shelf products. This approach offers the highest amount of flexibility, but it also tends to be the most time consuming and expensive option. Also, you need to be careful not to accidentally implement security laws. The second option is using ASP.NET Core Identity. You may have heard of it already. I've noticed that a lot of confusion still exists on what exactly it is, so let's have a look at Microsoft's documentation. Here, we can read that ASP.NET Core Identity is an application programming interface that supports user interface logging functionality. It can be used to manage users, passwords, profile data, roles, claims, email confirmations, and so on. So in essence, you can look at it as a bunch of screens for user management combined with the data store for storing users and credentials, and of course, the necessary classes and methods to integrate it in an application. All in all, it's a good starting point for anything user management‑related. Also interesting, the documentation mentions that ASP.NET Core Identity adds a UI to log into web apps, but you should still use something like Duende IdentityServer to secure an API or single‑page app. That doesn't mean you cannot use ASP.NET Core Identity on its own, but it doesn't support OAuth and OpenID Connect. It supports generating tokens which you can then use to create your own homegrown endpoints, but as we learned in the beginning of the course, that is exactly what we don't want to do, so very often, it's used together with something like IdentityServer. And then there's that other thing or concept that tends to pop up when you start Googling for ASP.NET Core security, the Microsoft Identity Framework. This is a different beast altogether. It's a set of helper libraries and documentation to help integrating your application with Microsoft Entra AD and Azure AD B2C. Entra AD, which is the new name for Azure AD, is an IDP that supports OpenID Connect just like IdentityServer. It's also considered to be a part of the Microsoft Identity Framework, so in that regard, the Microsoft Identity Framework is an umbrella term for all things related to identity and access management on Azure. It also includes user registration screens, management screens, and so on, but all on Azure. If you're interested in that, I've got a course that deals with Entra ID or Azure ID and building secure applications on Azure that may be of interest to you. Now, instead of having used Identity Server as we did, we could have directly integrated our application with Microsoft Entra ID with the help of some of the libraries from the Microsoft Identity Framework. So for us at this moment, the Microsoft Identity Platform isn't of interest. We will integrate with Microsoft Entra ID/Azure AD a bit later on in the course, but not from our client. We will do it at level of our identity provider to enable federation scenarios. So which option should you choose? Well, if you're thrown into an existing application or environment where user management, a user and credential data store, etc are already somehow in place, you'll probably end up with a custom approach. You will also end up with this if you require functionality that is not supported by ASP.NET Core Identity. But if you can start from scratch, I would suggest to investigate and consider ASP.NET Core Identity. If it fits your requirements, it will definitely give you a head start. So what will we do? Well, I am assuming that some of you will be in the first situation and others in the second, so we will cover both approaches. I'll mostly go for the custom approach though as that makes it much easier to understand what exactly is happening when implementing things like user store integration, federation, multi‑factor authentication, and so on. ASP.NET Core Identity tends to hide a lot of what is really going on beneath the covers. It sometimes feels a bit magical, but that also means that it is not ideal for teaching how all of this works. Yet, near the end of the course, we will integrate identity server with ASP.NET Core Identity as well. The first thing we'll need is a data store, so let's have a look at a good database schema for it.

Inspecting the User Database Schema
We'll start with a simple database, and we will build up on it during the course. To get started, we're okay with just two tables, a user table and a user claims table whom have a one‑to‑many relationship. In the user table, we will store fields that have to do with the local authentication process like the username, subject ID, and password. Other user‑related values like first name and last name can then be stored in the UserClaims table. There is no need for them to have separate columns in the user table which makes this approach flexible in case additional claims have to be added later on. Both tables also have a concurrency stamp field. This improves the trustworthiness of our data as they help with correctly handling concurrency issues. Take the following example, two admins are trying to update the same user, Kevin. Both have loaded Kevin's user table fields on screen. The first admin changes Kevin's username to Sven and saves. The second admin changes Kevin's username which now actually has Sven as value to Tom and saves. Without a concurrency stamp in place, username Sven will be overwritten with username Tom. That's not what we want. The first admin's changes are now gone. With a concurrency stamp in place, every change to the user table will result in a new concurrency stamp being generated. Whenever a user is saved, concurrency stamp in the database will be compared to the concurrency stamp that's been loaded when fetching the user. If they don't match, the update is not allowed. So with a concurrency stamp in place, the second admin would get an error message and that is what we want. Alright, this is already enough to get started. Keep in mind, this schema is just a starting point. We will revisit it quite a few times and improve on it, but for now, we're good to go. nto the demo.

Demo - Creating a User Database
I prepared a few starter classes to get us up and running. You can find them in the exercise folder for this module or in the repository for this course on my GitHub, The starter classes are user and UserClaim entities, a related DbContext, and a service that interacts with the DbContext. Let's copy them over to our Marvin.IDP project and have a look. The entities are pretty self‑explanatory. They exactly match what we looked into on the slides. The context exposes these entities as DbSets, but the context doesn't resolve yet. We still need to add a reference to Entity Framework Core, so let's right‑click the project and choose to manage NuGet packages. Let's look for Microsoft.EntityFrameworkCore.SQLite, and let's install it. We'll keep on using SQLite as a data store so everyone can keep coding along regardless of your operating system, but as we're using Entity Framework Core, it's trivial to switch this out for a data store of your liking, and let's also add a reference to Microsoft.EntityFrameworkCore.Tools, that'll make it easier to add migrations in a minute. Alright, that should do the trick. Let's open the DbContext again, and it indeed resolves now. So we've got two datasets. There's a one‑to‑many relationship between User and UserClaims setup, and I added some data to see to the database. This matches the two in‑memory users we've been working with up until now. In the SaveChangesAsync method, we ensure that the concurrency stamp is taken into account and that's already it. All in all, a pretty straightforward Entity Framework Core setup. Let's set this up in our HostingExtensions class. We want to call in to AddDbContext passing through the type of the DbContext that is named IdentityDbContext. Let me import the Marvin.IDP.DbContext namespace so it resolves. On the options, we state that we will use SQLite. For that to resolve, we need to import the Microsoft.EntityFrameworkCore namespace. We also passed through a connection string, MarvinIdentityDBConnectionString. We don't have that string defined yet, so let's open the appsettings file and do that. There we go, a SQLite connection string. Let's save this. Now, we can add a migration. For that, let's open the Package Manager console and execute the add‑migration command. We passed through InitialMarvinIdentity migration as the name for the migration, and let's make sure we have the Marvin.IDP project selected on top here. Let's execute this, and we hit an exception, even though it does look like the migration was correctly created. What's that all about? The exception we hit seems to be of type HostAbortedException. This is a potentially expected behavior that can happen when adding migrations, updating the database, and so on. The thing is though this exception should not result in an unhandled exception, instead it should be handled and ignored as is detailed by Microsoft's davidfowl over on GitHub. You're looking at that right now, so let's follow his lead and catch and eat this exception. Let's open the program file, and before catching all exceptions, let's catch anything of type HostAbortedException and eat it. Alright, that should do it. Let's save this, and just to check, let's remove the Migrations folder that was just created, and let's add that migration again. That's already looking a bit better. Now let's call update‑database to execute the migration. And there we go. If we have a look at Solution Explorer, we now see MarvinIdentity.db showing up. Our database has been created. To have a look at what is in this database, I like using the SQLite compact toolbox extension. If you don't have that yet, have a look at the Extensions menu. When you click Manage Extensions, you can look for extensions, and you'll immediately see it show up because it is the second most downloaded Visual Studio extension, so it is quite popular. I already have it installed, and let's have a look at what's in our database. Let's have a look at the users. Here's our two users, David and Emma, and let's have a look at the claims. Here are their claims. So far, so good. Let's learn how interaction with IdentityServer works.

Interacting with IdentityServer
Out of the box, IdentityServer does not have a user interface. As you remember, we added it in the beginning of the course. This is code we can either import from somewhere or we have to write ourselves, just like the code to integrate with a custom user database. What we're interested in is how exactly interaction from that user interface with IdentityServer's internals works, and that is what the interaction service is for, available as an implementation of the IIdentityServerInteractionService interface. It provides services to be used by the user interface to communicate with IdentityServer, mainly pertaining to user interaction. We can inject it via constructor injection into our ASP.NET Core web pages for the user interface of IdentityServer. In the next demo, we're going to inspect how this interaction service works in the UI we added in the beginning of the course. After that, we're going to change it a bit. We're going to switch out the in‑memory test users that are currently used and replace it with our own users from our newly‑created database. Important disclaimer, let's say. As mentioned before, this user interface sometimes changes, and I have no control over that. We're getting the latest version as created by Duende software and that varies over time. I will, of course, update the demo source code when needed, but with this in mind, it may be that what you've got on your machine differs a bit from what you see in the clips in which we're working with identity service user interface. That being said, the principles I'll cover remain the same, and if the change is significant, I will make sure that the clips are updated as well. Anyway, with that disclaimer out of the way, we're now ready for the first of many IdentityServer interaction demos. Let's dive in.

Demo - Inspecting UI Interaction with IdentityServer
In this demo, we'll check out how the current UI works and communicates with IdentityServer via the IdentityServerInteractionService. If we look at the Pages folder, we see that there's different pieces of functionality that have been implemented. There's views and there's models for dealing with consent, for dealing with the grants page, for dealing with the home page, and so on. What we're interested in is the login part as that is what we'll be working with, so let's open the Index page model from the Account/Login folder. In the constructor, an IIdentityService is injected. Let's have a look at some of the methods on this. What we see here are common pieces of functionality that's exposed to this service, pieces of functionality that you will probably need when creating a UI. For example, you can get the authorization context that contains a set of information about the authorization request, like the client, the redirect URI, additional parameters passed through together with the authorization request, and so on. Next to that, there's methods here to grant consent, deny authorization, or revoke consent. Most of the time, though, the GetAuthorizationContextAsync method and the isValidReturnUrl method are the ones that are used. So this already gives you a better idea of what this is used for. A scheme provider is injected as well, which will provide our page model with scheme information, like the default authentication scheme for this identity provider, which is named IDSRV. The identity provider store provides a way to interact with stored, additional IDP integrations, and the IEventService is used to raise events like a user login success event, so other parts of the code can subscribe to it if needed and act accordingly. And lastly, a TestUserStore is injected. As the code comments mention, this is where we're going to plug in our own custom identity management system that accesses our custom database. Let's collapse the methods to definitions so we can see what else is in there. Not that much really. We've got an OnGet method which is used to show the login view, an OnPost method to handle posting the form. The BuildModelAsync method is just a helper method to create the related model for the view. Let's have a look at the OnGet action first. First, a model is built to show this page. The return URL you see passed in here is not the redirection URL, so it doesn't have anything to do with calling back. This return URL is something you will see being passed through in most actions, and it is used to allow the flow to continue from one view at level of the identity provider to the next view at level of the identity provider. Let's have a look at that BuildModelAsync method. The first piece of interaction we see is the interaction service being used to get the current authorization context. We just inspected that, it contains information about a current authorization request. Then there's a shortcut in case we're using IdentityServer as a gateway to one external identity provider, so the user doesn't see the local login screen where they can enter a username or password at level of IdentityServer when just one external provider is configured. After that, the currently available authentication schemes are fetched. We know what this is. We've got cookies and OpenID Connect as schemes at level of our web app. If IdentityServer supports multiple authentication schemes, this is the code that's used to create providers from it, so it can show these providers on the login page. For each provider, a button will be shown like a Login with Azure AD button, a Login with Facebook button, and so on. That allows the user to choose how to log in. You will encounter this again. Up next, we need to know if the client that sent the authorization request is enabled. If that's not the case or if other restrictions apply as far as allowed schemes are concerned, the provider list is filtered. And lastly, the ViewModel is created and set. This is thus used when getting the Login page. Then let's have a look at the OnPost method. This is where the user's username and password combination is checked, but first, some border cases are handled. We're checking whether we're still in the context of an authorization request. This is to avoid users directly posting to this action without going through the rest of the flow. Another border case is handling the Cancel button. If that checks out and the ModelState is valid, the user's credentials are validated. This is currently using the in‑memory test user store. If the credentials are valid, the user is fetched, and eventually, a cookie is created. That's the authentication cookie. Issuing that is done by calling SignInAsync on the current HttpContext. In other words, from this moment on, we are signed into IdentityServer. If we're dealing with a native client, a loading page is returned which is better for the user experience. In our case, we're dealing with a web client, so we check if the return URL still checks out, and if it does, we are redirected to it. In this case, that would be a redirection to the authorization endpoint as we are still in the context of an authorization request. IdentityServer now knows how to continue and picks up the flow again. And this is how you write code to interact with IdentityServer. Each view has an OnGet and an OnPost. In OnGet, the data is created to be able to show you the page, and in OnPost, posting is handled. If IdentityServer‑related information is needed, like the information in the authorization request, information about the clients, checking whether we're still in a valid place in the flow, and so on, the interaction service is used. Let's learn how we can integrate this with our custom store.

Building Your Identity with a Profile Service
Often, IdentityServer requires identity information about users when creating tokens or when handling requests to the user info or introspection endpoints. That's the case in our case. We are expecting the claims to be coming from the UserInfo endpoint, but they might have well been delivered directly in the token. The issue would remain the same. No claims like given name would have been returned with the code we just wrote, but why is that the case? Well, by default, IdentityServer only has the claims in the authentication cookie to draw upon for this identity data. You're looking at the piece of code from the login page where this cookie is built. So say for the default claims provided by the identity provider, no user‑related claims are added. We could add these here to the cookie, but that would be impractical. Those claims don't need to be in that cookie. It will only make the cookie bigger than it should be. Luckily, IdentityServer defines an extensibility point for allowing claims to be dynamically loaded as needed for the user. This extensibility point is the IProfileService. A common use case is implementing this interface to access a custom database or an API that contains the identity data for users, and that sounds exactly like what we need. Let's fix this.

Demo - Building Your Identity with a Profile Service
Let's start by having a look at that AddTestUsers extension method we used to call when configuring IdentityServer. We are at IdentityServer's GitHub, and we are looking at the implementation of that extension method. Obviously, the test users are registered for injection, but more important for us is that we see that a TestUserProfileService is added as a profile service, that's an implementation of IProfileService. Thanks to this, IdentityServer knows how to access additional claims about these test users. We are no longer using these test users, so we're going to implement our own profile service. Let's add a new class to the Services folder. We'll name it LocalUserProfileService. As we learned, it has to implement the IProfileService interface, so let's do that. It's defined in Duende.IdentityServer.Services. Let's import that namespace, and let's implement the interface. There's just two methods we have to implement, GetProfileDataAsync and IsActiveAsync. If we look at the ProfileDataRequestContext passed in to GetProfileDataAsync, we see that it has an IssuedClaims property. For our use case, we want to set that IssuedClaims property to the user's claims. As you can see, the ProfileDataRequestContext contains additional properties that may be useful in a service that's used to load profile data. The first thing we need to do is get a hold of our user. By calling into context.Subject.GetSubjectId, which is defined in Duende.IdentityServer.Extensions, we can get the current user's subject id, and we can use that to call into our local user service, so let's inject that service first. For that, we use constructor injection, and we store it in a local read‑only field, and then we get the user's claims. For that. we await a call into GetUserClaimsBySubjectAsync. Seeing we are awaiting that call, we need to mark our GetProfileDataAsync method with an async modifier. And with that, we've got the user's claims. By calling into AddRequestedClaims, our claims are added to the issued claims collection. So far for that, then we just have to implement IsActiveAsync. We first get the subjectId again, and then we call into IsUserActive on our localUserService. Here too, we need to mark the method with the async modifier, and that's it for our profile service. Let's open the HostExtensions file and register it. To do that, we call into AddProfileService when setting up IdentityServer. The type we pass through is LocalUserProfileService. Summary
We started out with learning that OpenID Connect, in fact, doesn't really deal with users and credentials. It's up to us to safely implement this. For that, we can either go for a custom approach or use ASP.NET Core Identity. We started with a custom approach, but we will cover both. Out of the box, Identity Server does not have a user interface, nor does it have a notion of integrating with users. This is code we have to write ourselves or rather start from what is provided by the IdentityServer team, and that contains a UI, the interaction service available as an implementation of the IIdentityServerInteractionService interface provides services to be used by that user interface to communicate with Identity Server, mainly, pertaining to user interaction. We had a look at how that was implemented, and after that, we switched out the test user store for our own user service, which allowed us to log in with users from our database. We weren't there yet, though. We didn't get additional identity‑related claims back from our IDP, that's because IdentityServer only has access to the claims that were added to the sign‑in cookie. We could add additional claims, but that would just make the cookie bigger than it should be. IdentityServer defines the IProfileService for that. This is an extensibility point for allowing claims to be dynamically loaded as needed for the user. A common use case is implementing this interface to access a custom database that contains the identity data for users, and that's exactly what we did in the last demo. So, we've got a user database, but what about managing those users? Where should those screens go? Also, we really need to get rid of that clear text password. Let's do that and more in the next module.

Best Practices for User Management
Coming Up
We ended the previous module with a few users in the database, but those were a given. We don't know how to manage these yet. In this module, we'll fix that. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it, so it's time to deal with user management. We'll start with learning where the user management screens can and/or should live. After that, we will implement a full user registration flow. This allows us to learn how to add functionality to create and activate a user, including how to safely store a password. From that, we will learn what to look out for when implementing additional user management‑related tasks like password resets. Let's dive in.

Where Should User Management Screens Live?
In this module, we will implement identity self‑management screens to allow users to register themselves. Screens and functionality like this is pretty common in an Identity and Access Management system, but that begs the question, where should his functionality live? Well, you could create a separate web application strictly for user management or user self‑management. You could also integrate that level of the identity provider, or you could use a combination, say screens related to user registration could be implemented at the level of the IDP, but user self‑service functionality like resetting a password could go into an application of its own, and administration‑related tasks like allowing an admin to manage a user could go in yet another application. I've seen all kinds of approaches, and from a security point of view, which is what we are mostly interested in, all are valid. From that point of view alone, it is hard to say what the best or better approach is. The choice is more related to non‑security‑related concerns. Questions you can ask yourself to help you make this decision are questions like do you want to be able to scale up the identity provider without having to scale up a user admin application? If the answer is yes, that may lead to separating them out. Another question that leads to separating them is do you want to be able to deploy them separately? And what about development? Are you okay with managing different solutions in code bases, or do you want to avoid that? If you absolutely want to avoid that, separating them out might not be the best idea. Also is the risk that changing something at the level of the user management screens could potentially have an effect on your identity provider worth it and so on? I cannot make that decision for you, obviously, but I hope these questions and the answers you give to them will help you a bit already. In our case, we will add these screens at the level of the identity provider. Let's start implementing this.

Safely Storing Passwords
We're currently storing our passwords in clear text. That's bad, very bad actually. Passwords should be stored after being salted, hashed, and with key‑stretching in place, that's a lot of techniques in one sentence. Let's look into them. A salt is, in essence, a cryptographically random piece of data that is attached to the password before it is hashed. It, thus, serves as additional input for a hashing function, and it is stored next to the hash password in the database or attached to it. Often, a Cryptographically Secure Pseudorandom Number Generator, or CSPRNG, is used to generate this. This provides a high level of randomness and makes the salt completely unpredictable. It's thus much more secure than a GUID or a regular random number generator. Salting protects against dictionary attacks like a lookup table attack and a rainbow table attack, but before I can explain that, we first have to know what hashing is. Hashing means performing a one‑way transformation on a password which turns the password into another string called the hashed password. As it's a one‑way transformation, it's almost impossible to turn the hashed password back into the original one. Good hashing algorithms are SHA256 and SHA512. Important here is that hashing should not be confused with encryption. Encryption is a two‑way transformation. You can decrypt something back to its original value after having it encrypted. A hash cannot be dehashed, so what do these hashing and salting techniques protect against? Well, hashing ensures that the password cannot be decrypted because it's a one‑way transformation, and as I just mentioned, salting protects against lookup table and rainbow table attacks, but what are those attacks? A lookup table attack is used to try and crack a hash. The general idea is to pre‑compute the hashes of the passwords in a password dictionary and store them and their corresponding password in a lookup table data structure. A good implementation of a lookup table can process hundreds of hash lookups per second even when they contain many billions of hashes. A rainbow table is much like a lookup table, but here the lookup tables are made smaller by sacrificing hash cracking speed. Because of that, the solutions to more hashes can be stored in the same amount of space, making them more effective. Salting protects against both of these attacks. Imagine that your password is ABC123, both the lookup and rainbow tables would have a hashed version of that that can be compared to your hashed password, so your password would easily be cracked. But if we apply a salt to the password before hashing it, the hashed version of ABC123 in the table will not match your version because your version has the salt applied to it. Precomputing a hash from a password with a salt you don't know is impossible, and including all password plus salt combinations in a lookup or rainbow table doesn't make sense because that would mean the tables would explode exponentially in size, making looking up a hash not worth the time. In other words, dictionary tables like these are ineffective thanks to salting. That brings us to the last technique, key stretching and the attack it protects against. Key stretching is a technique to discourage brute forcing a password by hashing it thousands of times, instead of just once, but maybe I should explain brute forcing first. Imagine someone getting a hold of your password hash and a salt. For your information, that probably means they got a hold of the user database so we are no longer talking about your application data being secure. Chances are that that's breached as well by now. All these password protection techniques are not there to protect someone from logging into an application, they are techniques to protect the password itself. Anyway, so someone with bad intentions has got the hash and the salt, and they cannot get the original password back from it. What they can do is try and brute force the password. In essence, that means trying every possible combination starting with commonly‑used passwords, salting, and hashing them, and then comparing that value with the password hash they have. At a certain moment, they will have found a combination that matches, which means they found your password. So that is brute forcing. Obviously, that's an awful lot of combinations that have to be checked, but computer power rises and fast CPUs or GPUs are able to try out millions of combinations per second, and that's an issue. Key stretching discourages this. Back to our definition. So, instead of hashing the salted password once, it's hashed thousands of times up to the point where it takes 1 or 2 seconds to get to the actual value. That means that on similar hardware, a brute force attack will now need 1 to 2 seconds for each combination, and that is definitely very discouraging. Note that this also means that when a user logs in, it'll take 1 to 2 seconds longer than a not key stretched password, but this is the best practice at the moment of recording. Checking a password should take a bit of time. As far as implementations go, PBKDF 2 and Argon 2 are both good key derivation and key stretching functions, and this is the point in this clip where all those keywords we just learned about will be combined because these functions result in a salted and stretched password hash. Here are the keywords again, but what does that mean? Well, you can input a salt in the PBKDF2 function which will be added to your password. The PBKDF2 function internally uses a pseudorandom function to process the input we give it. Any secure pseudorandom function can be used, but most often HMAC which means hash‑based message authentication code is used. As the name implies, this internally uses a cryptographic hash function like SHA256 or SHA512. So this is how it all nicely ties together. When we use PBKDF2, we get a salted hashed key stretched password as a result. It must be noted that the amount of key stretching and the algorithms you use during this process must be regularly evaluated and potentially changed. When computing power rises, you're going to need additional key stretching iterations. Key derivation functions can be replaced by better ones as can hashing functions. Luckily, we're storing passwords in a central location, instead of in each application. Thanks to that, doing this is quite easy to achieve. Let's implement this in a demo. It's going to be way easier than you might think from the description we just looked into as ASP.NET Cores APIs have got us covered.

Demo - Safely Storing Passwords
In this demo, we will ensure that passwords are safely stored. We're looking at our local user service. Here, we want to inject the password hasher for our user entity. We can do that with constructor injection, and we store it in a private read‑only field. I do need to import the Microsoft.AspNetCore.Identity namespace. PasswordHasher, as the name implies, is a service specifically used for hashing password through the hashPassword method and verifying them. It's actually one of the classes used in ASP.NET Core Identity. If you want, you can have a look at the source code for it via the URL on screen. Let's not forget to register it, so we switched to the HostingExtensions class, and in the Configure Services method, we register PasswordHasher as something that will be resolved when an instance of an IPasswordHasher is requested. Let's have a look at that source code. Version 3, which is the default, and we are thus using implements some familiar concepts. This is PBKDF2 with HMAC‑SHA256, a 128‑bit salt and 10,000 iterations. If we scroll down a bit, we see a salt getting generated as well, which is eventually stored at the end of the hashed password. So with this service, we end up with a hashed salted key‑stretched password, and that's exactly what we need. So let's switch back to our local user service and change our AddUser method a bit. We want to accept the password as well this time so we can hash it. We use the passwordHasher for that. We pass through the password to hash in the user entity, and we then set the password on the userToAdd object to the hashed password, and that's it already for the local user service. Let's also change the signature of the AddUser method on the contract, and that's it for the contract. Alright, now, let's ensure that we call this from our registration page. So we opened the backing code of our registration page, and we see that AddUser no longer works. That makes sense. We now need to input the password as well. So we passed through the password, and if we scroll up a bit, we no longer pass through the clear text password when newing up a user entity. There's something else we need to do though. Seeing we will now hash passwords, we also need to change the verification of the password as that currently still assumes clear text passwords, so let's open our local user service again. Let's find the ValidateCredentialsAsync method. Instead of simply checking for a clear text password, we now use the password hasher. We call into VerifyHashedPassword passing through the user entity, the hashed password, and the inputted password string. We return true if that was successful and false otherwise, and that should be it. Let's give this a try. Let's create a new user and let's input a few fields. There we go. Let's click Register, and we are redirected back to the Image Gallery client. Let's have a look at the output. We were redirected as our newly‑created user. So far, so good. Let's have a look at the database. And if you look at the password of the user we just created, we now see a salted, hashed, and key‑stretched password. So far, so good. One more thing, though, our existing users can no longer log in with their clear text password anymore as we now require a salted, hashed, and key‑stretched password. I generated a few hashes and matching salts which all translate to password as the password, so let me paste those in so we can at least continue logging in with our existing users, so there we go. That was quite a bit of explanation for a demo that didn't contain that much code, and all of that is thanks to ASP.NET Core's built‑in password hashing functionality. Let's talk about email addresses and account activation next.

Activating an Account
We're currently not storing a user's email address, yet it often is a good idea to do this. By doing so, we not only have a way to contact the user, but we also have a mechanism in place we can use for password resets. Yet, if we're going to store that address, we must make sure we verify it to ensure that we end up with a real email address the user owns. You could look at this as part of identity verification. By verifying that email address, we also discourage users from using other people's email addresses instead of their own. The idea is thus that on registration, a user will input an email address, and to that email address, we will send an activation link. The activation link is only valid for a limited amount of time. When the user clicks the link on time, the email address is verified, and we can activate the user's account. So without a verified email address, a user's account will not be activated. So where should we store this address then? In principle, it's a claim, the email claim, but it's also more than that, it becomes an essential part of the various identity‑related flows like the activation flow or the password reset flow. Later on, we'll also use it to link different means of authentication to the same user. For these reasons, it belongs in the user table. Next to storing the email address, we'll also want to store a security code which is used in the activation link and the time when it expires. Let's implement this in a demo.

Demo - Activating an Account
In this demo, we will add account activation functionality. First, let's enhance our user entity. We want to add an email, security code, and security code expiration date to it, and let's also ensure that our data seeding functionality, which is contained in the Identity DbContext fills out email addresses. So let's add an email address for David and an email address for Emma. Like that, when you start from scratch, both users will have email addresses and they also will once we add migration and update the database, so let's do that. Let's first add a migration, make sure you select the Marvin.IDP project here and execute the add‑migration command passing through a name for the migration, and let's update the database. For that, we execute the update‑database command, and there we go, that should do it. Up next, we want to ensure that an email is sent with an activation link. Let's open our LocalUserService and navigate to the AddUser method. The first check we're going to add is check whether the email address is unique. We do this to discourage users from creating multiple accounts, but it will also become important later on once we start linking accounts. Then we need a random token. For that, we can use a cryptographically secure random number generator. If you've done this in the past, you may remember that you've used an RNG crypto service provider instance for that, that can still be used, but it is marked as obsolete. Instead, we can now use the static GetBytes method on the RandomNumberGenerator class that's defined in System.Security.Cryptography. We convert it to a Base64‑encoded string and store it. Encoding this binary data is important. By encoding it, you ensure that the data remains intact when storing it or transferring it over lines and wires designed to work with textual data. So encoding has nothing to do with security as such, it has to do with ensuring the integrity of your data. We'll set the security code expiration date to 1 hour from now. And that's it for adding the code which will generate the code we need for our activation link. Then, we're going to need a new page, the page where the user will end up at once the activation link in the email has been clicked. So let's add a new folder activation underneath the User folder. To it, we add an empty Razor Page. We've done that before. Index.cshtml is an okay name for us. Let me paste in the code for the view. It's a very easy page. We simply show a message we get back from our back‑end code. We'll again work with an InputModel here, so let's add such an InputModel class, and it only has one property, a Message property which is bound to the UI. Seeing we want to ensure that this can be bound to our UI, we add it as a property on our page, and we annotate it with the BindProperty attribute. Just as what we did when creating the Registration page, we add the security headers and allow anonymous attributes to this PageModel. We'll need our local user service to call into our database, so let's inject that. For that, we use constructed injection, and I need to import to Marvin.IDP.Services namespace. Alright, so far for the plumbing of this page. Now let's get to the actual implementation. When OnGet is executed, it means that the user clicked an activation link sent via email. So in the OnGet action, we want to get the security code from the activation link, which means we accept it as a parameter. This shall be passed in as a query string parameter value. We try to activate a user by awaiting a call into ActivateUserAsync, passing through the security code. As that's an async call we await, we need to ensure that our OnGet method is an Async method as well. So we have it returned a task of IActionResult and mark it with the Async keyword. There we go. If a user is activated, we let that know, and if activation fails, we let that know as well. That ActivateUserAsync method, that doesn't exist yet on our local user service, so we will have to create that. It will accept the security code, and we want to have it return a task of bool. So let's click Generate method, that should have added the method to our ILocalUserService, which it indeed did. Let me put it on top here a bit. As far as coding guidelines are concerned, I do like this method to be above the SaveChangesAsync method. Now let's go to the LocalUserService where we can now implement it, so let me run through this. First, we check the input. If no security code was passed through, we through an argument null exception. Then we look for a user who has this security code as a still active security code. If we don't find one, activation failed. If we found one, that means we can activate a user we found, so we set the active Boolean to true, and we set the security code to null, which means that every subsequent request to activate the user will automatically fail and that makes sense seeing the user is already active now. If all checks out, we return true. Alright, back to our index page. After saving the changes, we return the page. Alright, we're getting there, but we're not completely there yet. Let's open the User Registration view. Seeing we now require an email address, we need to add a field to the view so the user can input it. So I add one additional input field for email, that doesn't resolve yet because email isn't yet a property on our InputModel, so let's add it there as well. And there we go, it's a required field with a maximum length of 200 and it should match the rules for email address. So we've got our registration view up to date and we've got the InputModel up to date. What isn't up to date yet is the code that effectively registers the user, so let's open the code behind of the registration page. In OnPost, we create a user and add claims. What we want to do here is add the email address and set the user's Active property to false, that ensures that a user won't be created as active immediately, and it also ensures that the email address is set on the user entity. Alright, what do we need to do next? Let's scroll down a bit. Here, we add the user to the database and we save the changes. What we should do now is generate the activation link and mail it to the user's email address. Now, I haven't got an exchange server for sending emails laying around, so we're going to fake this. Instead of really sending email, I'm just going to write out the activation URL. Like that, we can navigate to it, which will result in the same code being executed as would when we clicked the link that appeared in our mailbox. Let's just write it out to the Console output window. Time for the last page, one that is shown to the user after registration which urges them to check their mailbox. By now, you know the drill. Let's add a new sub folder to the User folder. We'll name it ActivationCodeSent. To it, we add a new Razor Page, an empty one which we will again name index.cshtml. A simple message as content is sufficient, and to the back‑end code, we again add our two attributes, the SecurityHeaders attribute and the AllowAnonymous attribute, which is defined in Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authorization. Alright, now let's ensure that we navigate to this page we just created after we write out the activation code, so we switch back to our user registration back‑end code and here we write out the activation link. The code that we see here is there to issue the authentication cookie, that means logging in the user, and that's exactly what we don't want to do anymore. We don't want to log in the user before the user has an active account, so we simply comment all of this out, and instead, redirect to the page that urges users to check their email. Alright, quite a bit of code, but we're good to go. Let's give this a try. Let's register a new user. I don't think I'm a user yet, so let's register myself, username, Kevin, password, my email address, and Google Chrome is helpful enough to fill out the other fields. Let's click Register. Alright, it looks like I've successfully registered, and I should now check my email to activate my account. In our case, that means checking the console output window to find the activation link, and here is that link. Let me copy that, and let's navigate to it, and there we go. Apparently my account was successfully activated. Let's try navigating to our client application and see if I can log in now. I'm redirected to the IDP. Let's log in as Kevin, and there we go. I'm in the Image Gallery client application. Just to be sure, let's have a look at the output, and we see that the user that's logged in is indeed the user I just created. Pretty cool, right? We now have account activation and a verified email address that opens up a set of additional user management scenarios. Let's have a look.

Additional User Management Related Best Practices
So we've seen a few examples on how to implement user management‑related screens, but obviously, there's more to this than what we've just done. We will implement additional screens and functionality later on in the course, mostly related to dealing with external providers and linking those to a user and multi‑factor authentication. You'll notice that the way to implement these screens is much like what we've done in this module. Not all Identity and Access Management systems, and thus, user management systems are the same. Some systems don't go much further than what we just did, yet some are way more extended. Depending on the functionality you need, different things should be kept in mind, so let's go over a few of the common ones. Some user management systems allow users to manage their personal information. If you allow users to change their email address, ensure you let the user confirm this new address before using it. You need to be sure that this is indeed the address the user has access to as it is this address that is used for sending password reset links to, amongst other things. If you use activation and confirmation links, don't forget to implement resent link functionality. An activation or confirmation link is only valid for a set amount of time and users must be able to receive a new one. For password resets, the user identity must be verified to allow them to reset their password. This can be done in multiple ways, but avoid asking those common questions like where were you born? What's your mother's maiden name, and so on. For most people, those answers can be found online. Instead, send an email with a password reset link to the user's verified email address, much like the account activation link, and that word verified is important here as well. You must make sure that the email address is verified before allowing this. It may sound like a good idea to automatically lock out users after they've unsuccessfully tried logging in a few times, after all, this helps avoid brute force attacks. You could implement this by locking out a user indefinitely, but that also means that you need a way for the user to unlock their account or you need to relay this task to an administrator. Some systems avoid this by locking out the user for a few minutes, instead of indefinitely. Like that, brute forcing is also discouraged, yet I would advise against locking out users. I know this may sound weird, after all, we've been used to this practice for years, but it has some serious disadvantages. For one, the functionality can easily be abused. Someone could, on purpose, lock out hundreds of accounts, and by doing so, cause denial of service, or DoS. A better way to discourage brute force attacks on authentication is, in fact, already in place in our system. We have ensured authentication takes a few seconds through key stretching, and that is an effective way to discourage brute force attacks. Another effective way would be adding a CAPTCHA. Let's have a look at some password policy best practices next.

Password Policy Best Practices
I'd also like to spend a minute on password policies as this is quite important. Most of us know the common sense best practices, use complex characters, regularly change your password, and so on. The thing is those best practices are not best practices anymore, they're hopelessly outdated. For example, long passwords are much better than passwords that include complex characters, uppercase, lowercase, and so on, and forcing a user to regularly change their passwords leads to users choosing variations of the password they already have. It's no secret that a lot of us have passwords like January2022 or 2022summer. The first type tends to be in use with people that work for companies that force them to change passwords every month, and the second example, every 3 months. So what are good policies then? Well, one, don't force users to change passwords regularly. Instead, encourage long passwords or pass phrases instead of complex characters. There's a reason why the password field in our database has a 200 character length. Also, encourage the use of password managers and allow copy pasting into the password field so passwords stored in those managers can easily be inputted. Also, check passwords against the database of often‑used passwords before allowing them. And lastly, it's definitely a good idea to allow or even force users to protect their account with an additional factor of authentication. We'll cover that later on. For now, it's time for the module summary.

Summary
We learned a lot in this module. Let's summarize the most important parts. User management screens can be implemented at the level of the identity provider or in a separate application. Implementing them is a matter of writing custom code which interacts with IdentityServerInteractionService. Important is to remember to pass through the returnUrl, this allows the flow to continue no matter how many screens you need to add. Passwords should be salted, hashed, and key stretched. ASP.NET Core's IPasswordHasher makes this very easy for us. Storing an email address allows you to implement a safe mechanism for password resets. It's important to verify this email address, for example, during account activation. Verifying this is thus typically achieved via an activation link that is sent to the email address. Password resets follow a likewise principle, but those links are only sent to a verified email address. Whenever an email address is changed, make sure to verify it again. That's it for this module. Local credentials are one thing, but in today's world, most of us already have a set of credentials somewhere. Let's learn more about that in the next module.

Integrating with Active Directory, Microsoft Entra ID, and Social Logins
Coming Up
Systems don't always have to locally store credentials. Users might want to use their Windows credentials, Azure Active Directory, or Microsoft Entra ID credentials, or even social credentials like their Facebook credentials to authenticate. That's what this module is about. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. So this module is all about scenarios in which your credentials do not live in a local database, but somewhere else, in Active Directory, Azure Active Directory, or at one of the many other login providers, social or not. We're going to integrate with all of these, and we will start by learning where we should handle that integration. Then we'll continue with the first example, we'll enable Windows authentication or integration with a local Active Directory. After that, we will learn how to integrate with Microsoft Entra ID as Azure Active Directory is called these days. And lastly, we'll also integrate with a social login, Facebook. Let's dive in.

Handling Integration with Third-party Providers
So, we want to allow users to log in with different means. Instead of or together with using a local password, a user may have accounts with credentials in other places. A user might have Windows credentials, Azure AD credentials, or even social credentials like they have on their Facebook account, and they might want to use all of them to authenticate. From a user's point of view, that can be seen as an advantage. They don't have to create yet another password. Not all of these providers use the same protocol as you will see, but the basic premise remains the same. An important question to answer is where will we put these integrations? Well, we could put them at the level of our client app. Just like we integrated with our IdentityServer, we can integrate with other providers. After all, our IdentityServer is also just an identity provider, but we'll quickly run into a few issues. I'm going to take Facebook integration as an example. Imagine that we integrate our client application with Facebook next to the existing integration with IdentityServer. That would mean we would end up with two different users between quotation marks in our application for the same physical person, me. One, my user with my subject as identifier as defined in our local identity database, and two, my user with userId as identifier as defined on Facebook. Seeing our Image Gallery app uses the user identifier to determine which images to show or rather which images are linked to a user. Well, I won't see the images I created when I logged in with my local credentials when I log in with my Facebook account, and that's not what we want. So that means we will have to write a bit of logic to work around that. That's one issue. A second issue is that the claim types will likely be different. Locally, my first name is the value of a claim of type given name. While at level of Facebook, my first name may be in a claim of type first name. So if a web application expects a claim of type given name, but you get one with type first name, the web application needs to take this into account and again work around it. And another issue is that claims might even be missing. I have a role claim locally, but obviously, I don't have that at level of Facebook and that will really cause a few issues. Working around or dealing with all these issues requires logic that does not belong at the level of the client. There's too much responsibility, too much complexity, and not enough reusable logic to put it there. It's better handled at the level of the identity provider who can take care of this once for all applications and not just the Image Gallery web client. Remember, all a client application needs is proof of identity. It shouldn't need to concern itself with all these things. In this module, we will handle all different types of integration with different providers, but we will just leave the users as separate users for now. In the next module, we will dive into linking all of these together. That is when we will learn about federation, federated identity and account linking in detail, including how to transform the incoming claims so we have something we can work with. Time to dive into our first integration use case, Windows authentication.

Use Cases for Windows Authentication
When using Windows authentication, you are using your corporate network's Active Directory domain identities or Windows accounts to identify users. It's a bit of a special one. It's been around for ages, and unlike the other integration scenarios we will cover, this one does not involve OAuth or OpenID Connect. It's also known as Negotiate, Kerberos, or NTLM authentication, and it can be configured for ASP.NET Core apps hosted with IIS, Kestrel, or HTTP.sys. It's best suited to internet environments where users, client apps, and web servers belong to the same Windows domain, so it's a typical requirement for enterprise applications or internal applications. In essence, it's just another way of proving who you are, but your credentials are not stored at the level of the identity provider, they're stored in Active Directory. This is, thus, the first time we will integrate with an external provider for proving who the user is, but before we do that, let's learn how this actually works.

Windows Authentication Beneath the Covers
Windows authentication often feels mysterious. How can a website safely know who you are through your Windows credentials without those credentials being exposed? Well, it's the web server that takes care of this. As mentioned before, Windows authentication can be configured when hosting on IIS, Kestrel, or HTTP.sys. We're going to assume IIS, or rather, IIS Express at this moment. Also, as this type of authentication is best suited to internet environments, I'm going to assume that we're on the same domain. In that case, the most common way to prove who we are with Windows credentials is integrated Windows authentication and that looks as such. So imagine that we are serving to a site that requires Windows authentication. This is configured on our web server, IIS, in our example. The first step is negotiation. That's the client, in other words, our PC, sending a login request to our web server IIS. Then there's the challenge part. IIS answers with a randomly‑generated token named the Challenge. The next step is that the client generates and hashes a response and sends it to the IIS server. After that, the server received a challenge hashed response and compares it to what it knows to be the appropriate response. If the received response matches the expected response, the user is successfully authenticated to the server. So our web server now knows who the user is, meaning it can provide information on that user to the pages we host on it when needed. By the way, these three messages negotiate challenge and authentication, tell us that we're dealing with NTLM as the authentication mechanism. NTLM, or NT Lan Man, and NT indeed stands for Windows NT for those of you who are old enough to remember that. Well, NTLM is a Microsoft Windows proprietary authentication scheme for HTTP, and it is one of the ways to achieve Windows authentication. Kerberos would be another protocol you can use for that. IIS Express, by default, uses NTLM. If you're using the full IIS, you can configure it to use another protocol like Kerberos. Interesting here is that there are never passwords or hashed passwords sent over the network. The client's identity is proven with this challenge response process we just detailed. Let's implement that.

Demo - Enabling Windows Authentication on IIS Express
In this demo, we'll enable Windows authentication with our identity provider. IdentityServer supports this when it's hosted using Kestrel on Windows with IIS and the IIS integration packages or HTTP.sys. The default ASP.NET Core approach is to use Kestrel, so that's okay, but we've used the identity provider in SelfHost most up until now, which allowed us to see what's going on by looking at the console window, but that's not IIS or IIS Express. To enable Windows authentication, we'll first have to host this on the web server like IIS Express and that's easy to do. We're looking at the launchSettings file which is a file that contains local setup settings for your server. There's one profile in here, this SelfHost profile. Let's add another profile, IIS Express. The command name is IIS Express, so this means that we're going to use IIS Express to launch and thus host our project. Next to the IIS Express profile, we also want to pass through some settings for IIS. Through these settings, we can enable Windows authentication. Watch out, though, don't disable anonymous authentication. An authorization request or a request to the discovery document is anonymous, so disabling that would mean our identity provider wouldn't work anymore. One more thing that's important. We also want to set the application URL to the SSL URL as we want to keep on running over an encrypted connection, and this is where we might hit a small roadblock. We've been running our identity provider on port 5001 up until now, but when using SSL together with IIS Express, the port must be between 44,300 and 44,399. So what we've got there, 44,300, is a good value. This does mean that we have to change our web client and API as they need to know where to find our IdentityServer. So let's save this and let's start by changing the authority at level of the API. So let's set this to port 44,300, and let's save, and let's do the same at level of our web client. We've got an IDP client configured here, let's switch that to the correct port, and here where we set up our OpenID Connect middleware, we also change the port number. Alright, let's save all of this, and let's set the identity provider as the only starter project for now. We can choose SelfHost as profile. Before we continue, let's restart Visual Studio so our settings are taken into account. On top of the screen here, we can now select IIS Express. Let's give that a try, and let's navigate to the new URL, and there we go. That still seems to work. Now, let's select multiple starter projects again to see if the integration with our IDP from our web client and API still work, so we select the API, the IDP, and the client application, and we launch again, and we're now redirected to our identity provider from our client application, so everything still seems to work, but we don't see a way to log in with our Windows credentials yet, some sort of button showing up here would be what we'd expect. Let's continue with the next demo.

Because of that, this method will always return false, and we will remain at the level of the identity provider. Where would at subject value be coming from? Windows users don't have a subject value, yet here we are. Well, let's switch back to our Windows Index page. We're newing up an IdentityServer user with primary as ID as value for the subject. Every Windows user has such a value which is used to identify the Windows user. Therefore, it's used as a subject. So what are we going to do to fix this?
