That isn't really correct. What we want to end up with is treating this as one of multiple ways one user can prove their identity, but that's something for the next module. Let's look at a few other providers.

Federation with Third-party Identity Providers
Windows credentials are just one way of authenticating without local credentials, typically suited for enterprise or intranet scenarios. There's another provider that fits that bill, Azure AD, or rather, Microsoft Entra ID, and what about non‑intranet enterprise scenarios? Think about Facebook, Google, X, Microsoft, and so on. Personally, I have accounts on all of these. It would be nice to be able to use those, instead of being forced to create yet another local password. This is convenient for your users, and it also shifts a lot of the complexities of managing logging in to a third‑party like Microsoft or Facebook. It's their identity provider that handles most of that. This principle can be seen as federated authentication and/or using a basic form of a federated identity, yet it is a bit too early to dive into that as this typically also entails linking user identities, which we will get to in the next module. Important for now to remember is that federated authentication can be achieved by integrating with additional identity providers. An IdentityServer can easily do that. We will use Azure AD and Facebook as examples in the upcoming demos, but let's first check out how this works. We're looking at that high‑level OpenID Connect overview from the beginning of the course. Our Image Gallery web application was a client that required authentication, and our IDP allows a user to provide that via a local password, but we can also consider our identity provider a client that requires an authenticated user and that gets that from another identity provider. After all, just like our Image Gallery client is a web app, so is our identity provider, so the idea is that the Image Gallery client requires an authenticated user and asks our identity provider to provide that via an identity token. To be able to provide that identity token, we must be logged in to our identity provider. To log in to our identity provider, our identity provider is going to ask Facebook, for example, to provide it with proof of authentication. In Facebook's case, that means we'll be redirected to Facebook, login, and Facebook will provide our identity provider with a token that is proof of my identity. Our identity provider will validate it and use the resulting information to authenticate. From that moment on, the identity provider knows who I am, so it can provide a token to our Image Gallery client application which our image gallery client application can use to log in. The protocol that's used can vary. Between our Image Gallery client and Identity Server, we use OpenID Connect. Our identity provider can then use that same protocol to integrate with a third‑party provider or another protocol like SAML or proprietary protocol. Most third‑party providers these days support OpenID Connect or a slight variation of it. In older enterprise systems, you will often still encounter SAML, although that's definitely becoming less prevalent. Microsoft has provided various packages to make integrating with these providers easy. Let's use one to integrate with Facebook in the upcoming demos.

Demo - Inspecting Support for Federating with a Third-party Identity Provider
In this demo, we'll have a look at how IdentityServer, through the sample UI, implements support for external providers, in other words, the part I deliberately skipped when we implemented Windows authentication previously. In the ExternalLogin folder, we find two pages, Challenge and Callback. Challenge sounds familiar, right? We also had that with Windows authentication. When challenging a scheme linked to an external provider like Azure AD or Facebook, the idea is that that initiates the round trip to the external identity provider. Once we come back from that external identity provider, we need to process the result. It's the Callback page that handles that. The rest of the flow to the external identity provider is handled by middleware we'll integrate with in the next demo. Let's start with the Challenge page. OnGet is executed when a user clicks on one of the external provider buttons on the Login page. Currently, that's just Windows in our case, but you've seen the code, right? The other external providers we'll integrate with, like Facebook and Azure AD, will have their buttons show up there as well. The scheme value and return URL are passed in. What's returned is a challenge result. Challenge result is the result of an ASP.NET Core Authentication Manager Challenge command, in other words, the built‑in authentication manager. That challenge method is part of controller base, so part of ASP.NET Core. By calling it, the scheme is challenged. In other words, whichever middleware we defined that matches the provider name as scheme will be triggered. To better understand this, let's switch to the client application. Here, OpenID Connect is a scheme that's defined. If we, in our client app, call a challenge command from a controller passing through OpenID Connect as the scheme name, the OpenID Connect middleware will be triggered to start the flow. Back to the identity provider. When calling challenge, authentication properties are passed through. The URL where we want to end up after the challenge has completed, which is the callback page, and two items that we want to see passed through to the callback page, the return URL and the scheme. After authentication at level of the third‑party provider, we end up at the callback page. This very much matches the interaction between our Image Gallery client and IdentityServer. In that case, the Image Gallery client is the client app that redirects to IdentityServer, and after authentication, IdentityServer redirects back to the client. In this case, IdentityServer is the client, and the third‑party provider is the identity provider. Let's open that Callback page. Let's have a look at the OnGet action. First, we read the external user from a temporary cookie, that cookie was created by the middleware that matches the scheme that was triggered in the previous step, so we will have an Azure AD and a Facebook scheme, and that will result in middleware being triggered that ends up writing this cookie. We get the claims that were returned from the external provider, and we try and find the users identifier at the level of that external provider. How that is done depends on the external provider. In most cases, it's the sub for OIDC or name claim for WS federation, but if your external provider uses another claim, this code must be changed. The rest of the code you see here, FindByExternalProvider, AutoProvisionUser is something we will ignore for now. This again, has to do with linking different means of authentication to one account. We're saving that for the next module, so let's comment this out. Up next is a call into CaptureExternalLoginContext. This is used to store claims, data, anything you might need in the local IdentityServer cookie. Let's have a look at that. A very difficult thing to do here when using OpenID Connect is storing the identity token that's been returned from the external provider, so it can be passed to that external provider to automate signing out, and then we're entering familiar territory. An IdentityServer user is created which is used to sign into our local identity provider. From this moment on, we no longer need that cookie from the external provider. All the information we needed from it has been used and potentially stored, so it's deleted, and then we continue with the flow as usual. I do have to input a subject and username to be able to continue. Later on, we will use a local subject and username for that, but at this moment, we've not linked accounts yet, so we simply pass through what we get from the external provider. That's the providerUserId, in other words, the identifier of the user at the level of the external provider. And while we're at it, let's get rid of trying to inject the test user store. We don't want to use it anymore. There we go. That's the last of it. From this moment on, the test users are not used anywhere in our code base. So this is how using external providers works, except that it doesn't really work yet. We still have some configuration and coding to do. Let's look into Azure AD first.

Integrating with Microsoft Entra ID (Azure Active Directory)
Azure Active Directory or Microsoft Entra ID is part of Microsoft Entra, it's an enterprise identity service that provides single sign‑on, multi‑factor authentication and conditional access to guard against 99.9% of cyber security attacks. It is one of Microsoft's flagship Azure products. We are currently interested in integrating with its identity provider component using Open ID Connect. To do that, helper libraries exist like Microsoft's Identity Framework libraries, but personally, I think they mostly cause confusion due to trying to hide as much of the Open ID Connect protocol away as they possibly can. In the end, we can look at Entra ID as an identity provider that implements Open ID Connect, which means we can use Microsoft's built‑in Open ID Connect middleware to integrate with it, and that is exactly what we will do. But first, we need to register our IdentityServer as a client application for Azure AD. Let's continue with the next demo.

The user is accessible via the Microsoft graph, so let's click that, and let's search for a delegated email permission. We see that it's part of the OpenID permissions, that sounds familiar. As you remember, OpenID Connect defines a set of standardized scopes. We can see a few of these coming back here. Let's grant access to those. Demo - Integrating with Facebook
We've got an application registered at Facebook. Now, it's time to add Facebook authentication to IdentityServer. The first thing we'll need is a piece of middleware to add to the request pipeline. It's this middleware that will handle the flow between IdentityServer and Facebook. Let's open the NuGet dialog and let's look for Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authentication. We've already used the OpenID Connect JWT bearer and cookies packages, and as you can see, there's also packages here to make integration with Google, Microsoft account, Twitter, and so on a bit easier. For Azure AD integration, we used the default OpenID Connect middleware. For Facebook authentication, we're going to use the Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authentication.Facebook middleware. The reason for that is that Azure AD plays nice with the OpenID Connect standard, but Facebook doesn't completely follow it. Instead, they rolled out their own slightly different version. This package makes integration with that easier, so let's install it and let's open the HostingExtensions class. We are in ConfigureServices where we previously configured integration with Azure AD. To configure authentication with Facebook, we call into AddAuthentication followed by AddFacebook which is coming from the package, we just added, we pass through a scheme name Facebook, in our case, and then we set the options for this new authentication provider. We input the application ID and application secret we noted down in the previous demo. The SignInScheme is set to the external cookie authentication scheme defined by IdentityServer. As we know by now, this scheme specifies the name of the cookie handler that will temporarily store the outcome of the external authentication. Just as when we integrated with Azure AD, we use the external cookie authentication scheme defined by IdentityServer and that's it. The rest of the code is there already, and we already checked that out in a previous demo. The breakpoints from our Azure integration are also still there, so let's build and run, and then we can step through it. We see an additional button to log in with Facebook. Let's click it, and we end up on our challenge page. This should start to feel quite familiar by now. On this page, Facebook will be challenged to provide us with some information on who logged in at level of Facebook. It does that through a token. Let's continue. I'm at level of Facebook, and I need to enter my password. That's because we specifically ask for that when setting up the registration at level of Facebook. Let's continue. By default, the Facebook authentication middleware requests my name, profile picture and email address. Let's allow that, and we end up in the OnGet method on the Callback page. Let's have a look at what we get back from Facebook. We get back the name identifier, that's the unique ID at level of Facebook, my email address, name, given name, and surname claims. Let's continue. We're at level of our client application. Let's have a look at the output and there we go, we're logged in as a user with sub value my ID at Facebook. As expected to, we don't see a lot of claims here yet. In the next module, we'll fix all of that. And that's it for the demos in this module. Let's look into a few challenges you might encounter when integrating with third‑party providers.

Challenges When Integrating with Third-party Identity Providers
As you've noticed, it's quite easy to integrate with a third‑party provider, and it definitely has its advantages, but there's also things to keep in mind when doing this, including possible disadvantages. The main thing you want to keep in mind is that by doing this, you are placing a lot of trust in a third‑party. In fact, you're making the identity provider you're integrating with part of your trust domain. If a security issue happens at the level of the provider you're integrating with, your IDP, and thus, your clients potentially become less secure as well. Now the big guys, Facebook, Microsoft, Google, etc are attacked every moment of every single day, so we could assume that they know what they're doing, but we also know that security issues do happen even with those big players. It's not a reason to avoid third‑party integration, but it is something to keep in mind. Then again, so is keeping your own identity provider safe. IdentityServer, which we are using, has a pretty good track record, but here two issues can happen. It's important to keep up to date with regular updates and penetration checks, so trust is important. Next to that, not all identity providers are created equal, it's up to the identity provider to decide what is supported. I'm going to use one example, signing out. Often, the idea is that when you sign out of your client application, you also want to sign out of your identity provider because otherwise, you will immediately be logged into your client again because your identity provider will potentially provide you with tokens as long as you are signed into it. Add another provider to the mix like Facebook. When you sign out of your client, you sign out of your identity provider and your identity provider triggers a sign out at level of Facebook. This is named a federated sign‑out. The issue here is that Facebook does not support that. From their point of view, it makes sense. They want people to stay logged into Facebook. The consequence of this is that as long as the user is signed into Facebook, they'll also be able to sign into our identity provider and thus our client application. Now, we can more or less work around that with the force reauthenication setting at Facebook we enabled, but in the end, this is all up to the third‑party provider, and it can change at any given time, so the takeaway here is investigate the possibilities of the identity provider you're going to integrate or federate with, so you don't run into unwelcome surprises. Let's learn about additional third‑party providers.

Integrating with Other Third-party Identity Providers
In the demo, we integrated with Azure AD on Facebook, but as mentioned before, a lot of other identity providers exist. We're looking at NuGet. Microsoft provides middleware for integrating with a lot of different identity providers, namely Google, Microsoft, Twitter, and so on. You can find those on NuGet. Just look for Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authentication. Next to that, you can integrate with any identity provider that supports OpenID Connect with Microsoft's OpenID Connect middleware, which we used at level of our web client. A few examples are ADFS, AzureAD, Auth0, Ping, TrustBuilder, WSO2 Identity Server, and so on. All of these support OpenID Connect. For some libraries exist that make it slightly more convenient to integrate with them, but you can achieve the same with the basic OpenID Connect middleware. If the identity provider you have to integrate with doesn't support OpenID Connect yet, it'll most likely support Saml2P. To integrate with such an IDP, have a look at the link on screen. And lastly, if you still need to support WS federation, have a look at the Microsoft.AspNetCore.Authentication.WsFederation package. Time for the module summary.

Summary
Most of us already have credentials somewhere like in their Active Directory, on Azure AD, or on social providers like Facebook, Google, Twitter, Microsoft, and so on. By integrating with those, our users can log in with those credentials, instead of being forced to create a local password. This is convenient, and it shifts a lot of the complexities of managing logging in to a third‑party like Facebook or Microsoft. Do keep in mind that when integrating with a third‑party provider, you must make sure you investigate what it can do and how trustworthy it is to avoid unwelcome surprises. You're making it part of your trusted domain. Security issues at their level are your issues as well. Also important is that integration comes with complexities from linking users over claims transformation to dealing with missing claims. Therefore, the integration does not belong in the client application, it belongs at level of the identity provider. We did three integrations, one using the old Windows authentication‑related protocols, and two, using OpenID Connect or a slight variation of it in case of Facebook. We started with Windows authentication. We learned that it is best suited for intranet environments where users, client apps, and web servers belong to the same Windows domain. It is a typical requirement for enterprise or internal applications. Important to remember here is that Windows authentication has to be enabled at the level of your web server. Then we integrate with Azure AD and Facebook over OpenID Connect or slight variations of the standard. So as far as integration goes, it comes down to starting a flow using a supported protocol from our IDP. Once we log into the third‑party provider, it can provide proof of authentication to our IDP which can be used to authenticate at the level of our IDP which then allows our IDP to provide proof of authentication, which is an identity token, to our client app. Integrating with almost any provider that supports OpenID Connect, SAML, or WsFederation is possible. We still have a few issues to fix though, let's do that next. We will dive into Federated Identity and linking user accounts to different credential providers.

User Provisioning, Federation, and Federated Identity
Coming Up
At this moment, multiple means of authentication have been integrated at the level of our identity provider, but all these users are treated as separate entities, and that is not realistic. In this module, we will solve that. I am Kevin, and I will guide you through it. This module is all about integrating external users with local users. It boils down to two terms common in Identity and Access Management, federated authentication, and federated identity. We will learn what both mean and apply them to our use case. By doing so, we will learn how to provision users and link third‑party providers to local users. Let's get started.

Integrating Local Users with External Users
Soon after we started integrating with Windows authentication and third‑party providers in the previous module, we had an issue. I logged in with my Windows account and I logged in with my Azure AD account and Facebook account, but I was treated as being a different user, even though it was me in all cases. Now you could keep it like this and leave it up to the client application to figure out that it is indeed the same user, but that's putting a lot of responsibility on the client, and often, that client does not even have the necessary context and information to know how to link those two together. Moreover, this would have to happen for all clients, and if another third‑party provider is going to be supported, all client applications would have to be adjusted. As we decided earlier, it's clear that the client is not the correct place to handle this. It's up to our identity provider to link together who we are at another IDP to our local account. That means that the client application, the Image Gallery client in our example, would get back one and the same sub value, so the user's identifier regardless of whether I used Windows authentication, Facebook authentication, or any other provider, or even my local password. And that brings us to two often‑used terms in Identity and Access Management, federation, and federated identity.

Federated Authentication and Federated Identity
We touched upon federated authentication and federated identity briefly in the previous module. Federated authentication or federation means federating out authentication to a third party, another identity provider like Facebook, the IDP of a company your company works with, Active Directory, and so on. So our identity provider relies on an other identity provider for authentication. That makes our identity provider and those other identity providers part of the same federation, which is where the term comes from. A federated identity is the means of linking a person's electronic identity and attributes stored across multiple distinct IDPs. In other words, we're linking our identity on Facebook or in Active Directory to our identity at level of our own IDP. Some of my claims may live on Facebook or on Azure AD or locally in the database, and together, they make up my identity. Now, that does not mean that those claims are only kept at the level of those external identity providers. In fact, it's very common to store claims that live as an external identity provider locally as well and regularly update them if only for performance reasons. So that is what we're going to do. We're going to link our identities, but to be able to do that, we need something to link them on. By that, I mean that we need some sort of key that exists in both systems and that we can trust to be correct. A very typical key to use is the verified email address, but this does assume that you trust the identity provider you integrate with to have correctly verified that email address. This key is essential. On the key rests the reliability of your federated identity, and this is not always easy. When creating a solution like this, especially for enterprises where integrations with tens of different identity providers are not uncommon, it is not unusual to not find a trustworthy key. Often, part of this can be automated, but part of it has to be done manually. For example, by manually creating a list which states that user A in system A is the same user as user B in system B. This, by the way, closely ties into user provisioning, which is another term you might have heard. That is the process that ensures that users are created, changed, disabled, deleted, and/or given the permissions and/or claims they need. So users that are registering themselves are provisioning themselves or their account. When we automate user creation while linking IDPs, we're also provisioning the user. In other words, the Identity and Access Management system does this, and of course, combinations exist where part of a user's identity is provisioned by the IAM system and part is provisioned by the user. Alright, we now know what we have to do. We'll start with enhancing our database schema to allow linking external providers to a user, and we will continue with implementing a flow that does exactly that.

Demo - Enhancing the Database Schema for Federated Identity
In this demo, we'll add a table to our user database so we can store the links to third‑party identity providers. Let's add a new class to the Entities folder at level of our IDP. We'll name the class UserLogin. What should be in this class? Well, we want to start with an ID which will serve as the primary key. Let's import the System.ComponentModel.DataAnnotations namespace so this resolves. By the way, we don't have to be explicit about this. A property with name ID will automatically be regarded as primary key, but I still prefer to be explicit about this as this, in my opinion, makes the code a bit more readable. This UserLogin class should be linked to our User class, so we add a UserID and the User navigation property. Then we need to know which provider we're integrating with. This can be Facebook, but it can also be Active Directory, Azure Active Directory, Google, the name of a provider of a company you're integrating with, and so on, it's a value we can choose. We name that value Provider. And lastly, we need the identifier of the user at the level of that provider. Like that, we can link that user to our user. We name that ProviderIdentityKey. We'll also add a ConcurrencyStamp and have our class implement IConcurrencyAware. That's it for the UserLogin class. To our user, we can then add a collection of user login. We name that collection Logins, and on our DbContext, we can add a DbSet. Like that, we can easily access our user logins. Alright, that should be sufficient. Let's add a migration for that. So we open the Package Manager Console, ensure that Marvin.IDP is selected, and execute the add‑migration command. We give the migration a name say AddUserLogin. The migration has been added. Let's update the database. While we're at it, let's make a few more changes. We want to allow the username, password, and email of a user to remain null. That's because, in this module, we will enable user accounts that don't have a local password, username, or email address, but instead, only log in with external credentials. So let's make these explicitly nullable in our entity model. EFCore takes this into account when generating column constraints for our SQLite database. Alright, let's add a migration for that, and let's update our database. That should be it. Onto the next demo.

Transforming Claims
Let's talk about claims transformation. Our client supports the profile scope, so if we request the profile scope, which we do, we'd expect to get back give name and family name claims amongst a few others, but that is not how we get them back from the external provider, Facebook, in our case. The claim types look like what's on screen now. When they are stored like that, they will not be returned when the profile scope is requested, so what we want to do is transform the input we get from Facebook to claim types we can work with. Another common thing to do during such a claims transformation step is add, change, or remove claims, so they are fit for your application. Let's do that.

Demo - Provisioning a New User with a Federated Identity (Part 2)
In this demo, we'll continue with provisioning a new user. We'll transform the incoming claim types, and thus, ensure that our client can again receive user‑related identity claims. There's a few claims we can map, email address, given name, and surname can be mapped to email, given name, and family name. One of the easiest ways to define these mappings is by using a dictionary. The key is the original claim type and the value is the new claim type, so we add a new dictionary of string, string, and we new it up with these mappings. Now, let's use it. Before passing through the list of claims to the method to provision the user, we transform them. We run through all the incoming claims, check the types, and if one matches, we create a new claim from it with a new type, but the original value. If no match is found, the claim is ignored. We also need two additional claims for our client to correctly function, the role claim and the country claim. We could ask for that by adding an additional view, but we won't do that in this demo. You already know how to do that. We've created a view for user registration previously. To be able to continue, I'll pass through fixed values, FreeUser for role and be for country. Lastly, we passed through this mappedClaims list to our localUserService. Let's give this a try. Let's choose to log in through Facebook. Let's continue, and that seems to have worked. Let's have a look at the output, and there we go, the expected and requested claims are returned to the client. Let's have a look at our database. We find one new user without a username and without a password, and for that user, we see that the expected claims have correctly been added. If we have a look at the UserLogins table, we also see that this user account is linked to Facebook as a provider. So far, so good. Now, this isn't everything you can potentially do here. You might want to update claims from the third‑party provider that are now stored in our database at regular intervals. You might want to automatically add additional claims coming from the third‑party provider or remove those that aren't available anymore at that level. Often, also additional checks on the key you use to identify a user can be required, for example, to ensure that you don't end up with duplicate values. All of this depends on your use case, but as far as implementation goes, it follows the same principles as what we just did in the last two demos. Let's have a look at a few common variations of this flow.

Provisioning a New User with a Federated Identity – Flow Variations
An obvious variation is asking for additional claims to be inputted manually, for example, a role claim, if you want to allow that or an address, a country, a phone number, and so on. You would ask for that during user provisioning. We didn't do that, but asking for additional information is a common practice. It's obviously custom work, but there is nothing new here. It's a matter of creating a new view with fields to fill out, just don't forget to pass through the return URL. We've done this a few times already, so you know how to do that. Another common variation is requiring a user to choose a local password or stronger local means of authentication. In those cases, third‑party integration is often only used for automatically filling out values for common claims. That is a typical thing to do if you don't really trust the third party provider, but want to make user provisioning or user registration a bit easier for the user. That being said, third‑party authentication can be kept together with a local password or not. Lastly, it is not uncommon to require a user to activate an account via an activation link, even when being provisioned by a third party, this requires less reliance on or trust in the process at the level of the third‑party provider. We already implemented sending such a link in the best practices for the user management module. So remember, you can customize this to your needs, allowing you to put more or less trust in the third party as you see fit. Let's continue with a demo in which we will link an external provider to an existing user.

Demo - Linking a Provider to an Existing User

As we remember from when we talked about linking identities on the slides, we need something to link them on, some sort of key that exists in both systems and that we can trust to be correct, but finding a good key isn't easy, and it is not unusual to not be able to find one that exists in both systems. That means that part of this can be manual work, that manual work could be finding a key in System A that can be linked to System B and then taking that list as the basis for linking, but we're in luck. My local user account has a verified email address which was used during account activation, and my account on Azure AD also has that same verified email address, so in this case, we can use that to link them. With the demos we've now seen, you should be able to create most common scenarios. Let's check out a few more common use cases.

Additional Federated Identity Use Cases
So we covered a few common federated identity use cases, but there's more. You could additionally implement a case where a local account already exists and isn't linked to a third‑party provider yet, just like we just did, but via a user profile page on which they can choose to link their local account to a third‑party provider. This could also be an automated check on login. When users aren't signed in and choose to sign in with their Microsoft account, for example, you could check whether the verified email address which is returned from Microsoft's identity provider matches a verified email address at level of your IDP. Then, we could ask the user if they want to link them, or related to it, allowing users to link their local account to multiple third‑party providers from that same profile page or via a likewise automated process. Another use case is the other way around, unlinking via a user profile page. And of course, the nuclear option, deprovisioning a user automatically or not. All of these use cases follow likewise flows and techniques as we just implemented. Know that these are not uncommon use cases, but that doesn't mean that each identity provider needs all of them, of course. Let's have a look at the module summary.

Summary
This module was all about integrating external users with local users. We encountered two common Identity and Access Management terms. Federated authentication means federating out authentication to a third party, another identity provider, like Facebook, the identity provider of a company your company works with, Active Directory, and so on. So our IDP would rely on other IDP for authentication. A federated identity is closely related to that, it's the means of linking a person's electronic identity and attributes stored across multiple distinct identity providers. In other words, we would be linking our identity on Azure AD to our identity at level of our own IDP, for example, and that is what we did throughout this module. We looked into various use cases and there's more we didn't cover. Of course, there's always more, but in the end, it boils down to provisioning users and accounts. There's two main takeaways from this module in that regard. One is that IdentityServer is very customizable. The UI is just an ASP.NET Core application, so you can add views to it, view models, call into a database, and customize the flows to your liking, and to finding a good key to link your identities across systems is essential, but it cannot always be automated. Often, manual work is required if only creating a mapping table. And with that, we can continue with dealing with credentials. Up next, we will tackle multi‑factor authentication.

Supporting Multi-factor Authentication
Coming Up
We can improve the authentication process by using multiple factors of authentication. In this module, you will learn how. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. We will start by learning what two‑factor authentication or multi‑factor authentication is. We will quickly learn that there's a variety of ways to implement it. We'll implement one of the most common ones today. We're going to use an authenticator app like Google or Microsoft Authenticator as a second factor. Let's learn what multi‑ factor authentication is.

Introducing Multi-factor Authentication
Multi‑factor authentication provides identification of users by means of the combination of two or more different factors. A factor should be seen as a type of communication. Three commonly‑used types are something you know like a password or a pin code, something you own like a digital pass, a cart, a smartphone, and something that is inseparable from you, something that you are like a fingerprint or an iris scan. Two other examples that are less common are something you do. A lot of smartphones these days can be unlocked with a gesture, that is a good example of this. Another potential factor is somewhere you are. This can be gathered from, for example, your IP address. I wouldn't consider this as a strong enough type of authentication on its own, but it can be useful as an additional type to check. The arc of typical example of multi‑factor authentication is withdrawing money from an ATM. You need something you know, which is your pin code, and something you possess, your bank card. Those two things are required to prove that you are who you say you are, in other words, to prove your identity. By the way, if only two factors are used, multi‑factor authentication is also referred to as 2FA or two‑factor authentication. MFA is broader. It means two or more factors are used while 2FA means exactly two are used. To be able to call something 2FA or MFA, it is important to use different authentication factors. Two‑factor authentication with two username/password combos is not a good idea. While the first factor is often a username and password combination, the second factor should not again be something that the user knows, it should be related to something the user possesses or something that is inseparable from the user, potentially combined with some of the other factors. What's often used these days is a combination of username/password combined with a one‑time password that is sent to you by mail, or even better, time‑based, one‑time password that's generated on your phone by using something like Google Authenticator. Let's look into that.

Supporting MFA with a One-Time Password Through Email
What we can do is require a user to input a one‑time password, or OTP, after the user has provided a valid username/password combination, Such an OTP is a generated unique password that is only valid for a single login session. It cannot be reused, so it is not vulnerable to replay attacks. That's already a lot better than a regular password. To deliver this password to our users, we can send a mail to their verified email address. That will work, and it's used relatively often, but is it a good idea? Well, the thing is that this isn't true, multi‑factor authentication. Remember, we need to use different factors of authentication, something we know, something we have, and so on. NIST, the National Institute of Standards and Technology, states that the ability to receive email messages does not generally prove the possession of a specific device, that kind of makes sense, right? In other words, this isn't fully a factor we can use as something we have. It doesn't completely fall under something we know either. Although, you could argue that knowing the username and password for the email account would be sufficient, but that is in the context of the email provider, not in the context of the identity provider we're trying to log into. So, sending an OTP via email falls somewhere in between the cracks. It is okay to use this when no better means are available, and even though it is not a real second factor, it's definitely better than only requiring a username/password combination, but we can do better. The National Institute of Standards and Technology advises to use a more secure authentication method like a soft OTP, and that is where authenticator apps come into play.

Supporting MFA with an Authenticator Application
An authenticator app, like Google Authenticator or Microsoft Authenticator, is an example of a soft one‑time password implementation. This is a piece of software, typically an app on your phone like Google Authenticator, that generates one‑time passwords on the device. There are two common standards that are often used for generating these OTPs, HMAC‑based OTP and TOTP. An HMAC‑based OTP generates an OTP based on hashed message authentication code, an HOTP. This is an event‑based OTP algorithm where the moving factor is an event counter. TOTP, or time‑based one‑time password, bases the moving factor on the time, it thus provides short‑lived OTPs. This is desirable for enhanced security, and this is the one we will use. Be careful though, it is essential that the time on the device which generates the TOTP is synced with the time on the server which validates the TOTP. If there's too much difference in time, the generated TOTPs won't match. Google Authenticator and Microsoft Authenticator both support generating HOTPs and TOTPs. I'm going to use Google Authenticator, but you can choose the one that suits you. So how does this work? Well, the TOTP is generated from a secret, both client and server must know that secret. If at the same time, the TOTP is generated from the same secret, they match, so the client generates a TOTP, and the user sends the generated TOTP to the server, for example, by typing it in or automatically via the client app. The server generates the TOTP as well, and if they match, we're good to go. So that part is quite straightforward, but first of all, we need to set this up in a secure manner. Our server, the identity provider in this case, needs to generate a secret, that is a 16 alphanumeric character string, so each user has their own secret. To generate the TOTP we need, that secret, so it must be transmitted to the Google Authenticator application, and we don't want to send this over the wire as that is a risk. So there's two ways to get that secret to the client device. We can let the user input it manually, or a more common approach, we can let the user scan a QR code, that QR code contains URI which contains the secret. An example of such a URI can be seen on screen. It follows the OTP out structure. Let's look at it in a bit more detail. The type is TOTP, that's a fixed value. Label is typically a combination of the issuer plus the user, for example, Marvin, as that is the issuer, our identity provider, and my email address. As parameters, we want to pass through the secret and the issuer, again, Marvin. Once the authenticator app has picked up the URL from the QR code, the user can now optionally enter a generated TOTP to verify that setup was successful. Once the secret has been safely stored at the level of our identity provider and in the authenticator app, registration is complete. So that's it for the registration flow. The second flow is the authentication flow. We just looked into that briefly, so let's now look into it in a bit more detail. Every time the authenticator app is opened, a random number is generated for use at a fixed interval. This is the TOTP, and that TOTP is generated from the stored secret. After a specified amount of time has elapsed which is typically 30 seconds, a new TOTP gets generated. The user should input the generated TOTP at level of our identity provider during the authentication process. There, the identity provider also generates a TOTP using the stored secret. When both match, authentication is successful. To deal with clock offsets between the device and server, typically the last few TOTPs are accepted. Also often to be able to open the Google Authenticator app, a user is expected to input a pin code, fingerprint, or face scan which adds an additional layer of security. This authentication factor is proof of something you own, the smartphone you're running the authenticator app on. Combined with username/password, which is something you know, we achieve true multi‑factor authentication. Let's implement support for this.

Demo - Supporting MFA with an Authenticator Application (Enhancing the Database Schema)
In this demo, we will enhance our database so it's ready to support MFA with an authenticator app. We'll have to add a table and store the secret and recovery code for each user, so let's add such an entity class. We'll name it UserSecret. Next, we'd be in concurrency aware. We give it an ID and a user id, including a navigation property, to user. We also give it a secret column and a name. In case you're integrating with multiple secret token types, this name makes it easier to find. We expose it via our DbContext which allows us to easily access this DbSet, and we also want to add it as a collection on our User object. By doing so, we create an object graph that's easy to work with. Let's add a migration. For that, we call into add‑migration in the Package Manager Console, and we give it a name, say AddUserSecret. Make sure that the Marvin.IDP project is selected as default project. And that takes care of the migration, now let's update the database, and there we go. That should do it. Let's continue by implementing the pages for the registration flow.

Supporting MFA with an Authenticator Application (Registration)
In this demo, we will learn how to create a page so we can register the authenticator application on a per user basis. We'll need a view on which we're going to generate a QR code, and we need the back‑end code for that. Let's add an MfaRegistration folder underneath the Pages folder, and to it, we add a Razor Page. An empty Razor Page will do and index.cshtml is a good name for that. We inject our local user service. For that, I need to import the Marvin.IDP.Services namespace. We're going to need this one soon to register the UserSecret. As we've done before, we annotate the page model with the SecurityHeaders attribute, but this time, we don't add allow anonymous as an attribute. Instead, we add the Authorize attribute. We will need a user to be logged in before being able to register a second factor of authentication. In the OnGet method, we need to generate a key URI as we learned on the slides, so we need our email address and a secret of 16 alphanumeric characters. We already know how to generate a cryptographically random secret as we did this before when we had to generate the code for the activation link, but this won't work as this can contain special characters as well, not just alphanumeric characters, so we need to make sure we only have alphanumeric characters in our secret. Let's do that. First, we add a character array with only alphanumeric characters, and then we add the code to generate the secret. Let me paste that in so we can run through it. We want to end up with 16 alphanumeric characters. We're using the generated bytes tokenData to get a random integer, which is then used to select an item from our character array. The default int has 32 bits, so 4 bytes. Let me import the System.Security.Cryptography namespace and System.Text. So because the default int has 32 bits, we need a 64‑byte token, so we generate the token, generate an int from each 4 bytes, convert that to a character from our character array and append it to the eventual secret string, and that results in a secret we can work with. Then let's get the current user from the database. We need it because we need access to the email address. Let's import the IdentityModel namespace for this to resolve, and let's make sure that our method is an async one. There we go. Our call into GetUserBySubjectAsyc now resolves. We've got the user object, which means we've got the user email address, which means we can create the key URI. Let me just import the system .NET namespace here. So generating the key URI follows what we learned about on the slides. We follow the same template, and we pass through Marvin, the email address, and the generated secret. We want to pass this key URI to the view because at the level of the view, that key URI will be used to generate a QR code from. We don't have an implementation for the view yet, but let's already add a ViewModel class. As we learned on the slides, we will need a key URI, and we will also pass through the secret to store once the user has successfully added our identity provider using the authenticator app. The ViewModel is only used for viewing, so it only contains our key URI string. Then we add another model, the InputModel, this is bound to the view, it thus, contains info that we can use on post like the secret. On our Index page, we then expose the ViewModel as a View property, and we also expose an InputModel as an Input property which is bound to the view. This follows the same principles as we've used throughout the rest of the course. Let's set these two properties. We new up a ViewModel passing through a key URI, and we new up an InputModel passing through the secret. Alright, onto that view. Let me paste that in so we can run through it. It's a pretty straightforward view. It contains a bit of explanation followed by a div that's going to contain our QR code which contains the key URI. The secret is also stored in a hidden field, so we still have it on postback, and there's a button the user will be able to click once registration was successful. By the way, it may sound unsecure to pass through a secret to a view, which means we pass it through to the browser, and that's of course, a fact. Users can simply look at the page source and view the secret, but that's okay in this case, because we want the user to know the secret. The user needs to input it in the authenticator app, either manually or by scanning the QR code. Remember the key URI also contains the secret. Anyway, we need something to generate the QR code. There's a lot of libraries that exist to do that. Microsoft advised using an old and trusted one, qrcode.js, so let's download it, and let's add the minified version to our wwwroot/js folder. So let me just copy this from the zip file and navigate to the Marvin.IDP/wwwroot/js folder and paste it in there. There we go. Now we have that, we can use it in our view. So let me import the script, and let's add a small custom script that generates a QR code in our QR code div from the URI. For that, we new up a QR code object that's coming from the script we just imported. We pass through the div we want the QR code to be generated in and the key URI. Note that from a usability point of view, it's a good idea to show the key URI containing that secret for those people who have issues scanning the QR code correctly. That should take care of that. Once users have scanned this, they will click the button on the Login view. At that time, we post back and have to save the secret for the user. So, let's add a method for that to our local user service. In this method, the input is checked. We try and get the user by subject, and if it's found, we add the secret for that user. Let's add this method signature to the contract as well, and let's add the OnPost action, so back to our MFA registration index.cshtml.cs file, and here we can add the Post method. We check whether the ModelState is valid. If it isn't, we simply return the page, so potential errors can be shown to the user. Otherwise, we look for the subject, and we try to add a user secret for the corresponding user. If it works out, we save the changes, and otherwise, we throw an exception, and that should be it as far as the registration page is concerned. One last thing, it would be nice to have a link to this MFA registration page that is only shown to authenticated users. Let's add that to the Index page. So if the user is authenticated, we add a link to our new mfaregistration/index. Let's give this a try. Let's first log in. We're logged in as Emma, been a while since we've seen these images, and let's navigate back to the identity provider. Here's our link to register for multi‑factor authentication. Let's click it, and we seem to hit an issue. There's no QR code here. We do see the link and it's generated correctly, so users could manually input this in their authenticator app, but it would be nice to show the QR code. So what's going on here? Let's have a look at Chrome's Dev Tools. If we look at the Console Output window, we see that the default CSP, which stands for Content Security Policy, isn't met, and therefore, the script that should render the QR code isn't allowed. A Content Security Policy is a security standard that provides an additional layer of protection from cross‑site scripting, clickcheck, and other code injection attacks. The error we see here is quite easy to get rid of. We could simply allow unsafe inline evaluation with, for example, on the share layout page with an additional meta tag. Like that, inline scripts would be allowed, but well, that would allow unsafe inline scripts, which is a risk if we'd be vulnerable for cross‑site scripting attacks. So while this works, we definitely do not want to do this. Instead, it's better to put the code to generate the QR code in a script that we load from our host, which we control, and like that, we don't have to allow unsafe inline scripts. So let me add a new script to our wwwroot/js folder. I choose to add a new item. I select the JavaScript file template, and I name it qrcodehelper.js. In it, I add an event listener to the load event, and once that's triggered, we execute the same code we initially added inline. We need to do this via an event listener for the load event because we want to make sure that the div which we are using to put the QR code in has been rendered. Alright, let's save this, and let's switch back to our MfaRegistration/Index page. On top here, we can now refer to our new qrcodehelper script. There we go. Let's save this, and let's give this another try. Let's log in as Emma again. There we go. Let's navigate back to our identity provider. Now let's have a look at our MFA Authentication Registration page, and here we are. This time, we do see a nicely‑generated QR code. Time to scan this QR code with my Google Authenticator app. I cannot field my phone while doing so, so you will have to believe me when I say that I am now choosing to scan a QR code, and I just scanned it, and it successfully added a registration for me. At this moment, TOTPs are being generated on my phone, which I can use as a second factor. That means that I can now click that registration was successful, and there we go. So far for that, let's continue with the authentication part.

Supporting MFA with an Authenticator Application - Authentication
The second factor the user will have to input during the authentication process is the time‑based one‑time password the users can read on their authenticator app, but how and when are we going to require that? Where shall we put that input field? Let's consider our options. We can require the additional input whenever a user authenticates, that can be okay, but it can also have a disadvantage. Users that log in with, in our case, Facebook or Azure AD and have two‑factor authentication on at that level, will also get to see that page. I will, thus, have to input yet another factor of authentication. So we could say, okay, we'll only ask for a second factor when a user is using local credentials. On the other hand, if users don't have two‑factor authentication on a level of the third‑party provider and we don't require it, they don't have to input the second factor at all, and that is not necessarily what we want either. Ideally, the third‑party provider would let us know via a token how the user authenticated. We can then read that out, and if it's not strong enough, ask for an additional factor, but not all third‑party providers will provide that information. On top of that, we can choose to enable it only for specific users or even only for specific client applications or a combination of both. This allows forcing some specific users to use MFA and/or allows adding an additional layer of security for clients that deal with sensitive data, so we've got a few options here, and this is something you have to consider for your situation. For our demo, we are interested in how all of this works, so we're okay with just implementing it for local credentials, so we can reuse the login view. What we will do in the demo can be applied for all the use cases we just looked into. Do consider that once we enable this, users will not be able to log in with local credentials anymore, unless they provide a second factor. So in a real‑life scenario, you'd have to make registering that second factor part of the provisioning or onboarding process.

Demo - Supporting MFA with an Authenticator Application (Authentication)
In this demo, we're going to require a second factor of authentication, a one‑time password users can read on their authenticator app, and we'll have to input when logging in. Let's start by adding an additional required field to the input model which is bound to the login view. So let's open that, and let's add a Totp string. We also want to add an input field for this to TOTP to the login view. Right below, password sounds like a good idea. Alright, so whenever a user inputs a valid username and password combination, we additionally want to check whether the inputted TOTP is valid. That means that we have to write a piece of code to generate the TOTP ourselves from the secret stored for the user in the registration step, and then we need to compare that to the value the user inputted. To generate such a code, we can use a helper library. Let's open the NuGet dialog. Quite a few helper packages for this exist. I often use TwoStepsAuthenticator. Let's search for that. There we go. Let's install it. So far for that. To generate the TOTP, we need a secret which is stored for the user, and we have access to that user. Let's have a look at the back‑end code for the index page. After the call to validate the user's credentials, the user is fetched from our localUserService. Let's add a new method to our localUserService which will return the secret for a certain user, so we open the localUserService and we add it. As we're used to by now, the input is first checked, and after that, we try and find a matching user secret using the inputted user subject and name to filter on. If it exists, we return it. Let's add this GetUserSecretAsync method signature to the contract as well, and there we go. Back to our index login page. So the first thing we do is validate username and password. If that checks out, we fetch the user, and after that, we validate the second factor. The first thing we do is get the TOTP secret for this user. If it's not found, we add an error to the ModelState and we show it on the page by returning the page. If a user secret is found, we can check the inputted code. To do that, we use our TwoStepsAuthenticator. We instantiate a new TimeAuthenticator as we're using a time‑based one‑time password. On it, we call CheckCode passing through the secret, the TOTP that was inputted, and the current user. If it doesn't check out, we add a model error and return the page, but if it does, we can simply continue, and thus sign in. We don't have to change that code or do anything about it. The code is already there from a previous demo. That's it. Let's give this a try. You see a new field show up here, TOTP. Let's try logging in as Emma with a random TOTP. This shouldn't work. And indeed, it doesn't. We get an error stating that the TOTP is invalid. That's looking good. Let's try that again. This time, inputting the to TOTP I see being generated in my authenticator app. Let's click Login and there we go, we are logged in again. Let's have a look at the module summary.

Summary
Multi‑factor authentication provides identification of users by means of the combination of two or more different factors. A factor should be seen as a type of authentication. Three commonly‑used types are something you know, something you own, and something you are. Two less commonly‑used types are somewhere you are or something you do. To be able to call something two‑factor authentication or multi‑factor authentication, it is important to use different authentication factors. An OTP is a one‑time password, this is a generated unique password that is only valid for a single login session. Different ways of generating these exist. The two most common ones are HOTP and TOTP. HOTP stands for hashed message authentication code one‑time password, and it is an event‑based OTP algorithm where the moving factor is an event counter. TOTP means time‑based one‑time password. The moving factor here is based on time. A good approach is using an authenticator app like Google Authenticator to generate these TOTPs. Up next, we will learn how we can integrate our IdentityServer with ASP.NET Core Identity.

Integrating with ASP.NET Core Identity
So we've built quite a few screens, and as you've noticed, the approach we took offers great flexibility, but you may not need that type of flexibility. ASP.NET Core Identity is a good alternative for the approach we took. As you remember, it's an application programming interface that supports user interface login functionality which can be used to manage users, passwords, profile data, roles, claims, tokens, email confirmation, and much more. Now, this isn't a deep dive into ASP.NET Core Identity. I'm assuming you've got a bit of experience with it. This module is really about integrating it in IdentityServer. Getting Ready for Production and Deploying Your Identity Provider
Coming Up
There's a few things we have to take care of before going to production. In this module, you will learn what these are, and you will learn how to deploy IdentityServer to Azure. I'm Kevin, and I'll guide you through it. Time to deploy. We will learn about configuration and operational data. We will learn about the Data protection APIs, about storing key material on Azure KeyVault, and about forwarded headers and why they are important. In general, we will learn how to deploy in such a way that the deployment is not only safe, but will also keep functioning behind a load balancer. We will also cover how to apply an IdentityServer license. Let's dive in.

Deploying IdentityServer to Azure
In essence, IdentityServer is just an ASP.NET Core web app. That means that you can start the deployment of IdentityServer to Azure just as you would with any other web app. You set up an App Service on Azure and somehow copy over the required files. There's many ways to do that. You can start from inside of Visual Studio, you can manually create a package to publish from the command line, you can integrate deployment in your CI/CD pipeline, and so on. I'm going to use the Visual Studio approach, but feel free to use any approach you like. In real life, a CI/CD pipeline with automated deployment is probably the most realistic approach these days. However, there's a few things we need to consider. Let's start with configuration and operational data. Configuration data includes resources like our API and identity resources, client configuration, and potentially, course or identity provider‑related configuration, and/or stores. We are currently hardcoding that and that might be okay for your scenario. You could say you want to use continuous deployment to change this when required. Using a settings file is also an option, but often you'd want to use a database for that so it can reliably be updated on the fly by an administration tool, and then there's operational data, grant results used for reference tokens, refresh tokens, authorization, and device codes, key management data, and server‑side sessions. Currently, all of this is stored in memory and that is not a good place to be. As long as we're deploying to one server, we should be okay, but that isn't realistic. When scaling up and using a load balancer, which is common, requests may end up at a different server, and thus, won't reliably succeed. Subsequent requests may end up at a different server, and thus, won't reliably succeed. If for example, a reference token is generated on Server A, meaning that the ground result is stored in the related operational data store, and a request to validate it and get the actual ground content ends up at server B which does not have access to that data store. Well, then validation will fail and that is not what we want. This also means that we cannot use SQLite as data stores. They are deployed together with IdentityServer on the same host, which means that just like an in‑memory data store, it also relies on requests from the same client ending up at the same server to succeed. So when deploying, it's best to switch to a data store that's accessible from a central location so that it doesn't matter which server the request ends up at. What we'll do in the demos is store the configuration data and operational data in a new SQL Azure database. We'll also switch out our SQLite user database for one on SQL Azure. The second thing to think about is data protection. Duende IdentityServer relies on the built‑in data protection feature of ASP.NET Core for protecting keys at rest, protecting persistent grounds at rest, protecting server‑side session data at rest, and session management. We are using at least, two of these, the persistent grants and session management features. Therefore, we need to correctly set up ASP.NET Core data protection. By default, it's set up to work great when deploying to a single machine, but as our identity provider becomes more popular, we will probably want to scale up and end up in that load balancing scenario again. In that case, the default one too. We need to store the data protection keys in a central location. The third thing to keep in mind is the key material used for signing our tokens. By default, IdentityServer creates signing credentials on the fly to sign the tokens, unless you configure it otherwise. This is quite handy during development, but it can cause an issue when going live as the IDP will probably end up behind the load balancer in production. That would mean we'd have different signing credentials, one for each server instance, and depending on how you configure through the load balancer, any request might end up at any server instance, so if, for example, an access token has been signed with the credentials of Server A, but is validated with the signing credentials of Server B validation will fail and that is not what we want. We're going to use Azure KeyVault to store that certificate. That load balancer scenario comes with yet another thing to keep in mind, proxy servers, load balancers, and other network appliances often obscure information about request before it reaches the host. For example, when HTTPS requests are proxied over HTTP, the original scheme is lost and must be forwarded in a header also because an application receives a request from the proxy and not its true source on the internet or corporate network, the originating client IP address must also be forwarded in a header. If we don't use forwarded headers, HTTPS would potentially be turned into HTTP and host names could be incorrect in the discovery document or when redirecting. The way to fix this is by enabling the forwarded headers middleware, so we'll have to configure that. The last thing to keep in mind, so there's actually four plus one things is the license. When deploying to production, we need a license for IdentityServer, that also applies for the Community edition. It's free, but it does require a free license to be applied. We'll cover this as well. Now, you can go further than this, of course, just like with any application. You could put the cache in between, you could go for distributed databases, and so on, but for most common scenarios, the four plus one things I just mentioned are what should be looked into. There's, of course, also things like automatically filling out values in your configuration files depending on the environment and so on, but that is not related to IdentityServer as such. We're focusing on the IdentityServer‑related concerns. As you noticed, I'm going to go for a full‑on Azure deployment, which is pretty realistic these days, but that does mean you need a bit of Azure knowledge to be able to do this. It's a huge subject in its own right, so I am going to assume that you are familiar with some Azure basics like working with common resources like an app service or a storage account and basic access management for those resources. If Azure is new to you, I suggest searching for one of the many introduction‑level Azure courses available here, on Pluralsight. All right, quite a few things to do. Let's start with the first demo.

Demo - Persisting Configuration Data

From this moment on, requests from my IP to this database server will be allowed. That's it for Azure for now. Let's switch to our code. We'll start with storing the configuration data in this database. For that, we want to use a helper package. Let's open the NuGet dialog. We want to use the Duende.IdentityServer.EntityFramework package. Let's choose the version that matches the version of IdentityServer we've been using up until now. Our database is an Azure SQL Server database. Support for that for EntryFramework Core is provided by the regular SQL Server provider, so let's add that package as well. That's that. Now, let's add that connection string we copied to the appsettings file. Let's give it a key IdentityServerDBConnectionString, and let's have a look at that connection string we copied. There's one thing missing here, the password. You will have to input your SQL Server password here. That's the password you chose when setting up your SQL Server on Azure. In my case, that's DemoP@ss. Let's copy this, and there we go. Then let's navigate to the ConfigureServices method in our HostingExtensions class. On our IdentityServer builder, we want to call into AddConfigurationStore. This adds and sets up a store for configuration data. It expects us to configure the DbContext, so for that, we state that we're going to use SQL Server. We call Builder.UseSqlServer, and to it, we pass through the connection string we just inputted in our appsettings file. We can now also comment out these calls that add in‑memory configuration resources because all that data will be stored in our new database, but we're not there yet. We still need to add the migrations so the database can be nicely created. The thing is, the DbContext for our configuration store is in another assembly, it's in the Duende.IdentityServer.EntityFramework assembly, that's the one we just added via NuGet. So the context is in that assembly, yet we want migrations in this project. We must tell Entity Framework Core this. So let's get the current assembly name. For that, we get the type info of one of the classes in this project, let's say the Program class, GetTypeInfo, that's reflection, so we do need to import the System.Reflection namespace, and from that type, we get the assembly's name. Then let's change our AddConfigurationStore code a bit. When calling in to use SQL Server, we can pass in an action for the options. What we want to do here is state that the assembly that will contain the migrations is our current assembly. Let's save this, and let's open the Package Manager Console. Let's make sure that Marvin.IDP is selected, and let's execute the add‑migration command. We passed through a name, say InitialIdentityServerConfigurationDataMigration, and we also passed through the context that the migration has to be added for. We need to be specific about this because we are now dealing with multiple contexts. We need to be specific about this, so the add‑migration command knows which context to use. Let's execute this, and there we go. The migration has been added. Up next is ensuring that whatever is in our Config class, in other words, how we configured all of this, will end up in our database, so what we want to do is seed the database from the configuration data, and we can write code for that. Let me add a new class, let's name it SeedData, and let me paste that it. This code, by the way, is mostly coming from IdentityServer's templates. What this code does is get an instance of our configuration DbContext, and then it looks to the clients, IdentityResources, ApiScopes, and ApiResources in our Config class and adds them. In our Program class, we then call into this method. Alright, let's create a database. For that, we execute an update‑database statement passing through the context. Let's execute this statement, and there we go. The migrations have been applied, which means that the necessary tables have been added to our database. Let's give this a try, and there we go. We're logged in as Emma, so all of this still works, even though we are no longer using in‑memory configuration, that is now coming from our Azure SQL Database. So far, so good. Now this EnsureSeedData method here is just something to get started quickly. Typically, you'd have some sort of admin UI that manipulates this database. If you're looking for something like that, there's a few options. Rock Solid Knowledge, a company that works together with Duende, sells AdminUI, that is a portal application to manage DuendeIdentityServerConfiguration. As an alternative, you could have a look at the Duende.IdentityServer.Admin application on GitHub. This is fully open source, community supported, and free, so if you're interested in that and you don't want to manually edit the database or write a UI for this yourself, check this out. One more thing, configuration data is accessed fairly often during authentication. To avoid too many calls to the database, it's a good idea to enable caching, and that's easy enough to do. A rudimentary in‑memory cache can be configured by calling into AddConfigurationStoreCache. To really get the best performance, you would probably not want to use an in‑memory cache, especially for something that will be deployed to different servers, but this will already go a long way. That's it for this demo. Up next is persisting operational data.

Demo - Persisting Operational Data
In this demo, we're going to learn how to store IdentityServer's operational data in a persistent data store. We're again in our ConfigureServices method in the Hosting Extensions class. This is going to be much like storing configuration data. The first thing we need is a connection string. If you want, you can store this data in a separate database, but there's no reason we cannot reuse our database we just use for configuration data, so let's do that. That means we don't need a new connection string. This time, we call into AddOperationalStore, and we, again, pass through the connection string and the migrations assembly. There's a few additional options you can set here, the default schema, pooling, etc. What we're currently interested in is EnableTokenCleanup. This is set to false by default. By setting it to true, we ensure that expired persisted tokens are automatically cleaned up, that ensures that our database won't grow out of control. Now, let's clean this up a bit. There we go. Up next is adding the migrations, so let's open the Package Manager Console, and we again execute the add‑migration command. We pass through initial IdentityServerOperationalDBMigration as name for the migration, and we pass through persisted grant DbContext as the context for the operational data. Migration has been created, so let's apply it. For that, we update the database, passing through the PersistedGrantDbContext. So far for that. We don't need to seed these tables as the data they contain will be created by IdentityServer while other apps interact with it. If you remember from the module in which we dealt with token expiration and reference tokens, you know that we are still using opaque, or reference tokens, and have offline access enabled. Both the successful refresh token and reference token grant are operational data, so let's give this a try, and if this still works, we know that our operational data is now correctly stored in our database on Azure as well. Let's log in as Emma, and there we go, works as expected. So far for that. Up next, we will switch our user database from SQLite to SQL Azure.
