Demo - Configuring Data Protection
In this demo, we'll configure the data protection APIs so they still work in a multi‑server environment. We're looking at the documentation right now. It's actually not that hard to do. We simply need a central location where we can store the key that is used by those APIs. There's quite a few options. We can store it in a database. We can use the file system, or we can use Blob Storage. On top of that, we can protect it with a key stored in Azure KeyVault, so let's go all in on Azure. We will store the data protection keys in Blob Storage and protect it with a key stored in KeyVault. Let's start with configuring this on Azure. We need two things, one is a location in a Blob Storage container where the data protection API keys will be stored and two is a key in a key vault used to protect that key. Let's navigate to my storage account. Let's open the Containers blade. I created a container here, pluralsightdemocontainer, especially for this purpose. Everything was left at its default values. Let's navigate to the Access Control blade. On the Role Assignment tab, we see that my account has permissions that allow it to work with Blob Storage and the storage queue, and this is important because when running this locally, I will be running in context of myself, my user. The identity in which context we are running needs these two roles. Now, working like this is just one way of working with Azure resources, of course. I assume you've worked with Azure before, so you know that you could also use a shared access signature token if you prefer that, that too is a support. This is all there is to it. A default Blob Storage container will do. Let's move one level up, and let's have a look at the container properties. We've got the URL to the container here. We're going to need that, so let me copy it, and let's immediately put it in our appsettings file. Let's add a new key, data protection, and underneath it. another key, Keys, I paste in the URL to the container and then append it with a key for the blob, say DataProtection keys. So far for that, this is where the data protection keys will be stored. Up next was KeyVault. Let's switch back to Azure. Now, let me navigate home. I've got a PluralsightDemoKeyVault set up here let's click it. In this KeyVault, we're going to store a key which will be used to protect our data protection keys, so let's click the Keys blade, and let's choose to add a new key. Let's name it DataProtectionProtectionKey. Now let's leave everything else at its default value. Let's click Create and there we go, it has been generated and is enabled. If we navigate to it and click it, we can find the key identifier. We're going to need that, so let's copy it to the clipboard, and let's put it in our appsettings file. There we go. Here too, the permissions are important. Let's have a look at those. Let's open the Access policies blade. When running locally, I'm running in the context of myself, and what I need to be able to do is get wrap and unwrap this key. If you have a look at the permissions assigned to me, we see that I indeed am allowed to do that. I've also got the necessary permissions to work with secrets and certificates, that will come in handy later on. Now, this user account is my main user account, so it has almost all potential permissions. That's great for running locally, but once you deploy, you don't want to give permissions that are not needed to whatever identity needs access. Later on in this module when we will effectively deploy our application to an app service, we will make sure that only the minimum amount of required permissions are given. For now, we're good to go, and this is it for Azure. Let's switch to our code. The first thing we'll want to do is add the necessary packages, so let's open the NuGet dialog. Our identity provider will have to communicate and authorize itself with KeyVault. For that, the Azure.Identity package must be added. What we also need is the Azure.Extensions.AspNetCore.DataProtection.Blobs passage which contains the necessary classes and methods to help persist the keys to Blob Storage. Let's install it. There we go. Lastly, to be able to protect those keys with a key store in Key Vault, we'll need the Azure.Extensions.AspNetCore.DataProtection.Keys package so let's look for that and let's install it. So far for that. Back to our ConfigureServices method in the HostingExtensions class. Here, we can now configure the data protection APIs. We call into builder.Services.AddDataProtection for that. On it, we state that we want the keys to be persisted to Azure Blob Storage. For that, we call into PersistKeysToAzureBlobStorage. For that to resolve, we need to import the Microsoft.AspNetCore.DataProtection namespace. What we want to pass through here is the URI to the blob from our appsettings. We also need to pass through a set of credentials under which context this action will run. Newing up default Azure credential is sufficient, that's defined in the Azure.Identity namespace. Locally, this will result in running in the context of my account, and when deployed, this will result in running in the context of whatever we configure the app service that will host our application to run on, typically, a managed identity, so this is exactly what we need, but we're not there yet. We want to protect these keys with a key from KeyVault. For that, we call into protect keys with Azure KeyVault. We pass through the key identifier as the first parameter and a default AzureCredential instance as second parameter. Let me create a URI from this value, and there we go, that should do the trick. Let's save this. So as mentioned a few times, in case I'm running this locally, these credentials are provided assuming I am logged into Azure on my local machine. When running in the cloud, the credentials are provided by the app service the code is deployed to, but that means that locally I need to log into Azure on my machine for this to work, so let's do that. If you've worked with Azure before, this will look very familiar. I've opened a PowerShell prompt and here I want to execute the az login statement. I'm asked to sign in, and there we go. Note that if this doesn't work on your machine, you probably don't have the Azure CLI installed. You can read how to do that via the link on screen. Demo - Storing Key Material in Azure KeyVault
In this demo, we will store our signing credentials in Azure KeyVault. As we learned, by default, IdentityServer creates signing credentials on the fly to sign the tokens, unless you configure it otherwise. We want to use a certificate stored in Azure KeyVault for this instead, so let's navigate to my KeyVault and create it. We essentially need a private public key combination. Certificates can contain that, so let's navigate to the Certificate blade, and let's choose to generate one. We'll give it the name, say PluralsightDemoSigningCertificate, and that's also what we pass through as CN value. CN stands for common name. Let's leave everything else at its default values and click Create. Let's navigate to it because we want to enable it, and there we go. And while we're on this page, let's copy the certificate identifier to our clipboard. We will need it. Let's save, and if we navigate back to our certificate and refresh the page, we see that our certificate is now enabled. Onto the code. Let's open the appsettings file, and let's add two new values. The first one is the KeyVault location and the second one is the certificate name. We can get both from the certificate identifier we just copied to the clipboard. The first part of that identifier is the KeyVault location, and the last part is the name of the certificate. To work with certificates from KeyVault, we can use a helper package, so let's open the NuGet dialog. What you want to search for is the AzureSecurity.KeyVault.Certificates helper package. Let's install it, and there we go. Let's open our HostingExtensions. In the ConfigureServices method, we can now try and get the certificate from KeyVault. To get a hold of a certificate, we can use a certificate client. That's defined in Azure.Security.KeyVault.Certificates, so let's import that namespace. To new it up, we need to pass through the KeyVault location. We store that in our appsettings. The second thing we need to pass through is a credential. We know that by now, and while I'm at it, let's store that credential in a variable and reuse it. There's no need to new it up all the time. There we go. To effectively get the certificate, we call GetCertificate on our client, and we pass through the certificate name, but this won't be sufficient. The certificate info we'll get back with this call is the public part of the certificate and that makes sense. Often, that is all you need, a public key to, for example, validate a signature or encrypt something. However, we need more. IdentityServer uses the certificate to sign tokens, and for signing, you need the private key. We cannot get that via the certificate client. Luckily, there's another client we can use for that, a secret client. That, as the name implies, allows getting secrets from KeyVault. Every certificate you create on Azure KeyVault is represented by three related resources, the certificate resource exposing the public part and some metadata, a key resource exposing the private key, and a secret resource exposing the full certificate, including the private key, and that is the one we need. Let's open the NuGet dialog, and let's search for Azure.Extensions.AspNetCore.Configuration.Secrets. That package contains the necessary helper client to work with secrets. Let's install it. There we go, and we can now also get rid of that certificate helper package we previously installed. We no longer need it. There we go. Back to our code. Let's get rid of this certificate's namespace import, and let's also get rid of this certificate client. Instead, we new up a secret client. That's defined in Azure.Security.KeyVaultSecrets, so let's import that namespace. We passed through a KeyVault URI and a credential. To get to the actual secret, we call GetSecret on it, passing through the certificate name, and that should be it. From the result, we can then new up a new X.509 certificate too. That's defined in System.Security.Cryptography.X509Certificates, so let's import that namespace. Important here is the first parameter. On the secret response, which is of type Azure.Response working on KeyVaultSecret, we get the value that is the KeyVault secret, and from that keyVault secret, we look into the Value property, that contains the actual certificate contents. Alright, with that, we've got a signing certificate. To ensure that IdentityServer effectively uses this signing certificate, we call into AddSigningCredential, passing through the certificate, and that should be it. Let's give this a try. To check whether the certificate from KeyVault is effectively used, we can have a look at the well‑known document. That contains a jwks_uri for JSON web keys. It's this URL that is used by middleware like our OpenID Connect middleware to read out the public key used to validate token signatures, so let's have a look. We see that two keys have been registered. The first one is the key previously generated by IdentityServer. It still shows up here because it's in my output directory. We are more interested in the second value because this is the key that's been loaded from our certificate from Azure KeyVault. So far for that. Onto the next demo.

Demo - Configuring and Using the Forwarded Headers Middleware
In this demo, we'll enable the forwarded headers middleware, which is required when running behind a proxy server or load balancer. We're looking at Microsoft's documentation in which a potential issue is described. Proxy servers, load balancers, and other network appliances tend to obscure information about a request, and it does so on the way because the request passes through that proxy server or load balancer. When an HTTP S request is proxied over HTTP, the original scheme is lost, and because an application receives a request from a proxy and not the true source of the request, the originating client IP address is lost. Therefore, both the original scheme and the originating client IP address are forwarded in a header. By conventions, proxies add these headers to the request, but that doesn't mean that our application knows where to find them, and that's where the forwarded headers middleware comes into play. What that does is ensure that these headers are read and filled out in the associated fields on HTTP context. Let's switch to our code. We're again in the ConfigureServices method and here we can configure the forwarded header option. For that, we call into builder.Services.Configure and we pass through what we want to configure, in our case, the forwarded headers options. Let's import to Microsoft.AspNetCore.HttpOverrides namespace for this to resolve. What we're stating here is which forwarded headers should be processed. XForwardedFor is a header that contains the IP address, and XForwardedProto is the header that contains the original scheme, and then we also need to add it to the request pipeline. For that, we call into app.UseForwardedHeaders. It's important that this middleware runs before other middleware. Doing so ensures that the middleware relying on forwarded headers information can consume the header values for processing. And there we go, this is it. We won't see an effect of this locally at the moment, but we will once we deploy because our identity provider will function correctly, no matter where we deploy it. The last thing we want to do before deploying to production is applying a license. Let's do that next.

Demo - Applying a License
In this demo, we'll apply a license, but I first need one, of course. If you're using the Community Edition, you can apply for a free license via the link on screen which will then be mailed to you. If you're applying for a paid edition, you will also receive a license key. What you'll get sent is a file named Duende_License.key. IdentityServer will automatically look for this file in the root directory, and if it exists, locally check the key, so let's add this to our solution. Let's switch to Visual Studio. Here's our license key. Let's have a look at the properties because we want this to be copied to the output directory, and there we go, and that's it. You don't need to do anything else. As an alternative, you can also input the license key via code. In that case, you just copy over the value you see on screen here and set it when configuring IdentityServer. On the IdentityServer options, you will find a LicenseKey property which you can set to the license key. Onto the last demo.

Demo - The Final Deployment
Alright, with that, all should be in place. Time for the actual deployment. We're in Visual Studio. Let's right‑click our project and let's choose Publish. We want to publish to Azure, so let's select that, and we're going to publish to an app service. Let's create a new one by clicking the plus button here, and let's name it PluralsightDemoMarvinIDP. You can also choose the subscription, resort group, and hosting plan if you want. Now let's click Create. There we go. Let's click Finish, and we are ready to publish. An interesting fact here is that the wizard was smart enough to notice that we're using KeyVault in a database in our solution. During this demo, we will have to set some access rights and one of the access rights we need to set is ensuring that our app service on which we will host IdentityServer is allowed to access the databases. Thanks to this wizard, that is no longer a manual process. When the database has been detected, it will automatically allow the app service to access it. Alright, let's click Publish. The deployment was successful, but apparently our app does not start. Why would this be? Let's have a look at our app service. If we navigate to App Services, we should see it. Here it is, it has correctly been created. We've got a few options to find out what is going wrong. Azure includes extensive logging capabilities which you can find underneath the Monitoring section. You can enable logging via the App Service logs blade. You can enable logs from the application, you can enable logs from the web server, detailed error messages, and failed request tracing. From the moment on, you've enabled one or more of these, logs will be collected. You can then view some of these logs coming in live via the log stream. Another thing you could do is add a diagnostic setting. This allows you to specify where whatever's being logged should end up. Like that, you can, for example, nicely store what's logged in a storage account. Another thing you could do to try and find out what's going wrong is attaching a debugger, that comes in handy during development. To do so, click the three dots of the Hosting section and choose the Attach Debugger option. Of course, if you choose to go this route, make sure you publish using the debug configuration, instead of publishing a release version. All this is just to say that you've got options. I happen to already know what's going on here. I mentioned a few times that passing through the default Azure credential when accessing KeyVault and the Blob container ensures that locally we're running under my credentials, while once deployed, we're running under the app service identity. What we haven't done yet is give the app service the necessary rights to access KeyVault and our blob container, so let's fix that. Let's navigate to the KeyVault first, and let's open the Access policies blade. Here, we want to add a new policy. We want to allow getting a key, unwrapping, and wrapping one. We also want to allow getting and listing secrets and getting and listing permissions. Let's click Next. Here, we can now choose the principle that will be allowed to do these things. It's a pretty small list here, but if we look for Pluralsight, we find our app service, so let's click that and let's click Next. Now let's continue and let's click Create, and there we go. We've just given our app service the necessary rights to be able to interact with KeyVault. We also have to give our app service access to our blob container, so let's navigate to the container. So I open my storage account, click Containers, and navigate to the Pluralsight demo container which contains the data protection keys. Let's open the Access Control blade, and let's choose Role assignments. We want to add our app service to the storage blob data contributor and storage queue data contributor roles. So let's click Add, and let's search for Storage Blob Data Contributor. Here it is. Let's click Next, and let's choose to select members. We want to look for our app service. Let's add it, and there we go. Let's assign, and our app service was now added to the Storage Blob Data Contributor role. We also want to add the app service to the Storage Queue Data Contributor role, that is also required for persisting our data protection keys, but this role cannot be set at level of our container, it is set one level up and inherited by it. So let's navigate one level up to our storage account. Let's choose Access Control, Role assignments, Add, and let's search for Storage Queue Data Contributor. Here we go. Let's look for our app service, select it, and assign. There we go. Let's give this another try. That looks good. Let's have a look at the well‑known document. We can access that as well. From this, we can gather that our database is correctly configured for access from the app service as generating this document requires looking through the configuration which we have stored in our database. We are also sure that data protection is set up and that the certificate is read from KeyVault. So here we go. Our identity provider has been successfully deployed to Azure. Time for the summary.

Summary
In this module, we looked into getting ready for production and deploying IdentityServer. In real life scenarios, IdentityServer will often live behind a load balancer in a multi‑server environment, whether you're hosting it on Azure or not. In such a setup, anything that's kept in memory and must stay as‑is or accessible for the IDP to function correctly has to move to a central location. Most of this module consisted of doing so. We started with configuration data. That includes resources like our API and identity resources, clients, and all other configuration‑related data. It's a good idea to use a persistent store for that, so it can be updated on the fly by an administration tool, but more importantly, shared across different instances of IdentityServer. Operational data concerns ground results like tokens and codes, key management data, and server‑side sessions. Storing that in memory can cause issues when an application pool recycles or when you're behind a load balancer. What we did was set up a persistent store for configuration and operational data using Entity Framework Core that can be shared by all IdentityServer instances. While we were at it, we did the same with our user database. The story for data protection keys and designing material is likewise, it needs to be accessible regardless of the server a request ends up at. One more thing of importance was the forwarded headers middleware. Proxy servers and load balancers often obscure information about the original request like the original scheme and the originating client IP. These values must be forwarded in a header so they don't get lost. Finally, we applied IdentityServer's license and deployed our identity provider to an Azure App Service, and that's it. If you've got questions, the best place to post them is on the Discussions tab on the course page, and if you want to be notified of course updates or new courses, you can follow me on X. Also, please consider rating this course. It gives us authors some feedback in regards to whether we're doing this right or not. I hope you enjoyed this course, as much as I enjoyed creating it, and I've got just one more thing to say before I go. You're ready to be awesome.
